Accepting a Child's Claim of Puberty
Ruling on accepting the claim of puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum ages for accepting a child's claim of puberty and apply the ruling to given scenarios.

Core Idea
A child's claim of puberty is accepted only if age meets Hanafi minimums.

This passage addresses a practical question: when does a near-pubescent child's claim of reaching puberty carry legal weight? The Hanafi school sets clear age thresholds and relies on the child's word unless outward evidence contradicts. Understanding this ruling helps you grasp how the law handles matters known only to the individual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimants and the claim
The passage speaks of a boy and girl (al-ghulam wa al-jariyah) who are 'murahiq' (near puberty). Their claim is 'balaghna' (we have reached puberty). Recognize that the ruling hinges on the child being close to the age of majority.
The term 'rāhiq' indicates approaching, not having reached, puberty.
2
Note the condition of minimum age
The commentary clarifies that the claim is accepted only if the boy has reached twelve years or more, and the girl nine years or more. Below these ages, the child is not believed because الظاهر كذبه (the outward situation indicates falsity).
These are the minimum ages for puberty in the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the legal effect
If the claim is accepted, the child is 'like an adult in terms of ruling' – their legal capacity is recognized for matters dependent on puberty (e.g., accountability, marriage consent).
The child becomes a mukallaf (legally responsible) from that point.
4
Apply the analogy with menstruation
The commentator compares this to a woman's claim about menstruation. In both cases, the matter is known only to the person themselves, so their word is accepted unless الظاهر (outward evidence) contradicts them.
This is a key usul principle: amr la yu'raf illa min jihathia.
5
Test the ruling with examples
Apply: A 10-year-old boy claims puberty → not accepted (below 12). A 13-year-old girl claims puberty → accepted (above 9) unless she shows no physical signs. A boy of 14 who looks young – his claim is still accepted because age satisfies the minimum.
The outward contradiction (e.g., no physical changes) can override the claim only if it is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحجر/ تعريفه وأحكامه فإن راهقا فقالا: بلغنا صدقا، وهما كالبالغ حكماً [١] قوله: فإن راهقا؛ أي الغلام والجارية، يقال: رهقه: أي قرب منه، وصبي مراهق: أي متقارب من البلوغ. [٢] قوله: فقالا؛ أي إذا أشكل الأمر في بلوغهما، ولم يعلم ذلك منهما، فقالا [٣] قوله: صدقا؛ أي القولُ قولهما، قيل: هذا إذا بلغ الغلام اثنتي عشرة سنة أو أكثر، والجارية تسع سنين أو أكثر، ولا يقبلُ فيما دون ذلك؛ لأنَّ الظاهر كذبه، وقد أشار المصنف إلى هذا المعنى لقوله: وأدنى مدّة له اثنا عشر سنة، ولها تسع سنين. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [٤] قوله: وهما كالبالغ حكماً؛ أي حكمهما حكم البالغين؛ لأنه معنى لا يتعرف إلا من جهتهما ظاهراً، فإذا أخبرا به ولم يكذِّبهما الظاهرُ قُبل قولهما، حتى إذا ادعى الغلام البلوغ وعمره أقل من اثني عشر سنة لا يصدق. وكذا إذا ادعته الجارية وعمرها أقل من تسع سنين لا تصدق، كما تقبل المرأة في الحيض؛ لأنه معنى لا يعرفُ إلا من جهتها، فالقول قولها إلا إذا كذبها الظاهر. كذا في «العيني».
Book of Legal Interdiction / Its Definition and Its Rulings: And if they (boy and girl) are near puberty and they say: 'We have reached puberty,' they are believed, and they are like adults in terms of ruling. [1] His statement: 'And if they are near puberty' – meaning the boy and the girl; it is said: rahaqahu means he drew near to him; a boy near puberty (murahiq) means approaching puberty. [2] His statement: 'and they say' – meaning when the matter of their puberty is ambiguous, and it is not known from them, and they say. [3] His statement: 'they are believed' – meaning their word is accepted. It is said: this is when the boy has reached twelve years or more, and the girl nine years or more; it is not accepted below that, because the apparent situation indicates they are lying. The author referred to this meaning by his saying: 'and the minimum duration for him is twelve years and for her is nine years.' This is in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. [4] His statement: 'and they are like adults in terms of ruling' – meaning their ruling is the ruling of adults; because it is a matter that is not known except from them apparently. So if they inform of it and the apparent situation does not show them to be liars, their word is accepted, to the extent that if a boy claims puberty while he is less than twelve years old, he is not believed. Likewise, if a girl claims it while she is less than nine years old, she is not believed. Just as a woman is accepted regarding menstruation, because it is a matter known only from her, her word is accepted unless the apparent situation shows her to be a liar. This is in 'al-'Ayni'.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This second passage from the same work deals with a subsidiary ruling: when a child who is near puberty (murahiq) claims to have reached puberty, and their claim is plausible in terms of age, then their word is accepted. The matn states: 'If they are near puberty and say: We have reached puberty, they are believed, and they are like adults in terms of ruling.' The commentary explains that the term 'rāhiqā' refers to the boy and the girl who have approached the age of puberty. The acceptance of their claim is conditional upon them being at least at the minimum age of puberty: for a boy, twelve years; for a girl, nine years. Below these ages, the claim is not accepted because the external circumstances indicate falsehood (the natural signs of puberty are unlikely). The author (the matn author) indicated this by previously stating the minimum ages. The commentary analogizes this to the acceptance of a woman's claim about her menstruation: since these matters are not externally observable except through the person's own knowledge, their statement is given weight unless contradicted by clear evidence. Thus, the presumptive continuity of childhood is overridden by the child's own testimony when the age is reasonable and no other facts contradict it. The mufta bihi follows this ruling.

Key Terms
راهق (rāhiq / murahiq): A boy or girl approaching puberty; near the age of majority.
صدق (saddaqa / yusaddaq): To be believed; their word is accepted as true regarding their own condition.
ظاهر (zāhir): Outward appearance or evidence; what is apparent to others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A 10-year-old boy claims he has reached puberty. According to the Hanafi ruling, is his claim accepted?
A) Yes, because he knows himself best.
B) No, because the minimum age for a boy is 12.
C) Yes, if he shows signs of puberty.
D) No, because only a girl's claim is accepted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a near-pubescent child's claim of puberty only at or above the minimum ages of 12 (boy) and 9 (girl), provided no outward evidence contradicts.

Accepting Adolescents' Claim of Puberty
Ruling on accepting adolescents' claim of puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting an adolescent's claim of puberty and identify the minimum ages required for the claim to be accepted.

Core Idea
An adolescent's claim of puberty is accepted if not contradicted by apparent circumstances; Hanafis set minimum ages of 12 for boys and 9 for girls.

This lesson examines a ruling from the Book of Interdiction regarding the credibility of adolescents who claim to have reached puberty. The Hanafi school establishes clear age thresholds to determine when their claim is plausible, balancing the inherent knowledge of the individual with objective indicators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
The ruling applies when there is doubt about an adolescent's puberty, and they themselves claim to have reached it.
The text uses the term 'murahiq' for adolescent.
2
State the General Rule
Their claim is believed (sadaqa), and they are treated as adults in legal rulings.
This is the default position if the minimum age is met.
3
Note the Condition: Minimum Age
The claim is accepted only if the boy is at least 12 lunar years and the girl at least 9 lunar years. Below that, the apparent circumstance belies them.
The text mentions 'adna mudda' (minimum period).
4
Understand the Reasoning (Talil)
Puberty is a matter known only to the individual, similar to a woman's claim of menstruation. Thus, their word is taken unless external evidence contradicts.
The commentary draws an analogy to menstruation (hayd).
5
Recognize the Limitation
If the adolescent is below the minimum age, or if physical signs clearly contradict the claim, the claim is rejected.
The text explicitly states: 'لا يصدق' (he is not believed).

Book of Interdiction / Its Definition and Rulings. If an adolescent boy and girl (approach puberty) and say: 'We have reached puberty,' they are believed, and they are like adults in terms of ruling. [Explanation]: His saying 'if they approach puberty' means the boy and the girl. It is said: rahaqa means near, and a child approaching puberty means nearing maturity. His saying 'so they said' means if the matter of their puberty is doubtful and not known from them, and they said. His saying 'they are believed' means their statement is accepted. It is said: this is if the boy has reached twelve years or more, and the girl nine years or more, and it is not accepted before that because the apparent (circumstance) belies them. The author indicated this meaning by his saying: 'and the minimum period for him is twelve years, and for her nine years.' Likewise in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. His saying 'and they are like adults in ruling' means their ruling is the ruling of adults; because it is a matter known only from them apparently. So if they inform of it and the apparent does not belie them, their statement is accepted. Thus, if a boy claims puberty and his age is less than twelve, he is not believed. Similarly, if a girl claims it and her age is less than nine, she is not believed, just as a woman is accepted regarding menstruation; because it is a matter known only from her, so her statement is accepted unless the apparent belies her. Likewise in 'Al-Ayni'. (1) Source: 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba' p. 546.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Interdiction states a ruling regarding the acceptance of adolescents' claims of having reached puberty. The commentary explains the key terms: 'rahaqa' means approaching puberty, referring to a boy (ghulam) and a girl (jariya). The ruling is that if there is doubt about their puberty and they both claim to have reached it, their claim is accepted (they are believed) provided they have reached a minimum age: for a boy, twelve lunar years; for a girl, nine lunar years. Below these ages, their claim is not accepted because the apparent circumstance (like physical immaturity) contradicts them. The reasoning given is that puberty is a matter known only to the individuals themselves, similar to a woman's claim of menstruation. Therefore, their word is taken unless external evidence belies them. The passage also notes that once believed, they are treated as adults in legal rulings. This is a standard Hanafi position found in the matn of Al-Wiqaya and its commentaries.

Key Terms
رهق (rahaqa): To approach or come near (puberty).
مراهق (murahiq): An adolescent boy or girl nearing puberty.
بلوغ (bulugh): Puberty, legal majority.
صدق (sadaqa): To be believed, accepted as truthful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi fiqh, at what minimum age is a girl's claim of puberty accepted?
A) 7 years
B) 9 years
C) 10 years
D) 12 years

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts an adolescent's claim of puberty if they have reached the minimum ages of 12 for boys and 9 for girls, relying on their inherent knowledge of their own condition.

Definition of Permission (Idhn) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the term 'idhn' in the Book of Ma'dhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal meaning of idhn (permission) and distinguish it from mere information (i'lam).

Core Idea
Idhn is the lifting of interdiction and dropping of a right – permission, not mere notification.

This lesson unpacks the definition of idhn (permission) as it appears in the Hanafi fiqh work 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. Understanding this definition is foundational for the Book of Ma'dhun, which deals with slaves permitted to trade. We will parse the wording and clarify why idhn means legal permission, not just notification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines the term 'idhn' (permission). Locate the exact wording where the definition is given: 'الإذن فك الحجر وإسقاط الحق'.
The term is also the key to understanding the status of the ma'dhun.
2
Analyze the components of the definition
Break down 'fakk al-hajr' (lifting of interdiction) and 'isqat al-haqq' (dropping of right). Consider what each entails.
Interdiction (hajr) is a prohibition from managing assets; lifting it removes that barrier. Dropping a right means the guardian no longer has the right to object.
3
Distinguish the legal meaning from the linguistic one
Note that linguistically 'idhn' means release. The legal meaning builds on that but specifically involves removing a previous restriction. Also, contrast with the view that it means merely informing (i'lam).
Imam al-Zayla'i's view is rejected because idhn is not mere information but an act that enables dispositions.
4
Understand the necessity of prior interdiction
The passage explains that the Book of Ma'dhun follows the Book of Interdiction because permission presupposes a previous state of interdiction. Reflect on why this ordering is logical.
This sequence shows the legal logic: first come restrictions, then permissions.
5
Apply the definition to the ma'dhun's capacity
Recognize that once idhn is given, the ma'dhun (permitted slave or minor) can engage in certain contracts. The definition determines the scope of that capacity.
If permission were only information, the master's right would not be dropped, making transactions potentially voidable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون

Book of the Permitted Slave. Book of the Permitted Slave. [2] Permission is the lifting of interdiction and dropping of the right. Know that the original state of a human is that he is owner of dispositions. [1] His saying: 'Book of Ma'dhun': bringing the Book of Ma'dhun after the Book of Interdiction is clearly appropriate, because permission necessitates prior interdiction. So as they are sequential in existence, they are also sequential in mention. Likewise in 'Nata'ij al-Afkar'. [2] His saying: 'Al-Ma'dhun': He said in 'Radd al-Muhtar': It means permission (idhn), so it is a verbal noun like ma'sur. Although it appears to be an adjective, it requires omission of the modified noun or the relative clause. In 'Al-Kirmani': It is said 'ma'dhun lahu' or 'ma'dhun laha', and omitting the relative clause is not Arabic usage. Al-Quhistani approved this, and the implied noun is 'idhn al-ma'dhun' (permission of the permitted slave), because the discussion is about actions, not persons. In 'Al-Misbah': The jurists omit the relative clause for understanding the meaning. [3] His saying: 'Permission is the lifting of interdiction': This is its legal meaning. The author of 'Radd al-Muhtar' said: Al-Turi said: Shaykh al-Islam said in his 'Mabsut': Permission is release in language; because it is the opposite of interdiction which is prevention, so it is a release from something to something. In 'Al-Nihaya': Permission in a thing is the removal of the barrier from one who is interdicted, and informing of his release in what he was interdicted from. It comes from 'adhina lahu fi al-shay' (to permit him in the matter). Imam al-Zayla'i went far by saying it is information, from which comes the adhan (call to prayer) which is information. Because idhn from 'adhina fi kadha' means to permit, while adhan from 'adhina bi kadha' means to inform. In 'Hashiyat Abi al-Su'ud': Qadi Zada said in 'Al-Takmila': I have not seen in Arabic language books that idhn means information. (Sources: Nata'ij al-Afkar 9:281, Radd al-Muhtar 6:154, Al-Misbah al-Munir p.10, Radd al-Muhtar 6:155, Al-Tabyin 5:204).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Ma'dhun (the Permitted Slave) and defines the key term 'idhn' (permission). The commentator explains why this book follows the Book of Interdiction: permission presupposes a prior state of interdiction. He then parses the word 'ma'dhun', noting that it is a verbal noun meaning permission, though it appears as an adjective. Technically, it is shorthand for 'ma'dhun lahu' (one to whom permission is given). The legal meaning of 'idhn' is lifting of interdiction (fakk al-hajr) and dropping of the right (isqat al-haqq). The text cites various authorities: Al-Turi and Shaykh al-Islam define it linguistically as release (itlaq), opposite of prevention (man'). Al-Nihaya defines it as removal of the barrier from an interdicted person and informing of his release. There is a disagreement with Imam al-Zayla'i, who equated idhn with i'lam (information). The commentator sides with those who distinguish: idhn is permission, not mere information. This definition is foundational for understanding the legal capacity of a ma'dhun slave or minor.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): The book about a slave or person who has been given permission by his master/guardian to engage in trade, understood as 'one to whom permission is given'.
إذن (idhn): Permission, the lifting of interdiction and dropping of the right.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction, prohibition from managing one's own affairs.
فك الحجر (fakk al-hajr): Lifting of interdiction, removal of legal restriction.
إسقاط الحق (isqat al-haqq): Dropping of a right (e.g., the guardian's right to object).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal meaning of 'idhn' (permission)?
A) Information (i'lam)
B) Lifting of interdiction and dropping of the right
C) Ownership of dispositions
D) Reaching puberty

Key Takeaway
Idhn (permission) is the lifting of interdiction and dropping of the right, enabling the ma'dhun to validly engage in trade.

The Scope of Permission for a Ma'dhun Slave
Explanation that permission for trade is not restricted by time or type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that permission to trade in one type extends to all types, and distinguish this from agency.

Core Idea
Permission is a removal of restraint, not an agency; therefore it is unlimited in scope.

This lesson explores the nature of a master's permission (idhn) for his slave to engage in trade. The Hanafi school views such permission as a relinquishment of the master's right to restrain (isqat al-haqq), not as an appointment of agency (tawkil). This distinction has significant practical consequences: permission is not restricted by time or type of transaction. We will examine the reasoning behind this view, consider the opposing position of Imam al-Shafi'i, and apply the ruling to concrete scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The master's permission (idhn) for the slave to trade. What is the ruling? The permission is valid (sahih) and unrestricted in scope: it covers all times and all types of trade unless explicitly limited to a specific act (which would then be utilization, not permission).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a rational reasoning (dalil aqli) based on classification. The author argues that permission is a removal of restraint (fakk al-hajr), and by definition, removal of restraint is an absolute release from a constraint, not limited by time or type.
The key phrase: 'هذا تفريع على أنَّهُ إسقاط الحق لا توكيل' – this is a branch of it being a relinquishment, not an agency.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the classification lead to the ruling? If permission is isqat (relinquishment), it has the characteristic of being unconditional and unrestricted. Agency (tawkil), in contrast, is inherently limited by time and scope. Therefore, the permission covers all types of trade and is not time-bound.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that permission is like agency (tawkil), and therefore it can be restricted by the master's specification. The Shafi'i position does not treat permission as a removal of restraint but as a grant of authority, which is inherently limited.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school. Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah supports the Hanafi view. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in the Hanafi school, a slave permitted to trade in one type can trade in any type, whereas in the Shafi'i school, the permission would be restricted to that type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يتوقت) : هذا تفريع على أنَّهُ إسقاط الحق لا توكيل، فإنَّ الإسقاط لا يتوقت، والتوكيل يتوقت. (٢) (فعبد أُذِنَ يوماً مأذون حتى يُحْجَرَ عليه، ولم يتخصص بنوع، فإن أذن في نوع عمّ إذنُهُ في الأنواع، هذا تفريع على أنَّهُ فك الحجر، وليس بتوكيل ; لأنَّ فك الحجر هو الإطلاق عن القيد، فلا يتخصص بتصرف، وفيه خلاف الشافعي ، والمراد أنه إذا أذن في نوع من التِّجارةِ " عم إذنُهُ في الأنواع، وكذا إذا قيل: اقعد صباغاً ، فإِنَّهُ إذن بشراء ما لا بدَّ لهذا العمل فيعم، وكذا إذا قيل : أد إليَّ الغُلَّةَ كل شهر كذا ، بخلاف ما إذا أُذِنَ بشراء شيء معين، فإنَّ هذا استخدام لا إذن.
And it is not time-bound: This is a branch of it being a relinquishment of right, not an agency assignment; for relinquishment is not time-bound, and agency assignment is time-bound. (2) So a slave permitted for a day is permitted until he is restricted, and it is not specialized by type; if he permits in one type, his permission applies in all types. This is a branch of it being a removal of restriction, not an agency assignment; because removal of restriction is release from a constraint, so it is not specialized by an action, and there is a difference with al-Shafi'i regarding it. What is meant is that if he permits in a type of trade, his permission applies in all types. Similarly, if he says: 'Sit as a dyer,' then it is permission to buy what is necessary for this work, so it applies generally. Similarly, if he says: 'Pay me the rent every month like this,' contrary to when he is permitted to buy a specific thing, for that is utilization, not permission.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and proof statement from the commentary of Al-Wiqayah. It addresses the nature of a master's permission (idhn) to his slave for trade. The author establishes that permission is a relinquishment (isqat) of the master's right to restrain the slave, not an agency (tawkil). Therefore, it is not limited by time or specific type of transaction. If the master permits the slave to trade in one type—like buying wheat—the permission extends to all types of trade, even if the master explicitly forbids others. The same applies if he says 'sit as a dyer' or 'pay me rent monthly.' However, if the master orders a specific transaction (e.g., 'buy this item'), that is considered utilization (istikhdām), not permission, and does not grant general trading authority. The opposite school, al-Shafi'i, holds that permission is like agency, thus it can be restricted.

Key Terms
إسقاط الحق (isqāt al-ḥaqq): Relinquishment of the master's right to restrain the slave from trading.
فك الحجر (fakk al-ḥajr): Removal of the legal restriction that prevents the slave from trading.
توكيل (tawkīl): Agency assignment; the master appointing the slave as his agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave, 'Go and buy wheat,' but does not mention other goods, can the slave legally buy barley?
A) Yes, because permission in one type covers all types.
B) No, because the master specified only wheat.
C) Yes, but only if the master is silent about other goods.
D) No, because the slave is not permitted to trade at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a master's permission for a slave to trade is a removal of restraint, thus it is unrestricted in time and type unless explicitly tied to a specific transaction (which would then be utilization, not permission).

Implicit Permission by Silence of the Master
Establishing permission through indication when master sees slave trading and remains silent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a master's silence constitutes implied permission for a slave to trade and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Silence implies permission when it prevents deception — and the slave's ownership is real.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute: when a master sees his slave buying and selling and remains silent, does the slave become a permitted trader (ma'dhūn)? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on the principle that permission is the removal of restraint, not agency. Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i argue that permission is agency and can be restricted. This lesson unpacks the proof and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The master's silence when seeing his slave buy and sell constitutes implied permission (idhn dalāla), making the slave a ma'dhūn (permitted trader) with full capacity to contract in his own right.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that permission is agency (tawkil). The slave acts on behalf of the master, so the master can restrict the authority. Silence does not imply general permission.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They argue that permission entails delegation of authority (wilāya) from master to slave. The master retains the right to revoke or limit this authority, so silence cannot be taken as blanket permission.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that permission is not agency but isqāt al-ḥaqq (relinquishment of the right to restrain) and fakk al-ḥajr (removal of legal disability). The slave's ownership in transactions is real, not derivative. Therefore, the permission is general unless explicitly restricted.
The key distinction: the ma'dhūn owns the profit, whereas an agent does not.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, a slave seen trading while master is silent can be bound by contracts and accumulate debts in his own capacity. In Shafi'i law, such a slave lacks authority, and third parties deal at risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويثبت : دلالة، فعبد راه سيده يبيع ويشترى وسكت مأذون ) ، هذا عندنا خلافاً لزفر والشافعي (١) ، وإنما يكون مأذونا دفعاً للغرور استخدام عبده فيما اشتدّ إليه حاجته ; لأنَّ غالب استعمال العبد في شراء الأشياء الحقيرة. .۲ وقسم يجب أن يكون إذناً ، وهو الأمر بالتصرُّفِ النوعي : كالأمر ببيع نوع معين من أ، أنواع الأعيان المذكورة، وهذا الفرق الذي مداره النوعية والشخصية هو المفهوم من كلام صاحب «الهداية». صرح به الأكمل. كذا في «حاشية جلبي). [ ۱ ] قوله : ويثبت دلالة ; أي يثبتُ الإذنُ دلالة، في «الحقائق»: إنما يجعل سكوت المولى إذناً إذا لم يسبق منه ما يوجب نفي الإذنِ حالة السكوت، كقوله : إذا رأيتم عبدي يتجر فسكت، فلا إذن له بالتجارة، ثم رآه يتجر ، فسكت لا يصير مأذونا اتفاقاً. كذا في رد المحتار»(۳). (٢) قوله : وسكت مأذون ; قال في «الهداية (4) : لا فرق بين أن يكون عيناً مملوكاً للمولى أو لأجنبي. [٣] قوله : خلافاً لزفر والشافعي الله لهما : إنّ الإذن توكيل وإنابة من المولى ; لأنه يستفيد الولاية من جهته، ويثبت الحكم وهو ا الملك له دون العبد ; ولهذا يملك حجره، فيتخصص بما خصه كالمضارب. ولنا: إنه إسقاط الحق، وفك الحجر على ما مرّ، وعند ذلك تظهر ملكية العبد ، فلا يتخصص بنوع دون نوع، بخلاف الوكيل ; لأنه يتصرف في مال غيره، فتثبت له الولاية من جهته، وحكم التصرف وهو الملك واقع للعبد ، حتى كان له أن يصرفه إلى قضاء الدين والنفقة، وما استغنى عنه يخلفه المالك فيه.
And it is established by indication: a slave whom the master sees selling and buying and remains silent is permitted. This is our view, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. And he is only permitted to prevent deception and utilization of his slave for what he urgently needs, because the usual employment of a slave is in purchasing small items. And a second category that must be permission is the command for a generic transaction: like the command to sell a specific type from the mentioned types of goods. This distinction based on generic versus specific is understood from the words of the author of Al-Hidayah. Al-Akmal stated it. As in Hashiyat Jalabi. (1) His statement: 'And it is established by indication' means the permission is established by indication. From Al-Haqa'iq: The silence of the owner is only considered permission if there was no prior statement negating permission during silence. For example, if he says, 'If you see my slave trading, be silent,' then he sees him trading and remains silent, he does not become permitted by agreement. As in Radd al-Muhtar. (2) His statement: 'and remains silent, he is permitted' said in Al-Hidayah: there is no difference whether the item is owned by the master or a stranger. (3) His statement: 'contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i' — they say that permission is agency and delegation from the master, because the slave gains authority from him, and the ruling of ownership goes to the master, not the slave; hence the master has the power to restrict him, so it can be specialized, like the case of mudarib. Our argument: It is relinquishment of right and removal of restraint as mentioned, and then the slave's ownership becomes apparent, so it cannot be specialized by type, unlike the agent who acts in another's property, so authority is established from the principal, and the ruling of the transaction (ownership) goes to the slave, so that he can spend it to pay debts and maintenance, and what remains goes to the master.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding implicit permission (idhn dalāla). The Hanafi position is that if a master sees his slave buying and selling and remains silent, the slave becomes a permitted trader (ma'dhūn). This is to prevent deception and to address the common practice where slaves purchase small necessities. The opponents (Zufar and al-Shafi'i) argue that permission is agency (tawkil) and can be restricted, so silence does not imply general permission. The Hanafi response reiterates that permission is relinquishment of the right to restrain and removal of the master's restraint (fakk al-ḥajr), not agency. The slave's ownership in transactions is recognized; he can pay debts and maintenance, and the remainder goes to the master. The text also clarifies that for silence to be considered permission, there must be no prior statement that negates permission during silence. Additionally, there is no difference whether the slave buys goods owned by the master or a stranger — both are covered by implied permission.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalāla): Proof by indication; here, the master's silence indicates permission.
مأذون (ma’dhūn): A slave who has been permitted by his master to engage in trade, with legal capacity to contract.
دفعاً للغرور (daf‘an lil-ghurūr): To prevent deception of third parties who transact with the slave thinking he is permitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the master's silence NOT grant permission to the slave according to the Hanafis?
A) When the master had previously said, 'If you see my slave trading, do not consider him permitted.'
B) When the slave buys items that are not customary.
C) When the slave trades with strangers.
D) When the master is not present at the time of trading.

Key Takeaway
A master's silence when seeing his slave trade constitutes implied permission (idhn dalāla) under Hanafi law, based on removal of restraint (fakk al-ḥajr), unless prior negation exists.

Ruling on Master's Prior Taking from Ma'dhun's Property
Explanation of the ruling on the slave's debt payment after emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a master's prior taking of a slave's property reduces the slave's debt and explain the slave's liability after emancipation.

Core Idea
A master's taking before debt does not reduce the slave's debt; the slave is sued for the remainder after emancipation.

In Hanafi law, a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun) incurs debts in the course of business. A question arises: if the master takes property from the slave before the debt is incurred, does that count as payment? This lesson clarifies that such prior taking does not affect the debt, and the slave remains liable for the full debt after gaining freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize that the case involves a ma'dhun slave who has incurred debts from trade. The master took some of the slave's property before the debt attached.
The slave's status as ma'dhun implies permission to trade.
2
Distinguish two categories of earnings
Separate earnings that the slave uses to pay the debt (his own earnings and sale price) from property the master took before the debt arose.
The commentary highlights this distinction with 'ashara ila al-farq'.
3
Apply the ruling
The master's prior taking is not credited toward the debt. The debt remains as is.
The matn states 'la bima akhadhahu sayyiduhu minhu qabla al-dayn'.
4
Determine post-emancipation liability
If any debt remains after the slave's resources (price and earnings) are exhausted, he is personally sued for it after emancipation.
The commentary says 'tuliba bima baqi ba'da 'itqihi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Not by what his master took from him before the debt, and he is sued for what remains after his emancipation. The commentary explains that the slave's debt is paid from his price and earnings; if any debt remains after emancipation, he is sued. The note in the commentary clarifies the difference between earnings used for debt payment and earnings taken by the master before the debt. The ruling is from the Hanafi madhhab as cited in Hashiyat al-Jalbi.

Master's Right to Collect Ghulla from Debt-Laden Slave
Ruling with proof: master's right to ghulla despite slave's debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits the master to take ghulla from a slave with debt, using istihsan to override qiyas, and apply the ruling to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits the master to take ghulla from a debt-laden slave because preserving the slave's ability to earn benefits the creditors.

When a trading slave owes debts, can the master still take the regular income (ghulla)? Strict analogy (qiyas) suggests the debt should take priority. However, the Hanafi school, following a juristic preference (istihsan), allows the master to take ghulla. This lesson unpacks the reasoning from Al-Hidayah and shows how istihsan operates to achieve a balanced outcome.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify that the master may take ghulla despite the existence of debt on the slave.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi.
2
Recognize the Qiyas (Analogy) That Would Prohibit
Understand the strict analogy: debt is prior to the master's right, so the master should not take ghulla.
This is the qiyas that the istihsan overrides.
3
Identify the Istihsan (Juristic Preference)
Note that the Hanafi school uses istihsan to permit the taking of ghulla, despite the qiyas.
Istihsan is a recognized source in the Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the Reasoning Behind Istihsan
The istihsan is based on benefit to the creditors: if the master cannot take ghulla, he may revoke the permission to trade, thus hindering the slave's earnings and harming the creditors.
Preserving the permission to trade is key.
5
Apply the Distribution of Income
The slave's earnings are first used to satisfy the master's ghulla, and the remainder goes to the creditors. If earnings are insufficient, the creditors can claim from the slave's person (raqabah).
The text explicitly states this order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللسيد أخذ غلة مثله مع وجود دين، وما زاد للغرماء ملكه، وتعلقه بالكسب لا يُنافي تعلقه بالرقبة، فيتعلّق بهما، غير أنه يبدأ بالكسب في الاستيفاء إيفاء لحقِّ الغرماء، وإبقاء لمقصود المولى، وعند انعدامه يستوفى من الرقبة. كذا في «الهداية».
(٢) قوله : وللسيد ... الخ ؛ أي للمولى أن يأخذ الغلة وهي الضريبة التي يضرب المولى على العبد كل شهر مع قيام الدين عليه استحساناً، والقياس أن لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ الدين مقدم على حق المولى في الكسب.
وجه الاستحسان : إن في ذلك نفع الغرماء؛ لأنَّ حقهم يتعلق بمكاسبه، ولا تحصل المكاسب إلا ببقاء الإذن في التجارة، ولو منعناه عن أخذ الغلة يحجر عليه، فيفسد باب الاكتساب. كذا في «الكفاية».
(٣) قوله : غلة ؛ الغلة : كلّ ما يحصل من ريع الأرض أو كرائها أو أجرة غلام أو نحو ذلك. كذا في «العناية».
[٤]قوله : للغرماء ؛ لعدم الضرورة فيها، وتقدّم حقهم.
And the master may take the like of its ghulla while debt is present, and what is extra is for the creditors. Its attachment to earnings does not negate its attachment to the person, so it attaches to both, except that it begins with earnings in fulfillment, to pay the right of the creditors and preserve the master's purpose, and when it is absent, it is fulfilled from the person. Thus in 'Al-Hidayah'. (2) He said: 'And the master, etc.' That is, the master may take the ghulla, which is the tax the master imposes on the slave every month while the debt is upon him by istihsan, and the qiyas is that it is not permissible because the debt is prior to the master's right in the earnings. The face of istihsan: In that is benefit for the creditors, because their right attaches to his earnings, and earnings are not obtained except by the continuation of the permission in trade, and if we prevent him from taking the ghulla, he would impose a restriction, thereby spoiling the means of acquisition. Thus in 'Al-Kifayah'. (3) He said: 'Ghulla'; al-ghulla is everything obtained from the yield of land or its rent or the wage of a slave or the like. Thus in 'Al-Inayah'. [4] He said: 'For the creditors'; because there is no necessity in it, and their right is prior.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling that the master may take the ghulla (the monthly income) from the slave even while the slave has outstanding debt. The qiyas (analogy) would disallow it because the debt has priority over the master's right in the earnings. However, istihsan permits it because it benefits the creditors: the slave can only earn if the permission to trade remains, and if the master cannot take ghulla, he would revoke the permission, thus harming the creditors' interests. The ruling is taken from the Hanafi madhhab and is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
غلة (ghulla): The regular income or tax that the master takes from the slave, such as from land rent or wages.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference used to override qiyas for a more equitable outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the master's right is absolute over the slave.
B) Because the qiyas clearly allows it.
C) Because it benefits the creditors by keeping the slave's permission to trade active.
D) Because the slave's debt is secondary to the master's right.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling permits the master to take ghulla from a debt-laden slave by istihsan, overriding qiyas, to benefit the creditors by preserving the slave's earning capacity.

Revocation of a Ma'dhun Slave's Permission Due to Running Away and Other Causes
Ruling on when the permission of a ma'dhun slave is revoked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four grounds for automatic revocation of a ma'dhun slave's permission and explain the Hanafi reasoning behind each, particularly the dispute over abaq.

Core Idea
A slave's permission to trade is revoked upon abaq, death, insanity, or apostasy of the master through indication or loss of legal capacity.

The Hanafi school holds that a ma'dhun slave's permission to trade is automatically revoked in four specific situations, reflecting the non-binding nature of the permission and the requirement that the master maintain legal capacity. This lesson examines each ground, with special attention to the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over whether running away (abaq) alone suffices for revocation. Understanding this ruling clarifies how the school balances implicit indications (dilalah) with explicit permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Grounds
From the matn, list the four events that automatically revoke the slave's trading permission: running away, master's death, permanent insanity, and joining the enemy as an apostate.
These are causes (asbab) for hajr, not conditions for validity.
2
Grasp the Underlying Principle
The principle is that the permission is not binding (ghayr lazim) and for its continuance requires the master's capacity (ahliyyah) to consent. Death, insanity, and apostasy remove this capacity. Running away, while not removing capacity, indicates the master's displeasure.
The principle is stated in Al-Hidayah: 'What is not binding is given the ruling of initiation for its continuance.'
3
Analyze the Abaq Case: Dilalah
Running away is a form of hajr bi al-dilalah: the fact that the slave rebels suggests the master would not consent to him trading. This indication suffices for revocation unless the master explicitly permits otherwise.
Hanafi usul: dilalah is considered valid evidence when there is no contrary explicit statement.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i View
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that abaq does not revoke because the master could initially permit a runaway slave. The Hanafi response distinguishes between initiation (where explicit permission overrides the indication) and continuation (where the indication governs in the absence of explicit consent).
This is the key point of ikhtilaf: the weight given to dilalah.
5
Apply the Ruling to Return from Abaq
According to the text, if the slave returns from running away, the permission does not automatically return. The correct view is that permission once revoked does not revive without a new explicit grant.
Imam Muhammad did not address this, but the later authorities (Al-Inayah, Al-Hidayah) confirm the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينحجر إن أبق أو مات سيده، أو جُن مطبقاً، أو لحق بدار الحرب مرتداً
[١]قوله : وينحجر إن أبق ؛ وإن عاد العبد من الإباق، هل يعود الإذن؟ لم يذكره محمد، والصحيح أنه لا يعود. كذا في «العناية».
[٢] قوله : فإنه يصح ...الخ ؛ أي ابتداء، يعني أنه لو أذن المولى لعبده الآبق في التجارة، وعلم به العبد كان مأذونا، فأولى أن لا ينافيه بقاء؛ لأنه أسهل من الابتداء. كذا في «العناية».
[٣] قوله : ولنا أن دلالة الحجر .. الخ ؛ توضيحه : إن الإباق حجر دلالة؛ لأنَّ المولى إنما يرضى بكونه مأذوناً على وجه يتمكن من تقضية دينه بكسبه، بخلاف ابتداء الإذن؛ لأنَّ الدلالة لا معتبر بها عند وجود التصريح بخلافها. كذا في «الهداية».
[٤] قوله : أما إذا أذنه؛ جواب عن دليل الشافعي له كما هو الظاهر.
[0] قوله : أو مات سيده... إلى قوله : مرتداً؛ أي يحجر في هذه الصور الثلاث العبد المأذون؛ لأنّ الإذن غير لازم وما لا يكون لازماً من التصرف يعطى لدوامه حكم الابتداء، هذا هو الأصل. فلا بد من قيام أهلية الإذن في حالةِ البقاء، والأهلية تنعدم بالموت والجنون المطبق، وكذا بلحوقه بدار الحرب؛ لأنه موت حكمي، ألا ترى أن ماله يقسم باللحوق بدار الحرب بين ورثته. كذا في «الهداية».
And he is revoked if he runs away, or his master dies, or becomes insane, or joins the enemy as an apostate.
[1] He said: 'And he is revoked if he runs away'; and if the slave returns from running away, does the permission return? Muhammad did not mention it, and the correct view is that it does not return. Thus in 'Al-Inayah'.
[2] He said: 'For it is valid, etc.'; i.e., initially, meaning that if the master permitted his runaway slave to trade, and the slave knew of it, he would be a ma'dhun, so it is more fitting that it does not negate continuation, because it is easier than initiation. Thus in 'Al-Inayah'.
[3] He said: 'And we have that the indication of restriction, etc.'; its clarification: Running away is an indication of restriction because the master is only satisfied with him being a ma'dhun in a way that he can pay his debt through his earnings, unlike the initiation of permission, because indication is not considered when there is explicit contrary statement. Thus in 'Al-Hidayah'.
[4] He said: 'But if he permits him'; a response to the proof of al-Shafi'i, as is apparent.
[0] He said: 'Or his master dies ... up to his saying: as an apostate'; i.e., the ma'dhun slave is revoked in these three cases because permission is not binding, and what is not binding from actions is given the ruling of initiation for its continuance; this is the principle. So the capacity for permission must exist in the state of continuation, and capacity is absent with death, permanent insanity, and similarly with joining the enemy because it is a constructive death; do you not see that his wealth is divided upon joining the enemy among his heirs? Thus in 'Al-Hidayah'.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage covers the conditions under which the permission of a ma'dhun slave is revoked. The matn lists four: running away, master's death, master's insanity, and master joining the enemy as an apostate. The commentary first discusses the first: running away. According to the Hanafis, the slave's permission is revoked when he runs away. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it is not revoked because the master can initially permit a runaway slave. The Hanafi response is that there is an indication (dilalah) of revocation because the master would not be content with the slave trading while rebellious; however, if the master explicitly permits, then the indication is overridden. As for the other three cases, they revoke because the permission is not binding and requires the master's legal capacity to continue, which is lost with death, insanity, or apostasy. The commentary also notes that if the slave returns from running away, the permission does not automatically return.

Key Terms
إباق (abaq): The running away of a slave from his master.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction or revocation of permission (in this context, the slave's permission to trade).
دلالة (dilalah): An indication or implied meaning that serves as evidence for a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On the issue of a slave running away (abaq), what is the main point of disagreement between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i?
A) Whether the master's permission can be given to a runaway slave initially.
B) Whether running away automatically revokes the slave's trading permission.
C) Whether the slave can return to the permission after returning from running away.
D) Whether the master can take ghulla from a runaway slave.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's permission is automatically revoked upon abaq, master's death, permanent insanity, or apostasy, and does not automatically return upon return from abaq.

Emancipation of a Slave Earned by a Ma'dhun: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipation effectiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the emancipation of a slave earned by a ma'dhun, and explain the condition regarding the ma'dhun's debt.

Core Idea
Master's emancipation of slave earned by ma'dhun: Abu Hanifa denies, Sahibayn allows with debt-compensation condition.

A ma'dhun is a slave permitted to trade; his earnings are subject to claims of his creditors. When he uses those earnings to purchase a slave, and then the master emancipates that purchased slave, a legal dispute arises: does the emancipation take effect? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagreed on the outcome. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Recognize that the case involves a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade), a slave purchased by that ma'dhun with his earnings, and then the master emancipating that purchased slave.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the emancipation is ineffective; the purchased slave remains a slave. This is because the master's ownership over the ma'dhun's earnings is limited by the creditors' prior claim.
'فلم يعتق' — 'he does not become free'
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the emancipation is effective. The master must compensate the creditors by paying the slave's market value. However, if the ma'dhun's total debt does not exceed his own value plus earnings (i.e., he is not insolvent), the slave becomes free without payment.
'يعتق ويضمن السيد قيمته للغرماء' — 'he becomes free and the master guarantees his value to the creditors'
4
Analyze the condition regarding debt
The Sahibayn's condition 'إن لم يحط دينه' (if his debt does not encompass) means that when the ma'dhun's debts are less than his total wealth (his own person and earnings), the emancipation is fully effective. If debts are greater, the master must pay the slave's value to protect creditors' rights.
The phrase 'برقبته وكسبه' clarifies that the ma'dhun's value includes his person and his earnings.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave remains a slave and creditors may claim him. Under the Sahibayn, the slave becomes free, and the master's liability to creditors depends on the debt situation. The mufta bihi in later Hanafi practice often follows the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يعتق عبد كسبه بإعتاق سيده : أي عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما : يعتق ويضمن السيد قيمته للغرماء ، وعتق إن لم يحط دينه : أي برقبته وكسبه.
So the slave he earned does not become free by the emancipation of his master: that is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [disciples], he becomes free and the master guarantees his value to the creditors, and he becomes free if his debt does not encompass [his property], meaning by his person and his earnings.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) who earns a slave through his own efforts, and then the master emancipates that earned slave. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave does not become free because the master is not considered to have ownership over the slave’s earnings in a way that would allow direct emancipation; the slave's earning are tied to the debt of the ma'dhun. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the slave does become free, and the master must pay his market value to the ma'dhun's creditors. However, if the ma'dhun's total debt does not cover the value of his own person and earnings (i.e., he is not bankrupt), then the emancipation is effective without compensation. This is a classic example of the Sahibayn prioritizing the master's general right of ownership over the slave's acquisitions, while Abu Hanifa restricts that right due to the creditors' prior claim.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; the slave's transactions are valid within the scope of the permission, and his debts are ultimately the master's concern.
غرماء (ghurama'): The creditors of the ma'dhun slave; they have claim on the slave's earnings and may also claim against the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa in this passage, what happens when the master of a ma'dhun emancipates a slave that the ma'dhun earned?
A) The slave becomes free and the master must pay his value to creditors.
B) The slave does not become free at all.
C) The slave becomes free and the master pays the value only if the debt is comprehensive.
D) The slave becomes free if the ma'dhun's debt is less than the slave's value.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipating a slave earned by a ma'dhun hinges on whether creditors' claims take precedence over the master's direct ownership, with the Sahibayn requiring compensation only when the ma'dhun is insolvent.

Sale of Ma'dhun to His Master: Price Restrictions and Validity
Ruling on validity and price limitations in ma'dhun-master sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sale between a ma'dhun and his master when the slave is in debt, and apply the rule regarding price favoritism (muhabah).

Core Idea
A ma'dhun with comprehensive debt treats his master as a stranger; sale is valid only at market price to protect creditors.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a ma'dhun slave is saddled with debt exceeding his assets, his financial independence is curtailed to protect creditors. This lesson examines the rules governing his sale to his own master, focusing on the price limitations and the consequences of overpricing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status of the ma'dhun
The ma'dhun must have debt that encompasses both his property and his person (he is effectively insolvent). Under this condition, his master becomes a stranger to his property for debt purposes.
2
Apply the price rule for the sale
The ma'dhun may sell to his master only at the market value (not less). If he sells for more, the master must either pay the excess to the creditors or annul the sale.
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on selling for less; the relied-upon position is that it is permissible.
3
Analyze the right of retention (habs)
The master has the right to withhold the sold item until payment. If he delivers it before receiving payment, his right to the price is voided because he cannot claim a debt from his own slave.
This protects creditors by ensuring the master does not gain the item without paying.
4
Note the consequence of delivery before payment
If the master delivers the sold item without receiving payment, the price becomes void. The master loses his claim to the price because he relinquished his right of retention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبيع من سيده بمثل القيمة لا بأقل، وسيده منه بمثلها أو بأقل) : أي يجوز بيع المأذون الذي شمل دينه ماله ورقبته من سيده ، وإنما يجوز؛ لأن سيده أجنبي عن ماله إذا كان عليه دين محيط. (فلو باع بالأكثر حط الفضل، أو نقض البيع ) : أي يؤمر السيد بإزالة المحاباة، أو نقض البيع. (وبطل ثمنه لو سلم مبيعه قبل قبضه ، وله حبس مبيعه لثمنه) : أي للسيد حبس المبيع لقبض الثمن، فإن سلم المبيع قبل قبض الثمن، أبطل حقه في العين فلم يبق له حق إلا في الدين، والمولى لا يستوجب على عبده دينا، فيبطل الثمن.
And he sells to his master at the like of the value, not less, and his master [sells] to him at the like or less: meaning the sale from the ma'dhun whose debt has encompassed his property and his person to his master is permissible. It is permissible only because his master is a stranger to his property when he has a comprehensive debt. So if he sells for more, the excess is deducted or the sale is annulled: meaning the master is commanded to remove the favoritism or annul the sale. And his price becomes void if he delivers his sold item before receiving its payment, and he has the right to withhold his sold item for its price: meaning the master has the right to withhold the sold item to receive the price. If he delivers the sold item before receiving the price, he voids his right in the specific item, so no right remains except in the debt, and the master cannot claim a debt from his slave, so the price is void.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage sets forth the rulings on a ma'dhun slave's sale to his own master when the slave is burdened with debt. The key point is that when the slave's debt encompasses both his property and his person (i.e., he is insolvent), the master is treated as a stranger in the slave's financial dealings. Therefore, the sale is permitted, but with price restrictions: the ma'dhun may sell to his master at the market price or less (but not less than the price? Actually the text says 'no less' for the slave, and the master may sell to the slave at the like or less). If the ma'dhun sells for more than the market price, the master is given a choice: either he pays the excess to the creditors (to remove the favoritism) or he annuls the sale. Additionally, the master has the right to withhold the sold property until he receives the full price. If he delivers it before receiving payment, the price becomes void because the master's right was attached to the specific item (the right of retention), and by delivering it he loses that right; he cannot claim a debt from his own slave. This ensures the creditors' interests are protected. The passage also alludes to a dispute (implied in the commentary) about whether selling at less than the value is permissible; the relied-upon position is that it is permissible, as it benefits the creditors by recovering some debt.

Key Terms
محاباة (muhabah): Favoritism or leniency in price; selling below market value or buying above market value to benefit the other party at the expense of creditors.
حبس (habs): The right to retain possession of a sold item until payment of the price is received (right of retention).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a ma'dhun sells an item to his master for more than its market value and the master delivers payment?
A) The sale is void immediately.
B) The master must pay the excess to the creditors or the sale is annulled.
C) The sale is valid and the master keeps the item.
D) The price is reduced to market value.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's sale to his master is valid only at fair market value; overpricing requires excess deduction or annulment, and delivering before payment voids the price.

Freeing a Ma'dhun Slave in Debt: Validity and Guarantee
Ruling with talil on master's guarantee upon freeing indebted ma'dhun slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on freeing a ma'dhun slave in debt and determine the master's guarantee amount.

Core Idea
Freeing an indebted ma'dhun slave is valid; the master guarantees the lesser of the slave's debt or value.

In this passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya, the author establishes that a master may free his ma'dhun slave even while the slave is in debt. The freeing is valid, but the master must compensate the creditors by paying the lesser of the slave's debt or market value. We will examine the rationale behind this ruling and the conditions that affect the guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is freeing (itq) a ma'dhun slave who is in debt. The ruling is that the freeing is valid (sahih).
2
Determine the master's guarantee
The master must guarantee the lesser of the slave's debt and his value. If debt is less, he pays the debt; if value is less, he pays the value.
The creditors' right attaches to the slave's person (raqaba) only up to the value.
3
Understand the talil (rationale)
The master's ownership of the slave remains intact, so he can free him. However, destroying the slave's value that was subject to creditors' rights triggers a guarantee (daman al-itlaf).
Knowledge of the debt is irrelevant to the guarantee.
4
Identify the slave's remaining liability
If the debt exceeds the value, the surplus (fadl dain) remains the slave's personal liability, even after emancipation.
The passage phrase 'wa huwa fadl daini mu'taqan' indicates this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح إعتاقه مديوناً وضمنَ السِّيدُ الأقل من دينه، وقيمته وهو فضل دينه معتقاً

(وصح إعتاقه مديوناً) : أي صح إعتاق المولى العبد المأذون حال كونه مديوناً سواء كانَ الدِّينُ محيطاً أو لم يكن ، لأن ملكه فيه باق.
وضمن السيدُ الأقل من دينِهِ، وقيمته أي إذا كان الدِّينُ أقل من القيمة يضمن الدين، إذ لا حق للغرماء إلا في الدِّين، وإن كانت القيمة أقل من الدِّينِ يضمن القيمة ؛ لأنه تعلّق حقهم بالرقبة، وهو أتلفها ، (وهو فضل دينه معتقاً) : أي ضَمِنَ المأذونُ الذي عتق فضل دينه على القيمة.

[ ١ ] قوله : مديوناً ؛ سواء لزمه الدين بسبب التجارة، أو الغصب، أو جحود الوديعة ، أو إتلاف المال. كذا في «العناية»).
[٢] قوله : لأن ملكه فيه باق ؛ والمولى ضمن القيمة بالغة ما بلغت إذا كان الدين مثلَها أو أكثر منها عَلِمَ بالدين أو لم يعلم به للغرماء ؛ لأنه أتلف ما تعلّق به حقهم معاً، واستيفاء من ثمنه ، وضمان الإتلاف لا يختلفُ بالعلم وعدمه. كذا في «العناية»
[٣] قوله : يضمن القيمة ؛ سواء عَلِمَ المعتق الدين أو لم يعلم به ؛ لأنه أتلف ما تعلق به حقهم بيعاً واستيفاء من ثمنه، وضمان الإتلاف لا يختلف بالعلم وعدمه، فلا يوجب أزيد من مقدار ما أتلفه، فبقي الباقي على العبدِ كما كان، ويطالب بعد العتق ، وهذا معنى قول المصنف الله : وهو فضل دينه معتقاً. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي»(۳).
And his freeing while in debt is valid, and the master guarantees the lesser of his debt and his value, and that is the surplus of his debt over his value at the time of emancipation. (And his freeing while in debt is valid): meaning the emancipation of the ma'dhun slave by the master is valid while he is in debt, whether the debt encompasses or not, because his ownership of the slave remains. And the master guarantees the lesser of his debt and his value: meaning if the debt is less than the value, he guarantees the debt, because the creditors have no right except in the debt; and if the value is less than the debt, he guarantees the value, because their right attached to the person (raqaba) and he destroyed it. (And that is the surplus of his debt over his value at emancipation): meaning the ma'dhun who is freed guarantees the surplus of his debt over the value. [1] His statement: 'in debt'; whether the debt is incurred through trade, usurpation, denial of deposit, or destruction of property. Thus in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'because his ownership of him remains'; and the master guarantees the value, whatever it reaches, if the debt equals it or more, whether he knew of the debt or not, for the creditors, because he destroyed what their right was attached to entirely, and collection from its price, and the guarantee of destruction does not differ with knowledge or lack thereof. Thus in al-Inayah. [3] His statement: 'he guarantees the value'; whether the manumitter knew of the debt or not, because he destroyed what their right was attached to, by selling and collecting from its price, and the guarantee of destruction does not differ with knowledge or lack thereof. So he does not owe more than the amount he destroyed; the remainder remains upon the slave as before, and he is pursued after emancipation. This is the meaning of the author's statement: 'and that is the surplus of his debt over his value at emancipation.' Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi (3).
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya states that a master can free his ma'dhun slave while the slave is in debt, and the freeing is valid. The master must guarantee the lesser of the slave's debt or his market value to the creditors. If the debt is less than the value, he guarantees the debt; if the value is less, he guarantees the value. The slave guarantees any remaining debt after being freed. The commentary explains that the debt can arise from various causes, and the master's guarantee applies regardless of his knowledge of the debt, because the guarantee is for destruction of the slave's value to which creditors had a right.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave by the master's act.
ضمان (daman): Financial liability; here the master's duty to pay the lesser of debt or value to creditors.
فضل دينه (fadl daini): The surplus of the slave's debt over his value after emancipation, which he personally guarantees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a ma'dhun slave's debt is 100 dirhams and his value is 80 dirhams, what does the master guarantee upon freeing?
A) 100 dirhams
B) 80 dirhams
C) 20 dirhams
D) Nothing, the slave is free and debt is void.

Key Takeaway
Freeing a ma'dhun slave in debt is valid; the master guarantees the lesser of debt or value, and the slave remains liable for any surplus.

Minor's Transactions: Hanafi Tripartite Classification and Shafi'i Dispute
States Hanafi ruling and contrasts with Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi threefold classification of a minor's transactions (beneficial, harmful, and neutral) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding the persistence of legal disability after guardian's permission.

Core Idea
A minor's transaction is valid if purely beneficial, invalid if purely harmful, and conditional on guardian permission if mixed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the Hanafi ruling on a minor's transactions, which is nuanced: not all acts are invalid. The school distinguishes three categories based on the nature of the act. The passage then contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's stricter view. Understanding this dispute illuminates Hanafi principles of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and the role of the guardian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three types of transactions mentioned: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and mixed (beneficial and harmful). For each, note whether the guardian's permission is required.
Beneficial: accepting Islam or a gift – valid without permission. Harmful: divorce or freeing a slave – invalid even with permission. Mixed: sale or purchase – suspended on guardian's permission.
2
Understand the rationale behind each ruling
Analyze why the Hanafi school applies different rules: partial capacity suffices for benefit, full capacity is required for harm, and guardian's oversight balances risk in mixed acts.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
State Imam al-Shafi'i's position: no transaction of a minor is valid, even with guardian permission, including acceptance of Islam. Note the specific example of Islam.
4
Examine the Shafi'i dalil
The Shafi'i proof: the minor's hajr (disability) is due to minority itself, which remains after permission. Persistence of cause implies persistence of effect. Unlike a slave's hajr, which is based on master's right and can be waived.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: the guardian does not remove the hajr entirely but allows specific beneficial or neutral transactions. The hajr is for the minor's protection, not absolute; partial capacity combined with guardian permission suffices. The minor can be both under guardianship and capable with permission – the categories are not contradictory because permission creates a temporary exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في حكم تصرف الصبي
وتصرف الصبي إن نفع كالإسلام والاتهاب، صح بلا إذن، وإن ضر كالطلاق والعتاق لا وإن أذنَ به ، وما نفع وضر كالبيع والشراء عُلقَ بإذن وليه) ؛ اكتفاء بالأهلية القاصرة في النافع ، واشتراطاً للكاملة في الضار، ودفعاً للضرر بانضمام رأي الولي في المتردّد بينهما ، وعند الشافعي ) لا يصح تصرفه بإجازة الولى، وكذا لا يصح إسلامه.
[ 1 ] قوله : وتصرف الصبي..الخ ؛ هذا شروع لبيان أحكام إذن الصبي بعد الفراغ من أحكام إذن العبد إلا أنه قدم الأول لكثرة وقوعه، ولكونه مجمعاً عليه في الجواز، لا يقال : قد سبق حالُ الصبي في كتاب الحجر» ؛ لأنا نقول : إنما ذكر هناك لبيان حجره ، وهنا لبيان كونه مأذوناً بإذن وليه ، فلا اعتبار. كذا في حاشية الجلبي) [٢]قوله : وما نفع وضر...الخ ؛ فإن قيل : إذا باع شيئاً بأضعاف قيمته كان نافعاً محضاً، كقبول الهبة، فيجب نفوذه بلا تعليق وتوقف. أجيب : بأن المعتبر في ذلك هو الوضع لا الجزئيات الواقعة اتفاقاً، والبيع في أصل الوضع متردد بينهما. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [٣] قوله : لا يصح تصرفه ...الخ ؛ لأنّ حجره لصباه نفسه، وهو باق بعد الإذن، وبقاء العلة يستلزم بقاء المعلول لا محالة، بخلافِ حجر الرق، فإنه ليس للرق نفسه بل لحق المولى، وهو يسقط بإذنه، لكونه راضياً بتصرّفه حينئذ ؛ ولأنه مولى عليه حتى يملك المولى التصرف والحجر عليه، والمولى عليه لا يكون وليّاً للمنافاة ؛ لأنّ الأول : سمة العجز، والثاني : سمة القدرة، وهما متضادان فلا يجتمعان.
Section on the ruling of the minor's transaction. The transaction of a minor: if it is beneficial, like accepting Islam or accepting a gift, it is valid without permission. If it is harmful, like divorce or emancipation, it is not valid even if he is permitted. And what is both beneficial and harmful, like sale and purchase, is suspended on the permission of his guardian; relying on partial capacity for the beneficial, and stipulating full capacity for the harmful, and preventing harm by including the guardian's opinion in what is ambiguous between them. And according to al-Shafi'i, his transaction is not valid with the guardian's permission, and similarly his Islam is not valid. [1] His statement: 'and the transaction of a minor...' This is the beginning of explaining the rulings of the minor's permission after completing the rulings of the slave's permission, except that he gave precedence to the former due to its frequent occurrence and because there is consensus on its permissibility. It cannot be said: the case of the minor has already preceded in the book of interdiction; we say: it was mentioned there for his interdiction, and here for his being permitted by his guardian's permission, so no repetition. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. [2] His statement: 'and what is both beneficial and harmful...' If it is said: if he sells something for many times its value, it is purely beneficial, like accepting a gift, so it should be valid without suspension. The answer: what is considered is the legal nature (wad'), not the coincidental particulars; sale in its original legal nature is ambiguous between benefit and harm. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. [3] His statement: 'his transaction is not valid...' because his interdiction is due to his minority itself, and it remains after permission, and the persistence of the cause necessitates the persistence of the effect inevitably, unlike the interdiction of slavery, for it is not due to slavery itself but due to the master's right, and it is dropped by his permission, because he is satisfied with his transaction then; and because he is under guardianship (maula 'alayh) so that the guardian has the authority to act and to place interdiction upon him, and the one under guardianship cannot be a guardian (wali) due to contradiction; because the first denotes incapacity, the second denotes capability, and they are opposites so they cannot coexist.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This section (fasl) presents the Hanafi ruling on the legal capacity of a minor (sabi) for transactions. The ruling categorizes transactions into three types: (1) Purely beneficial acts, such as accepting Islam or receiving a gift (hibah), are valid without any permission because the minor's partial legal capacity suffices for benefit. (2) Purely harmful acts, such as divorce (talaq) or freeing a slave (itaq), are invalid even if the guardian permits, because full legal capacity is required for causing harm. (3) Acts that have both potential benefit and harm, like buying and selling, are suspended (mu'allaq) upon the guardian's permission; the guardian's judgment is needed to prevent harm. The reasoning is given: partial capacity suffices for benefit, full capacity is required for harm, and the guardian's input is needed for mixed acts. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that no transaction of a minor is valid even with the guardian's permission, including the acceptance of Islam. The commentary explains that the Shafi'i view is based on the idea that the minor's legal disability (hajr) is due to his minority itself, which remains after permission, so the cause persists and the effect cannot be removed. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the hajr of slavery and that of minority: the slave's hajr is for the master's right, which can be waived by the master; the minor's hajr is for his own protection, but the guardian can still permit beneficial or neutral acts because the minor has partial capacity and the guardian supplements it. The commentary also addresses an objection: if a minor sells an item for many times its value, it is purely beneficial, so should it be valid without permission? The answer is that the classification is based on the legal nature of the act (al-wad'), not on accidental circumstances; sale is inherently mixed, so it is always in the third category. Thus, the Hanafi school provides a nuanced approach balancing protection and facilitation.

Key Terms
أهلية قاصرة (ahliyyah qāsirah): Partial legal capacity that allows a minor to perform acts that are purely beneficial; it is insufficient for harmful acts.
إذن الولي (idhn al-walī): Permission granted by a guardian (father or guardian) for a minor to perform a transaction that is neither purely beneficial nor purely harmful.
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction or disability preventing a person from executing certain transactions; here, the minor's hajr due to minority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a minor's sale of an item considered a mixed transaction requiring guardian permission, even if the sale is at a large profit?
A) Because the minor may change his mind later.
B) Because the legal nature (wad') of sale is inherently ambiguous between benefit and harm.
C) Because the guardian must always supervise all minor's transactions.
D) Because the minor lacks any legal capacity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies a minor's transactions into three types, with validity depending on benefit, harm, and guardian permission, unlike the Shafi'i school which invalidates all.
