The Hanafi Definition of Ghasb (Usurpation)
Definition and conditions of ghasb as a prohibited act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ghasb, identify its four conditions, and explain why removal of the owner's hand is essential.

Core Idea
Ghasb requires removing the owner's hand — not just any wrongful taking.

The text opens the book of ghasb with a precise legal definition, setting the boundaries of liability. Each term in the definition excludes a scenario: dead animals, free persons, non-valuable items, unprotected property, and acts without removal of possession. Understanding these elements is crucial for correctly applying the ruling on usurpation and distinguishing it from other forms of possession.

Guided Path
1
Parse the Definition into Four Conditions
Break down the phrase 'أخذ مال متقوم محترم بلا إذن مالكه يزيل يده' into its four conditions: (1) taking, (2) valuable protected property, (3) without permission, (4) removing the owner's hand. Note how each condition is necessary.
The phrase 'يزيل يده' is a point of difference with the Shafi'i school.
2
Identify Exclusions for Each Condition
For each condition, identify what is excluded: dead animals (not property), free persons (not property), Muslim's wine (not valuable), harbi's property (not protected), deposit (with permission), sitting on a mat (no removal of hand).
These exclusions are explicitly listed in the commentary.
3
Analyze the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Compare the Hanafi requirement of removing the owner's hand with the Shafi'i view that only establishing a wrongful hand suffices. Understand the Hanafi argument that liability (daman) is triggered by removal of the hand.
This dispute has practical consequences: mere touching without displacement may not be ghasb in Hanafi law.
4
Apply to Concrete Examples
Work through the examples given: using a slave (ghasb) vs. sitting on a mat (not ghasb). Determine why one involves removal of the owner's hand and the other does not.
The key is whether the usurper's action establishes a hand of disposal that displaces the owner's control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب [۲] (هو أخذ مال مُتَقَوِّم محترم بلا إذنِ مالكه ، يزيل" يده ) ، فالغصب لا يتحقق في الميتة ; لأنها ليست بمال، وكذا في الحرّ، ولا في خمر المسلم ; لأنها ليست بمتقومة، ولا في مال الحربي ; لأنه ليس بمحترم. وقوله : بلا إذن مالكه ; احتراز عن الوديعة، وإنما قال : يزيل يده ; لأنَّ عند أصحابنا هو إزالة اليد المحقة بإثبات اليد المبطلة، وعند الشافعي ) هو إثبات اليد المبطلة، ولا يشترط إزالة اليد قلنا : كلامنا في الفعل الذي هو سبب للضمان ، وهو إزالة اليد. [١] قوله : كتاب الغصب ; المناسبة بين كتاب الغصب» و«كتاب المأذون»: أنّ المأذون يتصرّف في الشيء بالإذن الشرعي، والغاصب يتصرف لا بإذن شرعي، فكان بينهما مناسبة المقابلة، إلا أنه قدم «كتاب المأذون ; لأنه مشروع، والغصب ليس بمشروع. كذا في «غاية البيان». [٢) قوله : الغصب في اللغة: عبارة عن أخذ الشيء مالاً كان الشيء أو غيره ، يقال : غصب زوجة فلان أو ولده من الغير على سبيل التغلب للاستعمال فيه من أهل اللغة، وفي الشريعة ما قال المصنف ه بقوله : أخذ مال... الخ، والمناسبة بينهما ظاهرة. [٣] قوله : يزيل يده ; أي على وجه يزيلُ يده، يعني أنه إن كان في يده أو يقصرُ يده إن لم يكن في يده، كما إذا غصب من يد المرتهن أو المستأجر أو المودع فإنّ الغاصب قصر يد المالك عن ماله في هاتيك الصور. كذا في «نتائج الأفكار»). [٤] قوله : يزيل يده ; حتى كان استخدام العبدِ وحمل الدابة غصباً دون الجلوس على البساط ; لأنه بالاستخدام والحمل ثبت يد التصرُّف عليه، وذلك يوجب زوال يد المالك عنه دون الجلوس على البساط ; لأنه لم يوجد فيه النقل والتحويل، والبساط فعل المالك ، وقد بقي أثر فعله في الاستعمال ، فلم يكن الغاصب مزيلاً يده.
The Book of Usurpation. [2] (It is taking valuable protected property without the permission of its owner, removing his hand.) So ghasb does not occur in a dead animal because it is not property, nor in a free person, nor in a Muslim's wine because it is not valuable, nor in the property of a harbi because it is not protected. His saying: without the permission of its owner; is a precaution against a deposit (wadi'a). And he only said: removing his hand; because according to our companions, it is the removal of a rightful hand by establishing a wrongful hand, while according to al-Shafi'i, it is the establishment of a wrongful hand, and removal of the hand is not required. We say: our discussion is about the act which is the cause of liability, which is removal of the hand. [1] His saying: The Book of Usurpation; the connection between the Book of Usurpation and the Book of the Permitted Minor is that the permitted minor acts on something with legal permission, while the usurper acts without legal permission, so between them is the connection of contrast. However, he presented the Book of the Permitted Minor because it is lawful, whereas usurpation is not lawful. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. [2] His saying: ghasb in language is an expression for taking something, whether it is property or otherwise. One says: he usurped so-and-so's wife or child from another by way of overpowering, as used by linguists. In sacred law, it is what the author said: taking property... etc. The connection between them is apparent. [3] His saying: removing his hand; meaning in a manner that removes his hand, i.e., if it was in his hand, or restricts his hand if it was not in his hand, as when one usurps from the hand of a mortgagee, lessee, or depositary, the usurper restricts the owner's hand from his property in those cases. Thus in Nata'ij al-Afkar. [4] His saying: removing his hand; such that using a slave or carrying an animal is ghasb, but not sitting on a mat, because by using and carrying, a hand of disposal is established, which necessitates the removal of the owner's hand; unlike sitting on a mat, because there is no transfer or moving of it, and the mat is the action of the owner, and the trace of his action remains in the use, so the usurper is not removing his hand.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines ghasb (usurpation) as a legal term. The definition includes four conditions: (1) taking (akhdh), (2) property that is valuable (mutaqawwim) and protected (muhtaram), (3) without the owner's permission, and (4) removal of the owner's hand (izalat al-yad). The commentary clarifies what is excluded: dead animals (not property), free persons (not property), a Muslim's wine (not valuable because it is not considered wealth in Islamic law), and the property of a harbi (non-Muslim at war) is not protected. The phrase 'without permission' distinguishes ghasb from a deposit (wadi'a), which is with permission. The requirement of removing the owner's hand is a point of difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: Hanafis consider removal of the rightful hand essential, while Shafi'is consider merely establishing a wrongful hand sufficient. The Hanafi response is that the act causing liability (daman) is the removal of the hand. The commentary also discusses the connection between this book and the previous book on the permitted minor, noting that one deals with lawful action and the other with unlawful. It provides linguistic and legal nuances, examples of what constitutes ghasb (using a slave, riding an animal) and what does not (sitting on a mat without moving it), based on whether the usurper's action displaces the owner's control.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Taking valuable protected property without the owner's permission, removing the owner's hand.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally considered valuable and permissible to own and trade.
محترم (muhtaram): Legally protected; property of a Muslim or dhimmi is protected, whereas property of a harbi (non-Muslim at war) is not.
يزيل يده (yuzil yadahu): Removes the owner's hand/possession from the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, which of the following actions constitutes ghasb?
A) Eating a dead animal found on the road.
B) Taking a Muslim's wine without permission.
C) Using someone's slave without permission.
D) Sitting on someone's mat without removing it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ghasb is specifically defined by four conditions, the most distinctive being the removal of the owner's hand, which triggers liability for damages.

Definition of Ghasp and Its Conditions
Defines ghasp and explains each condition with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ghasp, explain each of its four conditions, and identify what is excluded from each condition, including the dispute with al-Shafi'i regarding removal of the owner's hand.

Core Idea
Ghasp requires taking valuable protected property without permission, and crucially, removing the owner's hand.

This lesson examines the precise legal definition of ghasp (usurpation) as established in the Hanafi school. The definition is not merely about taking another's property; it is a technical term with specific conditions that determine when liability arises. We will parse each condition, understand its exclusions, and explore a key difference with Imam al-Shafi'i that has practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Recognize that ghasp is defined as a specific act (taking) and is inherently haram. The passage begins with the definition.
The ruling is haram, but the passage focuses on definition, not on the ruling per se.
2
List the Four Conditions
From the definition 'taking valuable protected property without permission, removing his hand', extract the four conditions: (1) property must be valuable (muttaqawwim), (2) property must be protected (muhtaram), (3) taking without permission of owner, (4) removal of owner's hand.
Each condition excludes certain items or situations.
3
Apply Exclusions to Each Condition
For each condition, identify what is excluded: valuable excludes dead animals and Muslim's wine; protected excludes property of a war enemy (harbi); without permission excludes deposits (wadi'ah); removal of hand is the point of dispute.
These exclusions illustrate the boundaries of ghasp.
4
Analyze the Dispute with al-Shafi'i
The Hanafi school requires removal of the owner's hand as a separate condition; al-Shafi'i does not. The commentary explains that the act causing guarantee (damān) is removal, not mere establishing of a wrongful hand.
This dispute has practical effects, e.g., on liability for accessions (zawa'id).
5
Apply to Concrete Cases
Using the condition of removal of hand, differentiate: riding someone's animal or using a slave is ghasp because it removes the owner's control; sitting on a mat without moving it is not ghasp because no removal occurs.
This step grounds the definition in tangible examples.

Book of Ghasp: It is taking valuable property that is protected without the permission of its owner, removing his hand. [Commentary: So ghasp does not occur in a dead animal because it is not property, nor in a free person, nor in a Muslim's wine because it is not valuable, nor in the property of a war enemy because it is not protected. His saying: without the permission of its owner; excludes the deposit. He said: removes his hand; because according to our companions it is removal of the rightful hand by establishing a wrongful hand, and according to al-Shafi'i it is establishing the wrongful hand, and removal of the hand is not stipulated. We say: our discussion is about the act which is the cause of guarantee, and it is removal of the hand. Footnote [1]: The connection between the Book of Ghasp and the Book of the Authorized: that the authorized acts on something with legal permission, while the usurper acts without legal permission, so there is a connection of contrast. Except that he prefaced the Book of the Authorized because it is lawful, while ghasp is not lawful. As in Ghayat al-Bayan. [2] His saying: ghasp in language: it is taking something, whether property or otherwise. It is said: he usurped someone's wife or child from another by way of overpowering, as used by linguists. In Shari'ah it is what the author said: taking property... etc. The connection between them is apparent. [3] His saying: removes his hand; i.e. in such a way that he removes his hand, meaning that if it was in his hand, or he shortens his hand if it was not in his hand, as when he usurps from the hand of a pledgee, lessee, or depositee, the usurper shortens the owner's hand from his property in those cases. As in Nata'ij al-Afkar. [4] His saying: removes his hand; so that using a slave and riding an animal are ghasp, unlike sitting on a mat, because with using and riding, a hand of disposition is established over it, which necessitates removal of the owner's hand, unlike sitting on a mat because there is no transfer and relocation, and the mat is the owner's act, and the effect of his act remains in the usage, so the usurper is not removing his hand. (1) See: Tashih al-Tanbih by al-Nawawi (p.79), etc. (2) Nata'ij al-Afkar (9:316).
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines ghasp (usurpation) in a precise legal manner, parsing each condition. The author begins by stating that ghasp is taking valuable (muttaqawwim) property that is protected (muhtaram) without the permission of its owner, in a way that removes the owner's hand (control). The commentary then explains what is excluded: dead animals (not property), free persons (not property), a Muslim's wine (not valuable because it is not permissible for a Muslim to own it), and the property of a war enemy (not protected because it is permissible to take it as booty). The phrase 'without permission' excludes deposits (wadi'ah), because the depository has implicit permission. The condition 'removes his hand' distinguishes ghasp from mere establishing of a wrongful hand, according to the Hanafi school. The text notes a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who holds that simply establishing a wrongful hand suffices for ghasp, even without removing the owner's hand. The Hanafi response explains that the act causing liability is the removal. The commentary also gives examples: using a slave or riding an animal constitutes ghasp because the usurper exerts control and removes the owner's hand, but sitting on a mat does not because it involves no transfer and the owner's effect remains.

Key Terms
مَال مُتَقَوِّم (mal muttaqawwim): Property that has legal value in Shari'ah; i.e., permissible to own and benefit from. Excludes dead animals, wine, and other non-valuable items.
مَال مَحْتَرَم (mal muhtaram): Property that is inviolable and protected by law; excludes property of a war enemy (harbi) and property of a Muslim apostate (in some interpretations).
إزالة اليد (izalat al-yad): Removal of the owner's physical or legal control over the property. This is a necessary condition for ghasp according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, which of the following is NOT a condition for ghasp (usurpation) to occur?
A) The property must be valuable (muttaqawwim).
B) The property must be protected (muhtaram).
C) The taking must be with the permission of the owner.
D) The taking must remove the owner's hand over the property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of ghasp requires four conditions: valuable property, protected property, taking without permission, and removal of the owner's hand—the last being a point of dispute with al-Shafi'i with practical consequences.

The Definition of Ghasp and Its Effect on Accessions and Actions
Expansion of ghasp definition through inter-madhhab dispute and practical applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis do not guarantee accessions of usurped property and distinguish when using a slave or riding an animal creates liability for compensation.

Core Idea
The Hanafi ghasp definition requires removal of the owner's hand—accessions are not guaranteed because that hand never existed.

This passage explores the practical consequences of the Hanafi definition of ghasp. It contrasts the Hanafi view with al-Shafi'i on accessions, then applies the definition to specific actions like using a slave or riding an animal. Understanding this distinction reveals how a single definitional condition—removal of the owner's hand—drives multiple rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi condition for ghasp
According to Hanafis, ghasp requires both establishing a hand (ithbat al-yad) and removing the owner's hand (izalat al-yad). The passage states that the Shafi'i view does not require removal. Confirm this from the text.
Al-Shafi'i holds that mere establishment of a wrongful hand suffices.
2
Apply this condition to accessions
Accessions like a child born to a usurped female slave or fruit from a usurped orchard were never in the owner's hand. Therefore, the usurper's hand did not remove the owner's hand from them, so no ghasp occurs for the accession itself. Consequently, accessions are not guaranteed (ghayr marmuna).
The Hanafi definitional condition logically excludes accessions from the guarantee.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i does not require removal of the owner's hand. Hence, establishing a wrongful hand over the original property also establishes it over accessions. This leads to accessions being guaranteed (marmuna).
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is clear: if the child is lost, the Hanafi usurper owes nothing; the Shafi'i usurper owes compensation.
4
Apply the definition to using a slave or riding an animal
Using a slave for one's own work or riding an animal is ghasp because the usurper is controlling the property and removing the owner's hand (even temporarily). However, liability for compensation depends on actual damage or destruction.
If the slave is used for someone else's work, no guarantee arises. If the rider dismounts and leaves the animal unharmed, no guarantee because no transport (naql) occurred.
5
Distinguish liability based on destruction vs mere use
If the animal dies from the riding or carrying, the usurper guarantees even without moving it (due to direct destruction). Similarly, if the slave is destroyed by the work, guarantee applies. Mere use without damage does not create guarantee.
This shows that the basis for compensation can be either ghasp (with transport) or itlaf (destruction).

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of ghasp by detailing its consequences, particularly in an inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position, based on the condition of removal of the owner's hand, holds that accessions (zawa'id) of usurped property—such as the offspring of a female slave or fruit of an orchard—are not guaranteed (i.e., the usurper does not have to compensate for them if they are lost) because the usurper never established a hand over these accessions; the owner's hand was not on them to be removed. Al-Shafi'i, who does not require removal of the hand, would guarantee accessions because establishing a wrongful hand over the original property suffices. The text then addresses specific actions: using a slave (e.g., for work) and riding an animal constitute ghasp, but they only create liability for compensation (daman) if the property is damaged or destroyed. Using a slave for himself creates guarantee, but using for someone else does not. Riding an animal guarantees the owner's share, but if the rider dismounts and leaves the animal in its place, no guarantee arises because there is no transport (naql). However, if the animal dies from the riding or carrying, the usurper guarantees even without moving it, because destruction by his act is a separate basis for liability.

Key Terms
زوائد المغصوب (zawa'id al-maghsub): Accessions or additions to the usurped property, such as offspring, fruit, or any increase that occurs while the property is in the usurper's possession.
إثبات اليد (ithbat al-yad): Establishing or placing a hand (control) over property. In ghasp, it refers to the usurper's act of taking control.
مضمون (marmun): Subject to guarantee or compensation; if property is marmuna, the usurper must compensate its value if lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fruit of the difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the definition of ghasp?
A) Whether ghasp is haram or makruh.
B) Whether accessions of usurped property are guaranteed.
C) Whether using a slave is considered ghasp.
D) Whether immovable property can be usurped.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of ghasp—requiring removal of the owner's hand—excludes accessions from guarantee, and liability for actions like riding or using depends on actual destruction or transport.

10 min
Accessions of Usurped Property: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on condition of ghasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on guarantee of accessions of usurped property and explain how it differs from al-Shafi'i's view based on the definition of ghasb.

Core Idea
Accessions of usurped property are not automatically guaranteed because the owner's hand was never removed from them.

The Hanafi school defines ghasb (usurpation) by the removal of the owner's hand (izalat yad). Al-Shafi'i holds that merely establishing a hand of transgression suffices. This definitional difference leads to a practical divergence: do accessions (zawa'id) arising after the usurpation automatically become guaranteed? In this lesson, we unpack the debate, the proofs, and the real-world consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core dispute is whether removal of the owner's hand is a necessary condition for ghasb. Determine what each school requires and why it matters for accessions.
Accessions are things like offspring or fruit that emerge after the act of usurpation.
2
State the Hanafi position and its condition
The Hanafi school holds that ghasb requires izalat yad al-malik (removal of the owner's hand). Without this, the act may be wrongful but does not meet the definition of ghasb for guarantee purposes.
Thus, accessions are not automatically guaranteed because the owner's hand was never removed from them.
3
State the Shafi'i counter-position
Al-Shafi'i argues that simply establishing a hand of transgression (ithbat yad al-'udwan) is sufficient. This means accessions are also covered under the guarantee of usurpation.
The Shafi'i view expands the scope of ghasb to include everything that comes under the usurper's control.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Apply each school's rule to a concrete example: a usurped pregnant slave gives birth. For Hanafis, the child is not automatically guaranteed; the owner must claim it separately. For Shafi'is, the child is immediately part of the guarantee.
This difference affects lawsuits and compensation.
5
Apply the Hanafi analysis to a new scenario
Consider a usurped garden that bears fruit. According to Hanafis, the fruit is not automatically guaranteed because it grew after the owner's hand was removed only from the land, not from the fruit itself.
The fruit is owned by the owner, but the usurper may be liable for costs or damages separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : لأن إثبات اليد...الخ ؛ أي لانعدام حد الغصب الذي ذكره المصنف . [٣] قوله : بدون إزالة اليد ؛ لأنها ما كانت ثابتة على هذه الزيادة حتى يزيلها الغاصب.
And according to al-Shafi'i, the removal of the owner's hand from the usurped property is not a condition, rather establishing the hand of transgression upon it is sufficient for the realization of usurpation. The fruit of the difference appears in the accessions of the usurped property, such as the child of a female slave and the fruit of a garden: they are not guaranteed according to us because the definition of usurpation we mentioned is absent. As in al-Kifayah. He said: 'Indeed, the accessions of the usurped property, such as the child of a female slave and the fruit of a garden.' [2] He said: 'Because establishing the hand...etc.' meaning because the definition of usurpation mentioned by the author is absent. [3] He said: 'Without removing the hand' because the hand was not established over this accession such that the usurper could remove it.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the definition of ghasb (usurpation). The Hanafi school, represented by the author, holds that a necessary condition for ghasb is the removal (izalah) of the owner's hand from the property by the usurper. Mere establishment of a hand through transgression (ithbat yad al-'udwan) without removal is insufficient. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, considering the act of taking control forcefully enough. The practical consequence (thamarat al-ikhtilaf) concerns the accessions (zawa'id) of the usurped property, such as a child born to a slave woman or fruit from a garden. Since the owner's hand was never removed from these accessions—they came into existence after the usurpation—the Hanafis rule that they are not automatically guaranteed (mamunah) by the usurper. The owner may, however, claim them, but not as a necessary consequence of the act of usurpation. The passage also includes a brief commentary identifying the accessions and clarifying that the hand was never established on them.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Taking another's property unjustly in a way that removes the owner's hand (izalat yad al-malik), according to Hanafi definition.
زوائد المغصوب (zawa'id al-maghsub): Accessions that arise from the usurped property after the act of usurpation, such as offspring or fruit.
إزالة يد (izalat yad): Removal of the owner's control or hand from the property; a condition for ghasb in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, are the accessions (zawa'id) of a usurped property, such as the offspring of a slave, automatically guaranteed (mamunah) by the usurper?
A) Yes, they are automatically guaranteed because they are part of the usurped property.
B) No, they are not automatically guaranteed because the owner's hand was never removed from them.
C) It depends on whether the accessions are physical or non-physical.
D) Yes, but only if the usurper knew he was usurping.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not automatically guarantee accessions of usurped property because the owner's hand was never removed from them, a direct consequence of defining ghasb by izalat yad.

Ghasb: Using a Slave, Loading an Animal, and Sitting on a Mat
Explanation of which physical uses constitute ghasb and which do not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which physical uses of another's property constitute ghasb and which do not, and apply the conditions (use for own benefit, movement, disposal) to concrete scenarios.

Core Idea
Ghasb requires an act of disposal (tasarruf) that removes the owner's hand; sitting is not ghasb because it lacks such disposal.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies three acts: using a jointly-owned slave, loading or riding an animal, and sitting on a mat. The key legal principle is that ghasb is not established merely by physical contact; it requires an act that amounts to taking possession or disposal. By examining these examples, you will learn the Hanafi boundary between ghasb and non-ghasb actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts and their default rulings
Note that using a slave and loading an animal are initially ruled ghasb, while sitting on a mat is not. This sets up the need for conditions.
2
Isolate the condition for each act
For the slave: using for oneself (not for the owner's work). For the animal: movement (naql) from its place. For the mat: the absence of disposal (tasarruf) makes it non-ghasb.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
Ghasb requires either (a) an act that physically transfers the item (naql) or (b) an act that destroys it (itlaf). Mere use without removal is not enough.
The analogy: using a slave is like loading an animal – both require movement to constitute ghasb, but destruction triggers liability even without movement.
4
Apply the conditions to edge cases
If the animal perishes during riding but the animal was not moved, the rider is still liable for destruction. If the slave dies while being used for the owner's work, no liability for ghasb.
The text mentions 'itlaf' as a separate basis for guarantee.
5
Synthesize the rule
Formulate: Ghasb occurs when a person uses another's property for his own benefit with an act of disposal that removes the owner's hand. Sitting does not achieve this.
The owner's hand remains through the act of spreading the mat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاستخدام العبد، وحمل الدابة غصب لا جلوسه على البساط. قوله : فاستخدام العبد؛ أي ولو كان مشتركاً، وهذا لو استعمله لنفسه، فلو في عمل غيره لا ضمان. كذا في «ردّ المحتار». قوله : وحمل الدابة ; أي ولو مشتركة، وكذا ركوبها، فيضمن نصيب صاحبها، ولو ركب فنَزلَ وتركها في مكانها لم يضمن ; لأنَّ الغصب لم يتحقق بدون النقل. كما في «المحيط». وينبغي أن يكون الاستخدام كذلك، لكن إذا تلف بنفس الحمل والركوب يضمن، وإن لم يحولها ; لوجود الإتلاف بفعله كما يأتي، وكذا يضمن ببيع حصته من الدابة المشتركة، وتسليمها للمشتري بغير إذن شريكه. كذا في «ردّ المحتار». [١]قوله : لا جلوسه على البساط ; فإنّ الجلوس عليه ليس بتصرف ; فلهذا لا يرجح به على المتعلق به عند التنازع ، فلم يصر في يده ، والبَسْطُ [فعل المالك]، فينبغي أن تثبت يد المالك عليه ما بقي أثر عمله لانتفاء ما يزيل يده بالنقل والتحويل. كذا في الزيلعي
Using a slave and loading an animal is ghasb, but sitting on a mat is not. He said: 'Using a slave means even if the slave is jointly owned, and this is if he uses him for himself; if he uses him for someone else's work, there is no guarantee.' As in Radd al-Muhtar. He said: 'Loading an animal means even if the animal is jointly owned, and likewise riding it; he guarantees the share of its owner. If he rides and then dismounts and leaves it in its place, he does not guarantee because ghasb is not realized without transportation.' As in al-Muhit. And it should be that using is similar, but if the slave perishes by the very act of loading or riding, he guarantees even if he did not move it, due to the existence of destruction by his act as will come. Likewise, he guarantees by selling his share of the jointly owned animal and handing it over to the buyer without the partner's permission. As in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] He said: 'Sitting on a mat is not ghasb because sitting is not an act of disposal; thus it does not give priority over the claimant in a dispute, and he does not come into possession of it. The mat [is the owner's action], so the owner's hand should be established over it as long as the trace of his work remains, due to the absence of anything that removes his hand through transportation or transfer.' As in al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's rulings on specific acts. It clarifies three scenarios: (1) Using a slave (istikhdam) is ghasb if done for one's own benefit, even if the slave is jointly owned, but not if done for the owner's work. (2) Loading or riding an animal is ghasb only if transportation (naql) occurs; merely riding and dismounting without moving does not constitute ghasb. However, if the animal perishes due to the riding, the person is liable because of destruction (itlaf). There is also a ruling that selling one's share of a jointly owned animal without the partner's permission is ghasb of the partner's share. (3) Sitting on a mat (julus ala al-bisat) is not ghasb because sitting does not involve an act of disposal (tasarruf) that removes the owner's hand; the owner's hand remains through the work of spreading the mat. The reasoning is that the condition for ghasb is transporting or transferring the item, which is absent in sitting.

Key Terms
استخدام (istikhdam): Using a slave for one's own benefit; considered ghasb in this context.
حمل الدابة (haml al-dabbah): Loading an animal with goods; considered ghasb if transportation occurs.
جلوس على البساط (julus 'ala al-bisat): Sitting on a mat; ruled not to be ghasb because it does not involve disposal or removal of the owner's hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rulings in this passage, which of the following acts constitutes ghasb?
A) Sitting on a mat belonging to another person without moving it.
B) Loading an animal with goods but not moving the animal from its place.
C) Riding a jointly owned animal without the partner's permission and moving it from its place.
D) Using a joint slave for the work of someone other than the owner.

Key Takeaway
The key for ghasb is an act of disposal (tasarruf) that removes the owner's hand; sitting, mere use without movement, or using for the owner's work does not constitute ghasb.

Legal Consequences of Ghasb: Sin, Return, and Compensation
Ruling on the obligation to return and the sin for intentional usurpation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of ghasb (sin, return, compensation) and distinguish between intentional and unintentional usurpation regarding sin.

Core Idea
Ghasb triggers liability to return or compensate regardless of intent; sin only attaches to intentional acts.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies the legal ruling (hukm) of ghasb (usurpation). It explains that the obligation to return the item or pay compensation applies whether the act was intentional or accidental. However, sin (ithm) is only incurred when the usurper knew the property belonged to another and deliberately took it. The author then cites Quran and hadith to prove that unintentional acts are exempt from sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ghasb (usurpation). The ruling has two components: a moral component (sin) and a remedial component (return or compensation).
Sin is not part of the legal liability; it is a separate moral judgment.
2
Distinguish between intentional and unintentional
If the usurper knows the item belongs to another and takes it, he commits a sin and must return/compensate. If he acts in error (e.g., thinks it is his own), the obligation to return/compensate remains, but there is no sin.
Intent is determined by knowledge of ownership.
3
Understand the proof for lifting sin
The author cites Quran 33:5 and the hadith 'Rufi'a 'an ummati al-khata' wa al-nisyan' to show that unintentional acts are not sinful.
The hadith is a proof for the removal of sin in cases of error and forgetfulness.
4
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether sin applies and what remedy is required. For example, if someone accidentally takes another's coat, he must return it but has no sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكمه : الإثم لَن عَلِم، وردُّ العين قائمة. [٢]قوله وحكمه الإثم ; ومعنى المسألة : إن كان الغاصب يعلم أن المغصوب ملك المغصوب منه ومع علمه غصب فحكمهُ الإثم وردّ العين إن كانت قائمة والغـرم إن كانت هالكة ، وإن لم يكن الغصب مع علمه بأن ظن أن المأخوذ مالُ نفسه مثلاً كانت المسألة بحالها في جميع ما ذكرنا سوى الإثم ; لأنّ الإثم حينئذ مرفوع عن الغاصب ؛ لقوله : وَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ فِيمَا أَخْطَأْتُم بِهِ، وَلَكِن مَّا تَعَمَّدَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ ) ; ولقوله : «رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان»(4).
And its ruling: sin if he knew, and return of the item if it still exists. [2] He said: 'And its ruling: sin; and the meaning of the issue: if the usurper knows that the usurped property is owned by the one from whom it is taken, and with his knowledge he usurped, then the ruling is sin and return of the item if it still exists, and compensation if it is destroyed. And if the usurpation was not with knowledge—such as he thought the taken item was his own property—then the issue stands regarding everything we mentioned except the sin, because sin is then lifted from the usurper due to His statement: 'There is no blame upon you for what you do unintentionally, but [blame is only for] what your hearts intend' (Quran 33:5) and due to His statement: 'My community is excused for error and forgetfulness.'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage states the general ruling (hukm) of ghasb. It covers two elements: (1) If the usurper knew that the property belonged to someone else and intentionally took it, he commits a sin (ithm) and is obligated to return the item (radd al-'ayn) if it still exists, or pay compensation (ghurm) if it is destroyed. (2) If the usurper acted in error—for example, believing the item was his own—then the obligation to return or compensate remains, but there is no sin because the action was unintentional. The proof for the removal of sin is the Quranic verse (al-Ahzab 33:5) which states that there is no blame for unintentional mistakes, and the hadith 'My community is excused for error and forgetfulness.' This distinction is crucial: the legal consequences of ghasb (return/compensation) apply regardless of intent, but the moral culpability (sin) only attaches to intentional acts.

Key Terms
إثم (ithm): Sin; moral blame associated with intentional usurpation.
رد العين (radd al-'ayn): Return of the actual usurped item if it still exists in its original form.
غرم (ghurm): Compensation; payment for the value of the item if it is destroyed or lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on ghasb, what is the difference between intentional and unintentional usurpation?
A) Only intentional usurpation requires return of the item; unintentional does not.
B) Both require return or compensation, but only intentional usurpation incurs sin.
C) Unintentional usurpation requires compensation but not return; intentional requires both.
D) There is no difference; both are treated identically.

Key Takeaway
The legal obligation to return or compensate in ghasb is independent of intent; sin is added only if the act was intentional.

Definition of Ghasb and Its Basic Ruling
Opening of the Book of Ghasb: definition and basic ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ghasb, explain the ruling of sin and return, and apply the proof from Quran 33:5 and the hadith on error.

Core Idea
Ghasb is taking property wrongfully; sin requires knowledge, but return or compensation is always wajib.

In this lesson, we examine the opening of the Book of Ghasb. The text defines when an action constitutes usurpation and clarifies that moral liability depends on awareness, while the obligation to return the item remains absolute. We will work through the commentator's expansion on the base ruling and the scriptural proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
The text gives the example of sitting on a mat. Determine whether this act constitutes ghasb. The commentator explains that sitting is not a disposal (tasarruf), so it does not establish possession (yad). The owner's hand remains as long as his effect (spreading) persists.
This shows that not every contact is ghasb; possession requires a disposing act.
2
Distinguish the two elements of the ruling
The matn states: 'sin for one who knows, and return of the item if it exists.' Separate the moral (ithm) from the legal (radd/ghurm) obligation.
Sin is conditional on knowledge; return/compensation is unconditional.
3
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites Quran 33:5 and the hadith 'My community is forgiven for error and forgetfulness'. Understand how these proofs remove sin when the usurper is unaware.
The Quranic verse explicitly absolves blame for mistakes.
4
Apply the proof to a scenario
If a person mistakenly thinks an item is his own, sin is lifted. But the obligation to return or compensate remains because property rights are not nullified by mistake.
This distinguishes moral from civil liability.
5
Note the epistemological status of the hadith
The footnote reports that the exact wording is not found in primary hadith sources, yet jurists rely on its meaning. Understand that juristic acceptance (shuhrah) can give operability even if textual transmission is debated.
This highlights the difference between textual authenticity and legal use.

Source Text

Book of Ghasb / Its Definition and Rulings. He does not sit on the mat. And its ruling: sin for one who knows, and return of the item if it exists. [1] His statement: 'He does not sit on the mat'; because sitting on it is not a disposal; for this, it does not give priority over the related party in case of dispute, so he does not become in possession. And spreading [is the act of the owner], so the owner's hand should be established as long as the effect of his action remains, due to the absence of anything that removes his hand by moving or transferring. Thus in al-Zayla'i. [2] His statement: 'And its ruling: sin'; the meaning of the issue: if the usurper knows that the usurped item is the property of the usurped from and with his knowledge of usurpation, then his ruling is sin and return of the item if it exists, and compensation if it perishes. And if the usurpation was not with his knowledge, such as he thought that the taken item was his own property, etc., then the issue remains as we mentioned in all aspects except sin; because sin is then lifted from the usurper due to His saying: 'And there is no blame upon you for what you mistake, but [only] for what your hearts intended' [33:5]; and due to his saying: 'My community is forgiven for error and forgetfulness.' (1) Radd al-Muhtar 6:178. (2) Radd al-Muhtar 5:222. (3) Al-Ahzab:5. (4) Al-Zayla'i said in Nasb al-Rayah 2:75: This is not found in this wording, although all jurists only mention it with this wording. The closest we found is with the wording: 'Allah has lifted from this nation three things,' narrated by Ibn 'Adi in al-Kamil from Abu Bakra, and it is more often narrated with the wording: 'Indeed Allah has overlooked for my nation error and forgetfulness.'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This lesson covers the opening of the Book of Ghasb (usurpation) in the commentary. The matn states two rulings: (1) that sitting on a mat does not constitute possession by usurpation, and (2) the general ruling of ghasb: sin if the usurper is aware, and the obligation to return the item if it remains or pay compensation if it perishes. The commentator explains the first ruling: sitting is not a physical disposal (tasarruf), so it does not establish the sitter's hand (yad) over the mat; rather, the owner's hand continues as long as his effect (spreading) remains, since there is no act removing his hand like moving or transferring. For the second ruling, the commentator clarifies that sin (ithm) is conditional on knowledge: if the usurper knew the item was not his, he is sinful; if he mistakenly thought it was his, there is no sin, based on Quran 33:5 and the hadith on error and forgetfulness. The footnote discusses the hadith's authenticity, noting that although jurists commonly cite it, its exact wording is not found in the primary sources, but similar wording exists.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Taking another's property wrongfully without permission, with forceful possession.
الإثم (al-ithm): Sin; moral liability incurred when the usurper acts with knowledge of wrongdoing.
رد العين (radd al-'ayn): Returning the specific usurped item if it still exists in its original form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He is not sinful but must return the item or compensate.
C) He is free from both sin and liability.
D) He is sinful only if the item perishes.

Key Takeaway
Ghasb incurs sin only if done with knowledge, but the obligation to return the item or compensate is absolute.

Obligation of Mithl for Usurped Mithliyyaat
Explaining that for mithliyyaat, the equivalent (mithl) must be paid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation for usurped mithliyyaat and explain why mithl is obligatory based on Quran and qiyas.

Core Idea
For mithliyyaat, the usurper must pay the like (mithl), not the value.

When someone usurps an item that has an exact equivalent in type and value, and it perishes, what must they pay? This lesson explains that for mithliyyaat—items sold by volume, weight, or uniform count—the obligatory compensation is the mithl (like), as established by the Quran and Hanafi legal reasoning. The commentator also clarifies the criteria for mithli and distinguishes it from qimi items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is usurpation (ghasb) of a mithli item that perishes. The ruling is wajib to pay the mithl (like).
The ruling is derived from the Quran and qiyas.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Quran 2:194, which commands retaliation in kind. This is used as a basis for equivalence in compensation.
The verse is cited in the text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic imperative to retaliate with the like is extended to property compensation via qiyas, with the illa of achieving justice by preserving both genus and value.
The text explains that mithl is more just because it maintains genus and value.
4
Define mithli
Mithli items are those sold by volume (makil), weight (mawzun), or counted with approximation (adadi mutaqarib), and they do not vary by craftsmanship.
The commentator clarifies that 'weighed' here means items whose price is based on weight and are uniform.
5
Distinguish mithli from qimi
Items that vary by craftsmanship, like a kettle or pot, are qimi (value items), not mithli. For qimiyyat, the usurper must pay the market value (qimah).
This distinction is crucial for proper compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغرم هالكة، ويجب المثل في المثلي : كالمكيل، والموزون، والعددي المتقارب 

[1] قوله : الغُرم ؛ - بضم الغين المعجمة، وسكون الراء المهملة - : ما يلزم أداءه. كذا في «الصحاح».

[٢]قوله : ويجب المثل في المثلي ؛ أي من غصب شيئاً له مِثْل : كالمكيل والموزون فهلك في يده فعليه مِثْلُه ؛ لأنَّ الواجب هو المثل ؛ لقوله : فَمَنِ اعْتَدَى عَلَيْكُمْ فَاعْتَدُوا عَلَيْهِ بِمِثْلِ مَا أَعْتَدَى عَلَيْكُمْ . ولأنَّ المثل أعدل لما فيه من مراعاة الجنس والمالية ؛ فلأنّ الحنطة مثل الحنطة جنساً ومالية ؛ لأنَّ ماليّة الحنطة المؤداة مثل ماليّة الحنطة المغصوبة ؛ لأنَّ الجودة ساقط العبرة في الربويات، فكان أدفع للضرر، فإنّ الغاصب فوت على المغصوب منه الصورة والمعنى، فالجبر التام أن يتداركه بما هو مثل [له] صورة ومعنى. كذا في «العناية».

[٣] قوله : والعددي المتقارب ؛ كالجوز والبيض.

اعلم أنَّهُ جعل هذه الأقسام الثلاثة مثلياً مع أنَّ كثيراً من الموزونات ليس بمثلي ، بل من ذوات القيم كالقمقمة والقدر ونحوهما، فأقول : ليس المراد بالوزني مثلاً : ما يوزن عند البيع ، بل ما يكون مقابلته بالثّمن مبيناً على الكيل أو الوزن أو العدد ولا يختلف بالصنعة، فإنَّهُ إذا قيلَ هذا الشيء قفيز بدرهم، أو من بدرهم أو عشرة بدرهم، إنما يقال : إذا لم يكن فيه تفاوت، وإذا لم يكن فيه تفاوت كانَ مثلياً. وإنما قلنا : ولا يختلفُ بالصَّنعة ؛ حتى لو اختلف كالقمقمة
And the compensation [is due] if it perishes, and the like (mithl) is obligatory for mithliyyaat (things with equivalents): such as what is measured by volume (makil), weighed (mawzun), and counted with approximation (adadi mutaqarib). [1] His statement: 'al-ghurm' - with damma on ghayn and sukun on ra - is what is obligatory to pay. Thus in al-Sihah. [2] His statement: 'And the like is obligatory for mithli': meaning whoever usurps something that has a mithl, such as what is measured or weighed, and it perishes in his hand, then he must pay its mithl; because the wajib is the mithl, due to His saying: 'Then whoever has assaulted you, assault him in the manner that he assaulted you' (2:194). And because the mithl is more just, as it takes into account both genus and value; for wheat is like wheat in genus and value; because the value of the wheat paid is like the value of the usurped wheat; because quality is disregarded in ribawi items, so it is more preventive of harm. For the usurper has deprived the owner of both form and meaning, so complete compensation is to make up with something that is like it in form and meaning. Thus in al-'Inayah. [3] His statement: 'and counted with approximation': such as walnuts and eggs. Know that he made these three categories mithli, even though many weighed items are not mithli but rather qimi (value items) like a kettle, a pot, and the like. So I say: the intent of 'weighed' here is not what is sold by weight, but rather what is priced by volume, weight, or count and does not vary by craftsmanship. For if one says 'this thing is a qafiz for a dirham', or 'a mann for a dirham', or 'ten for a dirham', that is only said if there is no variation, and if there is no variation it is mithli. And we said: 'and it does not vary by craftsmanship' so that if it varies like a kettle...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This lesson addresses the compensation for a usurped item that has perished. The matn states that for mithliyyaat (things that have exact equivalents), the usurper must pay the mithl (like). The commentator defines al-ghurm as what must be paid. The proof is from Quran 2:194, establishing equivalence. The commentator explains that mithl is more just because it preserves both genus and value, and in ribawi items, quality is disregarded. The matn lists three categories: makil (measured by volume), mawzun (weighed), and adadi mutaqarib (counted with minor variation). The commentator clarifies that 'weighed' here does not mean items sold by weight but items that are priced by weight and do not vary by craftsmanship. He gives an example: a kettle varies by craftsmanship, so it is a qimi (value item), not mithli. Thus, mithliyat requires payment of the same type, while qimiyyat requires payment of value.

Key Terms
المثلي (al-mithli): An item that has an exact equivalent in type and value, such as those sold by volume, weight, or count with no significant variation due to craftsmanship.
العددي المتقارب (al-adadi al-mutaqarib): Counted items that are approximately equal, like walnuts and eggs, where individual differences are minor.
المثل (al-mithl): The equivalent item that is like the usurped one in genus and value, not considering quality in ribawi items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a mithli according to the text?
A) A handcrafted silver kettle
B) A sack of wheat sold by weight
C) A painting by a famous artist
D) A custom-made suit

Key Takeaway
For mithliyyaat, the usurper must pay the like (mithl) to compensate fully in genus and value.

Definition of Mithli and Obligation of Mithl in Ghasp
Commentary defining mithli and explaining mithl obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mithli in Hanafi fiqh and explain why returning a like (mithl) is wajib for usurped fungibles, citing the Quranic proof and the Hanafi reasoning.

Core Idea
A fungible (mithli) has an exact market equivalent; the usurper must return a like, not value, to achieve complete justice.

This lesson examines the Hanafi definition of fungible goods (mithliyāt) and the obligation to return an exact like (mithl) in cases of usurpation (ghasp). The passage clarifies that not all weighed items are fungible—only those that do not vary significantly in craftsmanship. The ruling is grounded in the Quranic principle of retaliation with a like, applied by qiyas to property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is usurping a fungible item (mithli). The ruling is that the usurper must return an exact like (mithl) if the item perishes.
The obligation is wajib, not fard, because the dalil is zanni al-dalala.
2
Define mithli
Mithli items have a market equivalent without significant variation. The passage categorizes them as makil (measured by volume), mawzun (weighed), and mutaqarib al-adad (countable items of similar type), excluding craftsmanship-dependent items like pots.
Even among weighed items, only those sold by weight without quality variance are mithli.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is Quran 2:194, which commands transgression with a like (mithl).
The word 'mithl' is the operative term.
4
Trace wajh al-dalalah
The verse is extended by qiyas to property because the illa (cause) is preventing harm through equivalent compensation. The like provides complete redress in both genus and value.
Quality is disregarded in ribawi items; hence, exact like compensates fully.
5
Evaluate cross-madhhab status
The passage indicates no dispute; the ruling is muttafaq alayh among Hanafis and widely accepted.
Only the definition of mithli may differ between schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المراد به : الإثم. هذه خلاصة ما في «الكفاية». [١]قوله : الغُرم ؛ - بضم الغين المعجمة، وسكون الراء المهملة - : ما يلزمُ أداءه. كذا في «الصحاح». [٢]قوله : ويجب المثل في المثلي ؛ أي من غصب شيئاً له مِثْل : كالمكيل والموزون فهلك في يده فعليه مِثْلُه ؛ لأنَّ الواجب هو المثل ؛ لقوله : فَمَنِ اعْتَدَى عَلَيْكُمْ فَاعْتَدُوا عَلَيْهِ بِمِثْلِ مَا أَعْتَدَى عَلَيْكُمْ. ولأنَّ المثل أعدل لما فيه من مراعاة الجنس والمالية ؛ فلأنّ الحنطة مثل الحنطة جنساً ومالية ؛ لأنَّ ماليّة الحنطة المؤداة مثل ماليّة الحنطة المغصوبة ؛ لأنَّ الجودة ساقط العبرة في الربويات، فكان أدفع للضرر، فإنّ الغاصب فوت على المغصوب منه الصورة والمعنى، فالجبر التام أن يتداركه بما هو مثل [له] صورة ومعنى. كذا في «العناية». [٣] قوله : والعددي المتقارب ؛ كالجوز والبيض.
And the liability is lost, and the like is obligatory for fungibles: like measured, weighed, and countable items of similar type. Know that he made these three categories fungible even though many weighed items are not fungible but rather of value, like the qamqama and the pot and similar ones. So I say: the intended meaning of 'weighed' is not what is weighed at sale, but rather what is priced against the price based on measure, weight, or count and does not vary by craftsmanship. For when it is said 'this thing is a qafiz for a dirham, or a mann for a dirham, or ten for a dirham', it is only said when there is no difference, and when there is no difference it is fungible. And we only said: 'does not vary by craftsmanship'; because even if it differs like the qamqama and the pot, it is not fungible. Then what does not vary by craftsmanship: either it is not manufactured, or it is manufactured and does not vary like dirhams, dinars, and fulus, all of that is fungible. And if you understand this, you understand the ruling of items sold by length, for everything that is said 'a cubit of this cloth is sold for such-and-such' is only said when there is no difference, and it is what salam is allowed in. For it is known by specifying its length, width, and origin. The jurists have detailed fungibles and value items, and there is no need for that. So what has its like in the markets without significant difference is fungible, and what is not like that is of value items, and what was mentioned of measured items and their sisters is based on this. The intended meaning is sin. This is the summary of what is in al-Kifaya. [1] His saying: al-ghurm - with damm of the ghayn and sukun of the ra - is what is obligatory to pay. Thus in al-Sihah. [2] His saying: And the like is obligatory for fungibles; that is, whoever usurps something that has a like, like measured and weighed goods, and it perishes in his hand, then he must pay its like, because the obligatory is the like, due to His saying: 'Whoever transgresses against you, then transgress against him with the like of what he transgressed against you.' And because the like is more just, as it observes the genus and value; for wheat is like wheat in genus and value, because the value of the wheat given is like the value of the usurped wheat, because quality has no consideration in ribawi items, so it is more repellant of harm, for the usurper has deprived the owner of form and meaning, and complete redress is to compensate with what is like it in form and meaning. Thus in al-Inaya. [3] His saying: 'and countable items of similar type', like walnuts and eggs.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from Al-Wiqayah regarding the obligation of returning a like item (mithl) for usurped fungible goods. The author first explains what 'al-ghurm' means, then clarifies the definition of 'mithli' (fungible) which includes items sold by measure, weight, or countable items that do not vary significantly due to craftsmanship. It addresses a potential misunderstanding: not all weighed items are mithli; only those that are sold by weight without quality variation. The proof is from the Quranic verse (2:194) which commands retaliation with a like, applied by qiyas to property. The Hanafi reasoning is that mithl provides complete compensation in both form and value.

Key Terms
الغرم (al-ghurm): What is obligatory to pay; liability.
المثلي (al-mithli): Fungible item that has an equivalent in the market without significant variation in quality or craftsmanship.
المثل (al-mithl): The like or equivalent of a fungible item, used in compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a mithli item?
A) It is always sold by weight
B) It has a like in the market without significant variation
C) It is always manufactured
D) It must be a food item

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation to return an exact like for usurped fungibles is wajib, based on the Quranic command and the principle of complete justice in both form and value.

Valuation Date When Mithl Is Unavailable: The Three Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on valuation date

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the valuation date of usurped property when the mithl is unavailable, and explain the rationale for Abu Hanifa's mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
When mithl is unavailable, valuation date is not automatic; Abu Hanifa ties it to litigation, protecting the owner's right to wait.

When a usurped item can no longer be replaced with its exact like (mithl) because it has become unavailable in the market, the obligation shifts from returning the mithl to paying its value. But when exactly is that value determined? Our three great Hanafi imams differed on this point: Abu Hanifa held that valuation occurs on the day of litigation, Abu Yusuf on the day of usurpation, and Muhammad on the day the mithl becomes unavailable. Understanding this dispute refines your grasp of how liability transitions from specific performance to monetary compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State each imam's chosen date: Abu Hanifa (day of litigation), Abu Yusuf (day of ghasp), Muhammad (day of inqita').
The passage labels them clearly.
2
Unpack each imam's rationale
For Abu Hanifa: the obligation to pay value is not triggered by mere unavailability, otherwise the owner could be forced to accept value prematurely. For Muhammad: the shift to value occurs when mithl becomes unavailable. For Abu Yusuf: liability arises from the wrongful act itself.
Abu Hanifa's argument is aqli (rational) – protecting the owner's right to wait.
3
Define 'inqita'' (unavailability)
The passage defines unavailability as not being found in the market where it is usually sold, even if found in homes. This is a market-based criterion.
This definition is shared by all three, but they apply it differently to the timing.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if prices rise between the day of usurpation and litigation, the amount owed changes dramatically. Abu Hanifa's view favors the owner by allowing them to wait for the mithl; the other views could force earlier valuation.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن انقطع المثل فقيمته يوم يختصمان) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ لأنَّ القيمة تجب يوم الخصومة ، وعند محمد هل تجب يوم الانقطاع ؛ لأنه حينئذ ينتقل إلى القيمة، وعند أبي يوسف له يوم تحقق السبب ، وهو الغصب، فإنه إذا انقطع المثل. وحد الانقطاع أن لا يوجد في السوق الذي يباع فيه وإن كان يوجد في البيوت. ينظر: الشرنبلالية (٢ : ٢٦٢). [1]قوله : ورقعته ؛ قال الجَوْهَرِيّ : رقعةُ الشيء: أصله وجوهـره. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». [٢) قوله : فإن انقطع المثل...الخ ؛ أي إن لم يقدر الغاصب على مثلِهِ بأن انقطع المثل فعلى الغاصب قيمته يوم يختصمان ؛ أي المالك والغاصب، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لأنَّ النَّقل من المثل إلى القيمة لا يثبتُ بمجرد الانقطاع، إذ لو كانت القيمة ثابتةً بمجرد الانقطاع لكان يجبر المالك على قبول القيمة لو أتى بها الغاصب، ولا يمكن له من الصبر إلى مجيء أوان وجود المثل.
If the like is unavailable, then its value is on the day they litigate. This is according to Abu Hanifa, because the value is due on the day of litigation. According to Muhammad, is it due on the day of unavailability? Because then the obligation shifts to value. According to Abu Yusuf, it is due on the day the cause is realized, which is the usurpation. So if the like is unavailable, the limit of unavailability is that it is not found in the market where it is sold, even if it is found in houses. See: al-Shurunbulaliyya (2:262). [1] His saying: 'wa raq'atihi'; al-Jawhari said: 'raq'at al-shay' means its origin and essence. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'If the like is unavailable...' etc.; meaning if the usurper cannot get its like, because the like is unavailable, then the usurper must pay its value on the day they litigate; that is, the owner and the usurper. This is according to Abu Hanifa, because the transfer from like to value is not established by mere unavailability, because if the value were established by mere unavailability, the owner would be forced to accept the value if the usurper brought it, and he would not be able to wait until the time when the like becomes available.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the timing of valuation when the mithl of a usurped item becomes unavailable. According to Abu Hanifa, the value is determined on the day of litigation (yawm al-khusumah). According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, it is the day the mithl becomes unavailable (yawm al-inqita'), because that is when the obligation shifts to value. According to Abu Yusuf, it is the day of the wrongful act (yawm al-ghasp), which is the cause of liability. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa, as the commentary explains that the obligation to pay value is not established merely by the unavailability of the mithl; the owner is not forced to accept value before the possibility of obtaining the mithl is exhausted.

Key Terms
الانقطاع (al-inqita'): The unavailability of the mithl in the market where it is usually sold, even if found in homes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position on the valuation date when mithl is unavailable?
A) Day of ghasp (usurpation)
B) Day of inqita' (unavailability)
C) Day of litigation (yawm al-khusumah)
D) The day the owner demands payment

Key Takeaway
When the mithl of a usurped item is unavailable, the Hanafi mufta bihi is that value is determined on the day of litigation, protecting the owner's right to wait for the mithl.

Types of Countable Goods and Ruling on Value Substitution
Defines two types of countable goods and their dhaman rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-ʿadadī al-mutafāwit and al-ʿadadī al-mutaqārib and state the Hanafi rulings for substitution (ḍamān) when a usurped item perishes, including the intra-madhhab difference on the assessment date.

Core Idea
For goods with significant individual variance, value (qīmah) is paid; for near-uniform counted goods, a similar item (mithl) is required.

When a usurped item perishes, the usurper must compensate the owner. But what kind of compensation? This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāyah explains that the nature of the item determines the ruling: for items that vary greatly in value (like animals), you pay the market value; for items that are nearly identical (like eggs), you return a similar quantity. It also highlights a key difference between Imam Abū Ḥanīfa and his two disciples regarding when that value is calculated if the usurper destroys the item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of countable goods
The passage introduces al-ʿadadī al-mutafāwit (uneven countable) and al-ʿadadī al-mutaqārib (similar countable). Understand their definitions and examples.
Examples: mutafawit includes animals, garments, fruits like pomegranates; mutaqarib includes walnuts and eggs.
2
Understand the default guarantee rule for each
For mutafawit, the usurper must pay the value (qīmah). For mutaqarib, he must return a similar item (mithl) — like measured goods.
The reason: mutafawit has significant value variance, so no exact like exists.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on assessment date
When the usurper destroys the item (e.g., slaughters a sheep), Abū Ḥanīfa assesses value on the day of usurpation; Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad assess on the day of destruction.
If the item perishes without the usurper's act, all agree on day of usurpation.
4
Examine the exception for mixed goods
When wheat is mixed with barley, the mixture has no exact like, so value must be paid regardless of the original category.
This shows that categorical rulings can be overridden when the item becomes unique.
5
Conclude with the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The difference in assessment date affects the amount of compensation. If the value changes between usurpation and destruction, the owner receives more or less depending on which date is used.
Later Hanafi practice tends to follow Abū Ḥanīfa, but the dispute remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالعددي المتفاوت) : أي الشَّيءُ الذي يعدّ، ويكون أفراده متفاوتة، ولا يراد هاهنا ما يقابلُ بالشَّمنِ مبنياً على العدد : كالحيوان مثلاً ، فإنَّه يعد عند البيع من غيرِ أن يقال : يباع الغنم عشرة بكذا. ثم ذبحها، ضمن قيمتها يوم غصب لا يوم ذبحه عنده ، وعندهما يوم ذبحه، ولو تلفت بلا إهلاكه ضمن قيمتها يوم غصب. [١] قوله : كالعددي المتفاوت ؛ مثل الدوّاب والثياب، وكالرمان والسفرجل والبطيخ، أما العددي المتقارب : كالجوز والبيض فهو كالمكيل، حتى يجب مثلُه ؛ لقلة التفاوت، وفي البر المخلوط بالشعير القيمة ؛ لأنه لا مثل له، هذا كله فيما هلك المغصوب في يد الغاصب.
(Like the countable uneven): That is, the thing that is counted, and its individuals are varying, and what is intended here is not what is sold by weight based on number: such as animals for example, for they are counted at the time of sale without it being said: 'the sheep are sold ten for such-and-such.' Then if he slaughters them, he guarantees their value on the day of usurpation, not the day of slaughter according to him (Abu Hanifa), and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on the day of slaughter. And if they perish without his destruction, he guarantees their value on the day of usurpation. [1] His statement: 'like the countable uneven'; such as beasts of burden and garments, and like pomegranates, quinces, and watermelons. As for the countable similar (close in value): such as walnuts and eggs, then it is like measured goods, so the like is obligatory due to the small variance. And in wheat mixed with barley, the value (is obligatory) because it has no like. All this is concerning when the usurped item perishes in the hand of the usurper.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāyah discusses the ruling on substitute (ḍamān) when a usurped item perishes. It first defines al-ʿadadī al-mutafāwit: items that are counted but each individual differs in value (like animals, garments, fruits). For such items, the usurper must pay the value (qīmah), not a similar item. Then it mentions a difference: if the usurper slaughters the animal (or destroys it), according to Imam Abū Ḥanīfa the value is assessed on the day of usurpation (because that is when the guarantee began). According to the two disciples (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), the assessment is on the day of slaughter (because that is when the destruction occurred). However, if the item perishes without any action by the usurper, all agree the value is from the day of usurpation. Next, it defines al-ʿadadī al-mutaqārib: items that are counted but are nearly uniform in value (like walnuts, eggs). For these, the ruling is like measurable (makīl) items: the usurper must return a similar item (mithl) in quantity, not value. This is because the variance is minimal. Finally, it gives an example of mixed items: wheat mixed with barley has no exact like, so value must be paid. This applies when the item perishes in the usurper's hand.

Key Terms
العددي المتفاوت (al-ʿadadī al-mutafāwit): Items that are counted but the individuals vary significantly in value, such that exact replacement is not possible; hence value must be paid.
العددي المتقارب (al-ʿadadī al-mutaqārib): Items that are counted but are nearly uniform in value, like nuts and eggs, such that a similar item (mithl) can be determined.
قيمة يوم الغصب (qīmat yawm al-ghaṣb): The market value of the usurped item on the day it was taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) On the day of slaughter
B) On the day of usurpation
C) On the day of judgment
D) On the day the owner claims

Key Takeaway
The type of countable good determines whether the usurper pays value or returns a similar item, and the assessment date for value differs within the Hanafi school when the usurper destroys the item.

Primary Obligation: Return of the Usurped Item
States the primary obligation is return of the actual item, supported by hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the primary obligation (wājib aṣlī) in usurpation is returning the actual item (ʿayn) when it exists, and distinguish this from the substitute obligation of paying value.

Core Idea
The usurper must return the very item he took, not its value.

In Hanafi law, when someone wrongfully takes another's property, the first and most fundamental duty is to give back the exact thing taken. This lesson explains why that is, what proofs support it, and a key condition for fulfilling that duty. We'll also see a minority view within the school and why the majority rules as it does.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is returning the actual usurped item (radd al-ʿayn). The ruling given is wājib (obligatory) as long as the item still exists. Determine the level of obligation: it is the primary obligation (al-mūjib al-aṣlī).
The phrase 'عليه رد العين المغصوبة' indicates personal duty.
2
Locate the textual proof (dalīl)
The author cites two ḥadīth: 'The hand is liable for what it has taken until it returns it' and 'It is not permissible for anyone to take the property of his brother... if he takes it, let him return it.' Identify these as aḥādīth (non-mutawātir) and note their strength of transmission (zannī al-thubūt) but clear meaning (qaṭʿī al-dalāla).
The second ḥadīth uses an imperative 'فَلْيَرُدَّهُ' which in Ḥanafī uṣūl indicates obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalāla (how the proof indicates the ruling)
The first ḥadīth establishes liability until return, implying return is the discharge of that liability. The second directly commands return. Also, the reasoning (taʿlīl) is given: 'the hand is an intended right' – the owner's right to possess the specific item has been violated, so restoring that possession is the only complete remedy.
This reasoning supports why returning value is deficient: it does not restore the actual item.
4
Note the intra-Ḥanafī disagreement (ikhtilāf)
Some Ḥanafīs held that the original obligation is the value (qīmah), and returning the ʿayn is merely a substitute. Identify the two positions and the practical difference: for example, if the item is destroyed, the majority view is that the original obligation (ʿayn) has become impossible, so value becomes due; the minority view would treat value as always primary.
The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that the original obligation is returning the ʿayn.
5
Identify the condition: place of return
The passage adds that return must be made in the place where the usurpation occurred, because the value of items can differ by location. This is a condition (sharṭ) for proper fulfillment of the obligation.
If the item is moved, the usurper must bring it back to the original place for return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما ما دام قائماً فعليه رد العين المغصوبة، كما قال المصنف له قبيل هذا بقوله : ورد العين قائمة ؛ لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : على اليد ما أخذت حتى تردّ)، وقال: «لا يحل لأحد أن يأخذ متاعَ أخيه لاعباً ولا جاداً، فإن أخذه فليرده عليه). ولأنَّ اليد حق مقصود، وقد فوّتها عليه، فيجب إعادتها بالردّ إليه، وهو الموجب الأصلي على ما قالوا ، وردّ القيمة مخلص خلفاً ؛ لأنه قاصر، إذ الكمال في ردّ العين والمالية. وقيل : الموجب الأصلي القيمة، ورد العين مخلص، ويظهر ذلك في بعض الأحكام، والواجب الرد في المكان الذي غصبه فيه ؛ لتفاوت القيم بتفاوتِ الأماكن. كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
As long as it remains, he must return the usurped item itself, as the author said previously: 'And return the item as long as it exists,' due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'The hand is liable for what it has taken until it returns it,' and he said: 'It is not permissible for anyone to take the property of his brother in jest or in earnest; if he takes it, let him return it to him.' And because the hand is a intended right, and he has caused its loss, so returning it to him is obligatory, and it is the original obligation according to them, and returning the value is a substitute that delivers from liability because it is deficient, since perfection lies in returning the item and the wealth. And it is said: the original obligation is the value, and returning the item is a substitute, and this appears in some rulings. And the return is obligatory in the place where he usurped it, due to the variation of values by variation of places. As in al-Hidāyah.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage establishes the primary obligation in usurpation: the usurper must return the actual item (ʿayn) as long as it still exists. The author supports this with two hadith: one stating the hand is liable until return, and another prohibiting taking a brother's property even playfully. The reasoning is that the right of ownership over the physical item is intended, and the usurper has removed it from the owner's hand, so he must restore it. Returning the value (qīmah) is only a substitute (khalaf) when the item cannot be returned. It is deficient because the owner loses the specific item. There is also a view that the original obligation is the value, but the relied-upon position is that returning the ʿayn is primary. The place of return is the location of usurpation because values differ by location.

Key Terms
رد العين (radd al-ʿayn): Returning the specific physical item that was usurped.
الموجب الأصلي (al-mūjib al-aṣlī): The original legal obligation, which is to return the item itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary obligation of a usurper as long as the usurped item still exists?
A) Pay its value
B) Return the item itself
C) Give a similar item
D) Compensate the owner for loss of use

Key Takeaway
The usurper must return the exact item he took; this is the original and primary obligation, and paying value is only a fallback when the item is gone.
