Ghasb: Procedure for Destruction Claim and the Movable/Immovable Distinction
Procedural rule for destruction claim and condition of movability in ghasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the usurper's claim of destruction and explain the dispute over ghasb of immovables, including the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Ghasb only applies to movables; immovable usurpation is not ghasb and carries no liability unless for waqf.

This lesson examines two key aspects of ghasb: first, the procedural rule when a usurper claims the item was destroyed, and second, the definitional dispute over whether ghasb can occur in immovable property. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying correct liability rules in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural rule when usurper claims destruction
When the usurper claims the item was destroyed, the judge imprisons him until it is known that if the item remained he would have produced it. Then judgment for compensation is passed. This is a coercive measure to verify the claim, as the default presumption is that the item is still in his possession.
Imprisonment has no fixed limit; it is left to the judge's discretion, similar to imprisonment for debt.
2
Understand the condition for ghasb: movability
Ghasb legally means establishing an invalid hand by removing the owner's hand through an act upon the property itself. This requires physical transfer (naql), which is only possible for movable property (manqul). Immovables cannot be transferred, so ghasb does not apply to them according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The act of removing the owner from immovable is an act upon the person, not the property, so it is not ghasb.
3
Analyze the dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Shafi'i
Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i argue that the definition of ghasb—establishing an invalid hand—applies to all property, because the usurper's possession necessarily removes the owner's hand. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond that in immovables, removal of the owner's hand is achieved by evicting him, which is an act upon him, not the property, making it akin to chasing the owner away from livestock (which is not ghasb).
The dispute hinges on whether the removal of the owner's hand must be via an act directly on the property.
4
See the practical consequence and the fatwa for waqf
According to the majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if the usurped immovable perishes, the usurper is not liable for its value. However, later fatwa (especially for waqf properties) follows Imam Muhammad's view, imposing liability to protect endowment funds. Thus, the thamarah al-khilaf is significant: liability or no liability for destruction of real estate.
For non-waqf immovables, the original view is upheld; for waqf, the stricter view is applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ادعى الهلاك حُبسَ حتَّى يُعْلَمَ أنه لو بقي لأظهر، ثم قضى عليه بالبدل. وشرطه : كون المغصوب نقلياً، فلو غصب عقاراً وهلك في يده لم يضمن. قوله : فإن ادعى الهلاك...الخ ؛ أي ادعى الغاصب هلاك المغصوب، حبسه الحاكم حتى يعلم الحاكم أنّ المغصوب لو بقي في يد الغاصب لأظهره، ثمّ بعد الحبس قضى الحاكم بالبدل على الغاصب، وفي «التبيين» : ليس لحبسه حد مقدر، بل هو مفوض إلى رأي القاضي، كحبس الغريم في الدين وفي «العناية» : هذا الحبس إذا لم يرض المالك بالقضاء بالقيمة. قوله : ثم قضى عليه بالبدل ؛ بما اتفقا عليه من القيمة، أو أقام المالك بينة على ما يدعيه من القيمة. كذا في «العناية». وإنما يقضي عليه بالبدل ؛ لأنَّ الواجب عليه ردُّ العين والهلاك بعارض، فهو يدعي أمراً عارضاً خلاف الظاهر فلا يقبل قوله، كما إذا أفلس، والحال عليه ثمن متاع، فيحبس إلى أن يعلم ما يدعيه ، فإذا عَلِمَ الهلاكُ سقط عنه رده ، فيلزمه ردُّ بدله ، وهو القيمة. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : وشرطه ؛ أي شرط تحقق الغصب الموجب للضمان. قوله : نقليا ؛ أي تحقق الغصب فيما ينقل ويحوّل ؛ لأنَّ الغصب بحقيقته يتحقق فيه دون غيره ؛ لأنَّ إزالة اليد بالنقل والنقل واحد ، وقيل : التحويل هو النقل من مكان والإثبات في مكان آخر، كما في حوالة الباذنجان، والثقل يستعمل بدون الإثبات في مكان آخر، والعقار هو كل ما له أصل، كالدار والضيعة. كذا في «العناية». إزالة اليد في العقار يكون بما يمكن فيه لا بالنقل وهما يقولان : إِنَّ الغصب إثبات اليد بإزالة يد المالك بفعل في العين، وهو لا يتصوَّرُ في العقار ؛ لأنَّ يدَ المالكِ لا تزول إلا بإخراجه عنها، وهو فعل فيه لا في العقار، فصار كما إذ أُبعد المالك عن المواشي. قوله : وهلك في يده ؛ بأن غلب السيلُ على الأرض ، فبقيت تحت الماء أو غصب داراً فهدمت بآفة سماوية أو جاءَ السيلُ فذهب بالبناء. كذا في «الكفاية». If he claims destruction, he is imprisoned until it is known that if it remained he would have produced it, then judgment is passed against him for the substitute. Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya' is a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah dealing with the law of ghasb (usurpation). The text first presents a procedural ruling: when a usurper claims the usurped item has been destroyed, the judge imprisons him until it becomes clear that if the item still existed he would have produced it. Only then does the judge pass judgment for compensation (badal). This imprisonment has no fixed time limit; it is left to the judge's discretion, similar to imprisoning a debtor. The compensation is the value agreed upon or proven by the owner's evidence. The rationale is that the usurper's duty is to return the item itself; destruction is an incidental claim contrary to the apparent presumption (that property remains in his possession), so his claim is not accepted without verification. Once destruction is established, the obligation to return the item falls, and he must pay its value. The condition for this entire procedure and for the very definition of ghasb is that the usurped item be movable (manqul). Ghasb linguistically means taking something by force, but legally it is defined as establishing an invalid hand (yad mubtila) by removing the owner's hand through an act upon the property. In movables, this removal is accomplished by physical transport (naql). Thus ghasb is only conceivable in movable property. The text then presents the key intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute: if someone usurps real estate (immovable property like land or a house) and it perishes in his hand, is he liable? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, he is not liable at all because ghasb does not apply to immovables; the owner's hand is removed by evicting him, not by an act upon the property itself. This is analogous to merely chasing the owner away from his livestock—that is not ghasb. However, Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that ghasb does apply to immovables because the definition of ghasb—establishing an invalid hand—is satisfied; the usurper's possession necessarily removes the owner's hand, regardless of movability. The response of the two imams is that removing the owner from immovable is an act upon the person, not the property, so it does not constitute ghasb. Later fatwa, especially regarding waqf property, often follows Muhammad's view, making the usurper liable for damages and rent. Thus, while the school's original position (from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) absolves the usurper of liability for immovables, practical fatwa often adopts Muhammad's stricter view to protect endowments and the interests of orphans. The passage concludes by emphasizing that for non-liability purposes, the two imams do not recognize ghasb on immovables, but for other rulings (like the obligation to return the property or pay for use), ghasb is considered realized.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Legally: establishing an invalid hand (yad mubtila) on another's property by removing the owner's hand through an act upon the property.
المنقول (al-manqul): Movable property that can be transported from one place to another.
العقار (al-'aqar): Immovable property with a fixed foundation, such as land and buildings (e.g., house, estate).
الضمان (al-daman): Legal liability; specifically, the obligation to compensate for loss or damage.
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment by the judge as a coercive measure to ascertain the truth of the usurper's claim of destruction.
البدل (al-badal): The substitute or compensation due when the usurped item is destroyed; it is the value (qayyimah) at the time of usurpation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling if someone usurps a house (immovable property) and it is destroyed by a storm while in his possession?
A) He is liable for the value of the house.
B) He is not liable because ghasb does not apply to immovables.
C) He must pay rent for the period of use but not the value.
D) He is only liable if the house is waqf.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes movables from immovables in ghasb: only movables incur liability for destruction; immovables require a different legal basis for compensation.

Ghasp and Real Estate: The Intramadhhab Dispute
Dispute on applicability of ghasp to real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that ghasp does not apply to real estate, state the opposing views, and apply the ruling to determine liability for perishing and compensation for use.

Core Idea
Ghasp requires a physical act on the property; real estate cannot be seized, only the owner can be ejected.

This passage addresses a fundamental dispute in the definition of ghasp (usurpation). According to the majority of the Hanafi school, ghasp does not apply to land or buildings because the condition of a direct physical act on the property itself cannot be met. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree, leading to practical differences in liability when the property is destroyed or used. Understanding this dispute clarifies when a usurper must pay compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and the disputants
What is the ruling for when usurped property perishes? Who holds which view? Identify the two main positions: (1) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no liability for perishing; (2) Muhammad and al-Shafi'i: liability applies.
The base ruling is given in the phrase 'wa halaka fi yadihi lam yadman'.
2
Analyze the Hanafi definition of ghasp
What is the required condition for ghasp according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? It requires an act on the property itself (fi'l fil 'ayn). Why is this not met for real estate? Because removing the owner's possession is done by ejecting him, which is an act on the person, not the property.
This is the key to the Hanafi position.
3
Examine the opposing proofs
How does al-Shafi'i define ghasp? As establishing a wrongful hand (ithbat al-yad al-mubtila), which applies to real estate. How does Muhammad argue? That removal of possession in real estate can be achieved by other means, not necessarily transport.
These are the alternative definitions.
4
Trace the Hanafi response
How do Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rebut the opposing arguments? They maintain the requirement of an act on the property. The analogy to livestock (al-mawashi) illustrates that removing the owner from livestock does not constitute ghasp of the livestock itself.
The response is rooted in the literal condition.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
What is the practical difference? For perishing: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no liability; Muhammad and al-Shafi'i say liability. For compensation for use (ujrat al-mithl): even Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf require payment. For waqf property, the fatwa follows Muhammad's view imposing full liability.
The exception for waqf shows how later fatwa can override the school's position.

Source Text

Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the definition of ghasp (usurpation) and its applicability to real estate. The base ruling is that if usurped property perishes in the usurper's hands, he is not liable (for real estate according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). The commentary explains that this is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, while Muhammad (al-Shaybani) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that ghasp does apply to real estate. The reasoning for the Hanafi view is that ghasp requires a physical act impressing the property itself (fīl fī al-ayn), which is impossible for immovable property; the owner's possession is only removed by ejecting him, an act on the person, not the property. The opposing view states that the definition of establishing a wrongful hand (ithbāt al-yad al-mubṭila) is fulfilled. The text then discusses diverse scenarios: if the property is destroyed by a natural calamity, there is no liability; but if the usurper used the property, he must pay compensation for the benefit taken. Citations from al-Kifayah, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and fatawa indicate that for waqf property, the fatwa is with Muhammad's position imposing liability. The passage ends with an analogy to livestock: removing the owner from livestock does not constitute ghasp of the livestock itself.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasp): Usurpation: taking possession of another's property wrongfully. In this context, defined as establishing possession by removing the owner's possession through a physical act on the property itself.
عقار (ʿiqār): Immovable property, real estate. In this passage, land and buildings that cannot be physically transferred.
إثبات اليد (ithbāt al-yad): Establishing possession or control over property. In ghasp, it is establishing a wrongful hand (yad mubṭila).
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): Compensation equivalent to market rent, required for the period of use of the property even if ghasp is not established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, why does ghasp not apply to real estate?
A) Because real estate cannot be physically moved.
B) Because the owner's possession is only removed by ejecting him, which is an act on the person, not the property.
C) Because the definition of ghasp requires a contract.
D) Because real estate is not considered property in their view.

Key Takeaway
Ghasp of real estate is not recognized by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but the occupant is still liable for compensation for use; for waqf property, the stronger view imposes full liability.

Usurper's Liability for Defects Caused by His Actions
Clarifying liability for diminution in value caused by usurper's use of property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds the usurper liable for diminution caused by his actions and distinguish between liability based on ghasb versus itlaf.

Core Idea
Liability for diminution arises from the act of destruction (itlaf), not merely from wrongful possession.

This passage clarifies a key nuance in Hanafi law: a usurper is liable for any decrease in value caused by his use of the property, even if the property is real estate. The ruling is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams, but their reasoning differs: for Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it follows from the applicability of ghasb to real estate; for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it is because the usurper's action constitutes destruction (itlaf), and real estate is liable for destruction. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the correct legal basis in similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is the usurper liable for? The ruling states he must compensate for any diminution (nuqsan) in the property's value caused by his actions, such as living in a house or cultivating land, or renting out a slave who then becomes ill.
The ruling covers both real estate and movables.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text cites an analogy (qiyas) to destruction (itlaf). The phrase 'because it is destruction and real estate is liable for destruction' shows that any damage-causing act triggers liability, even if the property itself is not subject to ghasb.
The dalil is aqli (rational) rather than naqli (textual).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the analogy work? The effective cause (illa) is the act of causing damage. Just as destroying property outright requires compensation, so does any act that diminishes its value, regardless of whether the actor is a usurper.
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf use this reasoning.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Muhammad and al-Shafi'i hold a different basis: they consider occupation of real estate as ghasp itself, so liability flows from that. Despite the difference in reasoning, all agree on the outcome (liability).
This is an intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi (operative position) is that the usurper is liable for diminution caused by his actions, based on the itlaf reasoning. Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah supports the view of Abu Hanifa.
Authority is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

وضمن ما نقص بفعله كسكناه وزرعه أو بإجارة عبد غصب أي ضمن العقار وغيره أما في العقار كالسكنى والزرع وفي غير العقار كما إذا غصب عبداً فأجره فعمل فعرض له مرض أو نحافة ضمن النقصان حبس المالك حتى تلفت مواشيه لا يضمن كذا في «المبسوط» وليس هذا كالمنقول لأنَّ النقل فعل فيه وهو الغصب قوله وضمن في قولهم جميعاً أما على قول محمد والشافعي ظاهر وأما على قول الأعظم والثاني فلأنه إتلاف والعقار يضمن به كذا في «العناية» قوله بفعله يعرف النقصان بأن ينظر بكم كان يستأجر هذه الأرض قبل استعمالها وبكم يستأجر بعده وتفاوت ما بينهما نقصانها ومن صورة النقصان بفعله أن يفعل في الدار الحدادة والقصارة فضعف جدارها بذلك فانهدمت وإنما قيد بفعله لأنها إذا انهدمت بعدما غصبها وسكن فيها لا بسكناه وعمله بآفة سماوية فلا ضمان عليه عند الأعظم كذا في حاشية الجلبي قوله ضمن العقار لأنه إتلاف والعقار يضمن به كما إذا نقل ترابه لأنه فعل في العين ويدخل فيما قاله إذا انهدمت الدار بسكناه وعمله فلو غصب داراً أو باعها أو سلمها وأقر ذلك والمشتري ينكر غصب البائع ولا بينة لصاحب الدار فهو على الاختلاف في الغصب هو الصحيح كذا في «الهداية» قوله نحافة وهي بالنون والحاء المهملة الهزال ومنه نحيف قوله ضمن النقصان لأنه دخل جميع أجزائه في ضمانه بالغصب كذا في «العناية»
And he is liable for what diminished due to his action, such as living in it or cultivating it, or by renting out a usurped slave. Meaning: he is liable for the real estate and other things. As for real estate, such as living and cultivation. For non-real estate, as when he usurps a slave then rents him out so he works, then he suffers illness or emaciation, he is liable for the diminution. If he imprisons the owner until his livestock perish, he is not liable. Thus in al-Mabsut. And this is not like movable property because moving is an act on it and that is ghasp. His statement: 'And he is liable' is agreed upon by all. As for the statement of Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is obvious. As for the statement of the Greatest (Abu Hanifa) and the Second (Abu Yusuf), because it is destruction (itlaf) and real estate is liable for destruction. Thus in al-Inayah. His statement: 'by his action' the diminution is known by considering how much this land was rented for before its use and how much after, and the difference is its diminution. An example of diminution by his action: he performs blacksmithing or laundering in the house, thus its walls become weak and collapse. He specified 'by his action' because if it collapses after he seized it and lived in it, not due to his living or work but due to a natural calamity, there is no liability according to the Greatest (Abu Hanifa). Thus in the gloss of al-Jalabi. His statement: 'he is liable for the real estate' because it is destruction and real estate is liable for it, as when he removes its soil, because it is an act on the property. Included in his statement is if the house collapses due to his living and work. If he seizes a house and sells it or hands it over and admits that, and the buyer denies the seller's ghasab, and the owner of the house has no evidence, then it is upon the difference in ghasp is correct. Thus in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'emaciation' (nahaafa) is with nun and haa' (meaning) thinness, from nahif. His statement: 'he is liable for the diminution' because all its parts entered his liability by ghasp. Thus in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the liability of a usurper for diminution in value caused by his actions. The base ruling is that the usurper is liable for any diminution resulting from his use of the property, whether real estate (like living or cultivating) or movable property (like renting out a slave who then becomes ill). This liability is affirmed by all scholars, but their reasons differ: for Muhammad and al-Shafi'i it follows from their view that ghasp applies to real estate; for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, though they do not consider occupation of real estate as ghasp, they hold the usurper liable because his actions constitute itlaf (destruction), and real estate is liable for destruction. The method of assessing diminution is by comparing rental value before and after the use. An example: if the usurper uses the house for blacksmithing or laundering, weakening the walls until they collapse, he is liable. However, if the collapse occurs due to a natural calamity unrelated to his actions, there is no liability according to Abu Hanifa. The passage also clarifies that if the usurper merely imprisons the owner (causing his livestock to perish), that is not considered ghasp of the livestock. The definition of 'nahaafa' (emaciation) is provided, and it is reiterated that liability for diminution arises because all parts of the property come under his guarantee (daman) by virtue of the act of usurpation.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage. Here, the usurper is liable for the diminution in value caused by his actions.
نقصان (nuqsān): Diminution, decrease in value. The difference between the rental value before and after the usurper's use.
إتلاف (itlāf): Destruction or causing damage. In this context, any act that results in physical harm to the property, even if not outright demolition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a usurper lives in a house and it collapses due to a natural calamity unrelated to his actions, is he liable?
A) Yes, because he is in wrongful possession.
B) Yes, because he should have prevented it.
C) No, because the collapse is an act of God and not caused by his actions.
D) No, because real estate is not subject to ghasp.

Key Takeaway
A usurper is liable for any diminution in value caused by his actions, even if the property is real estate, because the act is considered destruction (itlaf).

Liability for Deficiency Caused by Usurper's Action
Liability for use-caused deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for deficiency caused by a usurper's action and explain how the deficiency is measured and the underlying legal reasoning.

Core Idea
The usurper guarantees any decrease in value caused by his use, measured by comparing rental value before and after.

This passage expands on a key ruling from al-Wiqayah: the ghasib (usurper) must compensate for any deficiency (nuqsan) that results from his use of the usurped property. The commentary clarifies the scope of liability, the method of measuring the deficiency, and the reasoning behind the ruling, which is agreed upon by all Hanafi scholars despite slight differences in juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is the usurper's use of the property causing a decrease in value. The ruling is that the usurper must compensate for that deficiency (wajib).
The liability applies to both real and movable property.
2
Understand the Measurement of Deficiency
The deficiency is measured by comparing the rental value of the property before the usurper's use with its rental value after. The difference is the amount of compensation.
Example: land rented for 100 before, 70 after use → deficiency = 30.
3
Analyze the Legal Reasoning
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf base liability on the principle of itlaf (destruction): the use that diminishes value is akin to destroying part of the property. Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i consider the liability apparent due to the clear causal link.
All agree on the ruling despite different usul reasoning.
4
Apply to Examples from the Text
If the usurper does blacksmithing in a house and its wall collapses due to that work, he is liable. If the wall collapses due to a natural calamity (heavenly affliction) unrelated to his use, he is not liable.
Causation is key: only deficiency directly caused by his action is guaranteed.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Agreement
All major Hanafi authorities (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on the legal outcome. The difference is only in the juristic reasoning, not the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
Zahir al-Riwayah texts (al-Mabsut, al-Hidayah) confirm this ruling.

Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. And he guarantees the deficiency caused by his action, such as his residence and cultivation, or by leasing a usurped slave. [1]- (And he guarantees the deficiency caused by his action, such as his residence and cultivation, or by leasing a usurped slave): meaning he guarantees the real property and other things. As for real property, such as residence and cultivation; and as for non-real property, like when he usurps a slave and leases him so he works, and then suffers illness or emaciation, he guarantees the deficiency. If the owner imprisons until his livestock die, he does not guarantee, as in al-Mabsut. And this is not like movable property, because moving is an act in it, which is usurpation. [1] His statement: 'and he guarantees' – in all of their statements. As for the statement of Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with him) and al-Shafi'i, it is apparent. And as for the statement of the Greatest (Abu Hanifa) and the Second (Abu Yusuf), it is because it is destruction and real property is guaranteed by it. So in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'by his action' – the deficiency is known by looking at how much this land would rent for before use and how much it would rent for after, and the difference between them is its deficiency. An example of deficiency by his action: he does blacksmithing and fulling in the house, so its wall weakens due to that and collapses. And he limited it to 'by his action' because if it collapses after he usurps and lives in it, not due to his residence and work, by a heavenly affliction, then there is no liability according to the Greatest (Abu Hanifa). So in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His statement: 'guarantees real property' – because it is destruction and real property is guaranteed by it, like if he moves its soil, because it is an act on the entity. And this includes if the house collapses due to his residence and work. If he usurps a house and sells it or hands it over and admits that, and the buyer denies the usurpation of the seller, and the owner has no proof, then it is based on the difference of opinion in usurpation – that is correct. So in al-Hidayah. [4] His statement: 'emaciation' (nuhafah) – it is with nun and unpointed ha': thinness. From it: nahif (lean). [5] His statement: 'guarantees the deficiency' – because all its parts entered into his guarantee by usurpation. So in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage from Umda al-Ri'aya expands on a line from the matn of al-Wiqaya regarding the liability (daman) of the usurper (ghasib) for any decrease in value caused by his action. The matn states: 'He guarantees the deficiency caused by his action, such as his residence and cultivation, or by leasing a usurped slave.' The commentary explains that this includes both real property (like land and houses) and movable property (like slaves). When the usurper uses the property and it suffers a decrease (e.g., the slave becomes ill or emaciated), he must compensate for that decrease. However, if the owner imprisons the usurper until the latter's livestock die, the usurper is not liable for that, as it is not part of the wrongful act. The commentary then discusses the reasoning: all scholars (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i) agree on the ruling. For Muhammad and al-Shafi'i the liability is clear, while for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is based on the principle that the usurper's action is a form of destruction (itlaf), and real property is guaranteed by destruction. The deficiency is measured by comparing the rental value before and after the act; the difference is the amount to be compensated. An example is given: if the usurper does heavy work in a house (like blacksmithing) causing the wall to weaken and collapse, he is liable. But if the collapse is due to a natural calamity unrelated to his action, he is not liable. The commentary also addresses a scenario where the usurper sells the property and the buyer denies knowledge of the usurpation; the ruling follows the standard rules of usurpation.

Key Terms
ضمن (daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or deficiency.
نقصان (nuqsan): A decrease in value or quality, measured here by change in rental value.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; wrongful taking and possession of another's property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the deficiency (nuqsan) measured in real property cases?
A) By the original purchase price
B) By comparing the rental value before and after the use
C) By the cost of repairing the damage
D) By an expert's arbitrary assessment

Key Takeaway
The ghasib must compensate for any decrease in value caused by his use, measured by the change in rental value.

Charity with Earnings from Usurped Property: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on charity with gains from wrongful possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on giving charity with profits from usurped property and explain the disagreement with Abu Yusuf's view.

Core Idea
Profits from usurped property must be given in charity — unless the property is not specifically traceable in some cases.

When a person wrongfully uses another's property and earns a wage or profit, can he keep it? The Hanafi school has a clear position, but there is a significant internal disagreement. This lesson unpacks the ruling from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, explaining when charity is obligatory and why Abu Yusuf disagreed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is the usurper earning a wage or profit from the usurped item. State the hukm: wajib to give charity with such earnings according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
Understand the Condition of 'Indication' (Ishara)
The profit is subject to charity only if the property is 'determined by indication' (mut'ayyan bi'l-ishara). Identify the four scenarios related to specific dirhams and indication.
The text lists three cases where charity is not given according to al-Karkhi.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's Opposition
Abu Yusuf holds that the usurper does not give charity with any profit because the profit arose under his guarantee (daman) and thus becomes his property.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf based on the legal principle of ownership through guarantee.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, with the detailed exceptions from al-Karkhi. Some later scholars prefer the absolute prohibition (must give charity in all cases).
See al-Multaqa and Fatawa al-Nawazil.

Book of Usurpation / Its definition and rulings. 145. And he gives charity with his wage, the wage of his borrowed item, and profit obtained by transacting in his deposit or his usurped item, determined by indication, or by purchasing with the deposited dirhams or the usurped dirhams and spending them. If he indicated them and spent others, or indicated others and spent them, or did not indicate and spent them – no [charity] – and this is the fatwa. [1] (And he gives charity with his wage, the wage of his borrowed item, and profit obtained by transacting in his deposit or his usurped item, determined by indication, or by purchasing with the deposited dirhams or the usurped dirhams and spending them. If he indicated them and spent others, or indicated others and spent them, or did not indicate and spent them – no [charity] – and this is the fatwa): meaning he gives charity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (peace be upon them), contrary to Abu Yusuf (may Allah be pleased with him), with the wage of a usurped slave. [1] His statement: 'and he gives charity with his wage' – meaning the usurper gives charity with the produce of the slave if he used it and the use decreased it, or the wage of the borrowed item if it decreased, and gives charity with what remains of the produce and wage. [2] His statement: 'and profit obtained by transacting in his deposit or his usurped item... etc.' – meaning if he transacts in the usurped item and the deposit by selling it and making profit, if it is determined by indication or by purchasing with the deposited dirhams or the usurped dirhams and spending them, he gives charity with the profit obtained in them if it is something determined by indication. But if it is not so determined, then there are four aspects: (1) if he indicated them and spent those same dirhams – similarly he gives charity. (2) if he indicated them and spent other dirhams. (3) if he indicated other dirhams and spent these. (4) if he did not indicate and spent them – he does not give charity in these last three cases according to al-Karkhi. It is said: this is the fatwa, and the chosen view is that it is absolutely not permissible (for him). So in al-Multaqa. Even after guarantee, it is correct. As in Fatawa al-Nawazil. Some scholars have chosen the fatwa according to al-Karkhi's view in our time due to the abundance of haram. And all this according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), but according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah be pleased with him), he does not give charity with any of it, just as if the type were different. This is mentioned by al-Zayla'i, so it should be memorized. [3] His statement: 'contrary to Abu Yusuf' – because it occurred in his guarantee and ownership. As for guarantee, it is apparent, and similarly ownership in the guaranteed items, because guaranteed items become owned by paying the guarantee, traced back to the time of usurpation.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from Umda al-Ri'aya discusses a key ruling regarding the disposition of profits derived from a usurped item (maghs/ghasb) or a deposit (wadi'a). The matn states: 'He gives charity with his wage, the wage of his borrowed item, and profit obtained by transacting in his deposit or his usurped item, determined by indication, or by purchasing with the deposited dirhams or the usurped dirhams and spending them. If he indicated them and spent others, or indicated others and spent them, or did not indicate and spent them – no charity – and this is the fatwa.' The commentary explains that this ruling is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. According to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the usurper who uses the usurped property and earns a wage or profit must give that earning in charity (sadaqah) because it is not lawful for him to keep. This applies if the item is 'determined by indication' (mut'ayyan bi'l-ishara) – meaning there is clear specification, e.g., a particular slave – or if he uses specific deposited/usurped dirhams to purchase something and then resells it. The commentary details four scenarios: (1) if he indicated the specific dirhams and spent those very dirhams – he gives charity; (2) if he indicated those dirhams but spent different ones; (3) if he indicated different dirhams but spent the specific ones; (4) if he did not indicate at all and spent the specific ones – in the last three cases, according to al-Karkhi, he does not give charity, and this is said to be the fatwa. However, the chosen view is that it is absolutely not permissible for him to keep the profit. Some scholars have preferred al-Karkhi's ruling in our times due to the prevalence of haram. Importantly, Abu Yusuf disagrees entirely, holding that the usurper does not give charity with any of such profits, because the profit occurred under his guarantee (daman) and thus becomes his ownership – guaranteed items become owned by the obligation to pay the security, retroactive to the time of usurpation.

Key Terms
أجر (ajr): The wage earned from a usurped slave or borrowed item.
وديعة (wadi'a): A deposit of property for safekeeping; the depositary is not allowed to use it.
إشارة (ishara): Indication or specification of a particular item or currency when transacting.
ربح (ribh): Profit from a commercial transaction involving usurped or deposited property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position (al-Karkhi's fatwa), in which of the following scenarios does the usurper NOT have to give charity with the profit from using deposited dirhams?
A) He indicated the specific dirhams and spent those very dirhams.
B) He indicated the specific dirhams but spent other dirhams.
C) He indicated other dirhams and spent those indicated dirhams.
D) He did not indicate any dirhams and spent the deposited dirhams.

Key Takeaway
The usurper must give charity with profits from usurped property when the property is specifically traceable; otherwise, per al-Karkhi's fatwa, charity is not required in three specific scenarios.

Charity on Profits from Usurped Property: The Intra-Hanafi Core Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on charity for profits from usurpation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf on whether a usurper must give charity on profits, and explain the proof for each.

Core Idea
Profit from usurpation must be given in charity according to Abu Hanifa; Abu Yusuf says it's lawful due to guarantee.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the purity of profits from usurped property. The core question: does the usurper own the profits because of the guarantee, or are they impure and require charity? We will examine the proofs of both sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The act is using a usurped property for profit (e.g., renting or selling). The ruling in question: must the usurper give charity (tasadduq) on that profit?
2
State the majority Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: charity is required because the profit arises from an impure act.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf: no charity is required because the profit occurs under the usurper's guarantee, making it lawful.
4
Analyse the opposing proof
Abu Yusuf's proof: the usurper owns the guaranteed item retroactively, so the profit is his.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The response: guarantee only covers the deficiency, not the whole property; the profit is still tainted.
See al-Hidayah for the full argument.

Source Text

Book of Usurpation / Its Definition and Rulings

And he gives charity for its hire, and the hire of what he borrowed, and profit obtained by disposing in the deposit, or the usurpation, specified by pointing, or by purchasing with the dirhams of the deposit or the usurpation, and he paid them. If he pointed to them and paid others, or to others and he was absolute, and paid them, no, and by it fatwa is given.
[1]
(And he gives charity for its hire, and the hire of what he borrowed, and profit obtained by disposing in the deposit, or the usurpation, specified by pointing, or by purchasing with the dirhams of the deposit or the usurpation, and he paid them. If he pointed to them and paid others, or to others, and he was absolute, and paid them, no, and by it fatwa is given): i.e., he gives charity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, for the hire of a usurped slave... [His statement: And he gives charity for its hire; i.e., the usurper gives charity for the produce of the slave if he utilized him and the utilization caused a deficiency, or the hire of the borrowed item and a deficiency, and he gives charity for what remains of the produce and hire. [2] His statement: And profit; obtained by disposing in his deposit or usurpation... etc.; i.e., if he disposed in the usurped item and the deposit by selling it and making a profit, if that was specified by pointing or by purchasing with the dirhams of the deposit or the dirhams of the usurpation and paid them, he gives charity for the profit obtained in both if it is of the type that is specified by pointing. And if it is not specified, then there are four aspects:
1. If he pointed to them and paid them, then likewise he gives charity.
2. And if he pointed to them and paid others.
3. Or pointed to others and paid them.
4. Or he was absolute and did not point and paid them. He does not give charity in these last three forms according to al-Karkhi; it is said: by it fatwa is given, and the chosen view is that it is absolutely not permissible. Like this in al-Multaqa, even after guarantee, it is correct. As in Fatawa al-Nawazil.
And some chose fatwa according to al-Karkhi's statement in our time due to the abundance of prohibited things, and all this according to them [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad], and according to Abu Yusuf he does not give charity for any of it, just as if the type differed; al-Zaylai mentioned it, so preserve it. [3] His statement: contrary to Abu Yusuf; because it occurred under his guarantee and his ownership. As for guarantee, it is apparent, and likewise ownership in the guaranteed items, because guaranteed items are owned by paying the guarantee retroactively to the time of usurpation.
(1) al-Multaqa (p. 175).
[PAGE_146]

146
If he usurped and changed so its name ceased...
Husn al-Diraya li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqaya
He took hire and took the wage, likewise for the hire of a borrowed slave that he rented out and took its hire, and likewise he gives charity for profit obtained by disposing in the deposit or the usurpated if it is of the type that is specified by pointing, and likewise he gives charity for profit obtained by purchasing with a deposit or a usurpation that is not specified by pointing if he pointed to them and paid them. So his statement: 'or by purchasing' is conjuncted to 'disposing'. As for if he pointed to them and paid others, or pointed to others and paid them, or was absolute and paid them: i.e., he did not point to anything but said: I bought with a thousand dirhams and paid from the dirhams of usurpation and deposit; then in all these forms the profit is permissible for him and he is not required to give charity.
If he usurped and changed so its name ceased...
(1)
The author of Nata'ij al-Afkar said: I say: In it there is some reflection; because that which occurred under his guarantee and ownership is only the part that is lost from the usurped item, not the entirety of the usurped item; because the discussion is when the produce caused a deficiency, so he is obliged to guarantee the deficiency along with returning the original, and it appears that the produce is in exchange for the benefits of the entirety of the usurped slave, not in exchange for the benefit of the lost attribute only. So what is the basis for saying that he does not give charity?
And for them [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad]: It occurred due to something impure, which is disposing in the property of another, and whatever has this state, its way is charity, because the branch takes the attribute of the root, and the retroactive ownership is deficient, so the impurity is not removed by it. Like this in al-Hidayah (2).
[1] His statement: 'and changed'; an indication that the usurped item changed by the act of the usurper. As for if it changed not by his act, like if the grapes become raisins by themselves or vinegar, or wet dates become dry dates, then the owner has a choice: if he wishes he takes it, and if he wishes he leaves it and claims its guarantee. Like this in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'so its name ceased'; a precaution for when he usurped a sheep and slaughtered it; because the greatest purpose is lost, which is milk and offspring, but the name of sheep has not ceased from it, because it is said: slaughtered sheep. Like this in al-Kifayah (4).
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a usurper (ghasib) must give charity (tasadduq) on profits and rents obtained from the usurped property. The base text states: 'He gives charity for its hire, and the hire of what he borrowed, and profit obtained by disposing in the deposit or usurpation...' The commentator explains that this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (rahimahumallah), contrary to Abu Yusuf (rahimahullah).

The ruling applies when the usurper uses the property (e.g., a slave) and either the usage causes a deficiency (in which case he must compensate for the deficiency and give charity for the remaining profit) or when he disposes of the property (e.g., sells it) and makes a profit. The key distinction is whether the usurped property is 'specified by pointing' (mut'ayyan bil-isharah) — i.e., specific coins or goods that are identifiable — or not. If it is specified, charity is required. If not specified (e.g., generic dirhams), then there are four scenarios:
1. He pointed to specific goods and paid them: charity required.
2. He pointed to specific goods but paid others: no charity (according to al-Karkhi).
3. He pointed to others and paid them: no charity.
4. He was absolute (did not point) and paid them: no charity.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that in these three latter cases, no charity is required, but it is also said that the profit is absolutely not permissible (la yahillu mutlaqan). Some later scholars have chosen to follow al-Karkhi's view due to the abundance of haram in our times, but the original position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is charity in all cases where the profit results from the unlawful use.

Abu Yusuf's position is that no charity is required at all because the profit occurred under the usurper's guarantee (daman) and ownership: since the usurped item is guaranteed, its benefits are owned by the usurper upon paying the guarantee retroactively from the time of usurpation. Thus, the profit is lawful for him (tayyib). The commentator in Nata'ij al-Afkar raises an objection to Abu Yusuf's reasoning: the guarantee is only for the deficiency, not for the entire property, so how can the profit be entirely his? The response from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (as in al-Hidayah) is that the profit arises from an impure source — the unauthorized disposal of another's property — and thus must be purified by charity, since the branch takes the attribute of the root (al-far' yahsumu 'ala wasf al-asl), and the retroactive ownership is deficient.

The passage also discusses cases where the usurped property changes in such a way that its name ceases (e.g., grape juice becomes vinegar not by the usurper's action — then the owner has a choice), and when a sheep is slaughtered (its name remains 'slaughtered sheep', so it is not considered a complete change). These nuances further refine the general ruling.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right, with the intention of ownership or use, or simply taking it forcibly.
تصدق (tasadduqa): To give charity; in this context, the usurper must voluntarily give away the profits/rents obtained from the usurped property to purify his wealth.
متعين بالإشارة (mut'ayyan bil-isharah): Specified by pointing: an asset that can be individually identified (e.g., a specific coin or item).
ضمان (daman): Guarantee/liability: the usurper's obligation to compensate the owner for the usurped property or its deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school regarding profits from using a usurped specified asset?
A) The usurper may keep the profits as lawful because of the guarantee.
B) The usurper must give the profits in charity.
C) The usurper must return the profits to the original owner.
D) The usurper must destroy the profits.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the majority position is that profits from usurped property must be purified by charity, due to the impure origin of the gain.

Specification Matters: Al-Karkhi's Four Aspects on Charity for Non-Specified Assets
Ruling on charity for non-specified usurped assets with al-Karkhi's qualification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Karkhi's four scenarios to determine whether a usurper must give charity on profits from non-specified usurped assets.

Core Idea
For non-specified assets, charity is only required when the usurper points to and pays the exact same items.

The general ruling that charity is required on profits from usurped property is qualified by the nature of the asset. If the asset is not 'specified by pointing' (mut'ayyan bil-isharah), al-Karkhi introduces four scenarios that determine the obligation. This lesson unpacks those scenarios and the contemporary fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'mut'ayyan bil-isharah'
An asset that is specified by pointing is one that can be individually identified, such as a specific coin in a stack. Non-specified assets are generic like dirhams in a wallet.
This distinction is crucial for the application of al-Karkhi's four aspects.
2
Learn the four scenarios
Al-Karkhi outlines four possibilities: (1) He pointed to the specific goods and paid those exact goods: charity required. (2) He pointed to specific goods but paid other goods: no charity. (3) He pointed to other goods and paid them: no charity. (4) He was absolute (did not point) and paid from the goods: no charity.
3
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that in scenarios 2-4, charity is not required. The profit is permissible.
Some early texts say the profit is absolutely not permissible, but the fatwa practice has shifted.
4
Note the contemporary fatwa
Due to the abundance of haram in later times, some scholars choose al-Karkhi's view to ease the burden, allowing the profit to be kept in these three cases.

قال صاحب نتائج الأفكار » ) : أقول : فيه نوع تأمل ؛ لأنَّ الذي حصل في ضمانه وملكه إنّما هو البعض الفائتُ من المغصوب دون مجموع المغصوب ؛ لأن الكلام فيما إذا نقصته الغلة، فوجب عليه ضمان النقصان مع استرداد الأصل، والظاهر أنّ الغلة بمقابلة منافع مجموع العبد المغصوب المستغل لا بمقابلة منفعة وصفه الفائت فقط فما وجه القول بأنه لا يتصدّق.
ولهما: إنه حصل بسبب خبث وهو التصرُّف في ملك الغير، وما هذا حاله فسبيله التصدق ، إذ الفرعُ يحصل على وصف الأصل، والملك المستند ناقص، فلا ينعدم به الخبث. كذا في «الهداية»(۲).
[١]قوله : وغير؛ إشارة إلى أنّ المغصوب تغيَّرَ بفعل الغاصب، أما إذا تغير بغير فعله مثل إن صارَ العنب زيباً بنفسه أو خلاً ، أو الرطب تمراً، فإن المالك فيه بالخيار إن شاء أخذ ، وإن شاءَ تركه وضمنه. كذا في «العناية» [۲] قوله : فزال اسمه ؛ احتراز عمّا إذا غصب شاةً ذبحها ؛ لأنه فات أعظم
المقاصد
(۳)
وهو الدر والنسل، ولكن لم يزل اسم الشاةِ عليها ؛ لأنه يقال : شاة مذبوحة.
كذا في «الكفاية»(4).

And he gives charity for its hire, and the hire of what he borrowed, and profit obtained by disposing in the deposit, or the usurpation, specified by pointing, or by purchasing with the dirhams of the deposit or the usurpation, and he paid them. If he pointed to them and paid others, or to others and he was absolute, and paid them, no, and by it fatwa is given.
[1]
(And he gives charity for its hire, and the hire of what he borrowed, and profit obtained by disposing in the deposit, or the usurpation, specified by pointing, or by purchasing with the dirhams of the deposit or the usurpation, and he paid them. If he pointed to them and paid others, or to others, and he was absolute, and paid them, no, and by it fatwa is given): i.e., he gives charity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, for the hire of a usurped slave... [His statement: And he gives charity for its hire; i.e., the usurper gives charity for the produce of the slave if he utilized him and the utilization caused a deficiency, or the hire of the borrowed item and a deficiency, and he gives charity for what remains of the produce and hire. [2] His statement: And profit; obtained by disposing in his deposit or usurpation... etc.; i.e., if he disposed in the usurped item and the deposit by selling it and making a profit, if that was specified by pointing or by purchasing with the dirhams of the deposit or the dirhams of the usurpation and paid them, he gives charity for the profit obtained in both if it is of the type that is specified by pointing. And if it is not specified, then there are four aspects:
1. If he pointed to them and paid them, then likewise he gives charity.
2. And if he pointed to them and paid others.
3. Or pointed to others and paid them.
4. Or he was absolute and did not point and paid them. He does not give charity in these last three forms according to al-Karkhi; it is said: by it fatwa is given, and the chosen view is that it is absolutely not permissible. Like this in al-Multaqa, even after guarantee, it is correct. As in Fatawa al-Nawazil.
And some chose fatwa according to al-Karkhi's statement in our time due to the abundance of prohibited things, and all this according to them [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad], and according to Abu Yusuf he does not give charity for any of it, just as if the type differed; al-Zaylai mentioned it, so preserve it. [3] His statement: contrary to Abu Yusuf; because it occurred under his guarantee and his ownership. As for guarantee, it is apparent, and likewise ownership in the guaranteed items, because guaranteed items are owned by paying the guarantee retroactively to the time of usurpation.
(1) al-Multaqa (p. 175).

146
If he usurped and changed so its name ceased...
Husn al-Diraya li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqaya
He took hire and took the wage, likewise for the hire of a borrowed slave that he rented out and took its hire, and likewise he gives charity for profit obtained by disposing in the deposit or the usurpated if it is of the type that is specified by pointing, and likewise he gives charity for profit obtained by purchasing with a deposit or a usurpation that is not specified by pointing if he pointed to them and paid them. So his statement: 'or by purchasing' is conjuncted to 'disposing'. As for if he pointed to them and paid others, or pointed to others and paid them, or was absolute and paid them: i.e., he did not point to anything but said: I bought with a thousand dirhams and paid from the dirhams of usurpation and deposit; then in all these forms the profit is permissible for him and he is not required to give charity.
If he usurped and changed so its name ceased...
(1)
The author of Nata'ij al-Afkar said: I say: In it there is some reflection; because that which occurred under his guarantee and ownership is only the part that is lost from the usurped item, not the entirety of the usurped item; because the discussion is when the produce caused a deficiency, so he is obliged to guarantee the deficiency along with returning the original, and it appears that the produce is in exchange for the benefits of the entirety of the usurped slave, not in exchange for the benefit of the lost attribute only. So what is the basis for saying that he does not give charity?
And for them [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad]: It occurred due to something impure, which is disposing in the property of another, and whatever has this state, its way is charity, because the branch takes the attribute of the root, and the retroactive ownership is deficient, so the impurity is not removed by it. Like this in al-Hidayah (2).
[1] His statement: 'and changed'; an indication that the usurped item changed by the act of the usurper. As for if it changed not by his act, like if the grapes become raisins by themselves or vinegar, or wet dates become dry dates, then the owner has a choice: if he wishes he takes it, and if he wishes he leaves it and claims its guarantee. Like this in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'so its name ceased'; a precaution for when he usurped a sheep and slaughtered it; because the greatest purpose is lost, which is milk and offspring, but the name of sheep has not ceased from it, because it is said: slaughtered sheep. Like this in al-Kifayah (4).
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a usurper (ghasib) must give charity (tasadduq) on profits and rents obtained from the usurped property. The base text states: 'He gives charity for its hire, and the hire of what he borrowed, and profit obtained by disposing in the deposit or usurpation...' The commentator explains that this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (rahimahumallah), contrary to Abu Yusuf (rahimahullah).

The ruling applies when the usurper uses the property (e.g., a slave) and either the usage causes a deficiency (in which case he must compensate for the deficiency and give charity for the remaining profit) or when he disposes of the property (e.g., sells it) and makes a profit. The key distinction is whether the usurped property is 'specified by pointing' (mut'ayyan bil-isharah) — i.e., specific coins or goods that are identifiable — or not. If it is specified, charity is required. If not specified (e.g., generic dirhams), then there are four scenarios:
1. He pointed to specific goods and paid them: charity required.
2. He pointed to specific goods but paid others: no charity (according to al-Karkhi).
3. He pointed to others and paid them: no charity.
4. He was absolute (did not point) and paid them: no charity.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that in these three latter cases, no charity is required, but it is also said that the profit is absolutely not permissible (la yahillu mutlaqan). Some later scholars have chosen to follow al-Karkhi's view due to the abundance of haram in our times, but the original position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is charity in all cases where the profit results from the unlawful use.

Abu Yusuf's position is that no charity is required at all because the profit occurred under the usurper's guarantee (daman) and ownership: since the usurped item is guaranteed, its benefits are owned by the usurper upon paying the guarantee retroactively from the time of usurpation. Thus, the profit is lawful for him (tayyib). The commentator in Nata'ij al-Afkar raises an objection to Abu Yusuf's reasoning: the guarantee is only for the deficiency, not for the entire property, so how can the profit be entirely his? The response from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (as in al-Hidayah) is that the profit arises from an impure source — the unauthorized disposal of another's property — and thus must be purified by charity, since the branch takes the attribute of the root (al-far' yahsumu 'ala wasf al-asl), and the retroactive ownership is deficient.

The passage also discusses cases where the usurped property changes in such a way that its name ceases (e.g., grape juice becomes vinegar not by the usurper's action — then the owner has a choice), and when a sheep is slaughtered (its name remains 'slaughtered sheep', so it is not considered a complete change). These nuances further refine the general ruling.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right, with the intention of ownership or use, or simply taking it forcibly.
تصدق (tasadduqa): To give charity; in this context, the usurper must voluntarily give away the profits/rents obtained from the usurped property to purify his wealth.
متعين بالإشارة (mut'ayyan bil-isharah): Specified by pointing: an asset that can be individually identified (e.g., a specific coin or item).
ضمان (daman): Guarantee/liability: the usurper's obligation to compensate the owner for the usurped property or its deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Karkhi, when does a usurper need to give charity on profits from a non-specified usurped asset?
A) Only if he points to the specific items and pays those exact items.
B) Only if he does not point at all.
C) In all cases of profit from usurpation.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
For non-specified usurped assets, charity is only required when the usurper points to and pays the exact same items; otherwise, the profit is permissible.

Charity on Profit from Usurped Property: The Change and Payment Distinction
Ruling on profit from usurped property with change and payment distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling: when profit from usurped property requires charity, and distinguish the exception when the usurper points to and pays from his own money.

Core Idea
Profit from usurped property is tainted; charity purifies it, unless the usurper paid from his own funds.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: what happens when someone seizes another's property, uses it to earn profit, and the property changes so its name is lost (fazala ismuhu)? The Hanafi school generally requires charity (tasadduq) on such profit because it stems from an impure cause (khabath). However, there is an exception when the usurper explicitly uses his own money. We'll dissect the reasoning of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad and examine a doubt raised by the author of Nata'ij al-Afkar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key condition
Note the condition 'فزال اسمه' (its name ceased). This signifies a complete change in the property's identity. The profit from such property requires charity.
This is a threshold for ownership transfer in Hanafi law.
2
Understand the exception
When the usurper points to the specific usurped property and pays for it from his own money (not the usurped funds), the profit is permissible and no charity is needed.
The text lists several scenarios of pointing and payment.
3
Analyze the objection
The author of Nata'ij al-Afkar argues that the usurper only owes compensation for the loss of value, not the entire property, so profit should belong to the owner.
This doubt challenges the basis for required charity.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad respond that the profit is tainted because it arose from unlawful disposition. The branch (profit) takes the description of the root (usurpation), so impurity remains.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the principle 'al-far' yattabi' al-asl'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is the relied-upon position: charity is wajib.
This is confirmed in Al-Hidayah.

فأجر وأخذ الأجرة ، فكذا بأجرة عبد مستعار قد أجْرَهُ وأخذ أجره، وكذا تصدَّقَ بربح حصل بالتَّصرُّفِ في المودع أو المغصوب إذا كان مما يتعين بالإشارة، وكذا يتصدق بربح حصل بالشراء بوديعة أو مغصوب لا يتعين بالإشارة إذا أشار إليها ونقدها، فقوله : أو بالشّراءِ عطفٌ على التّصرُّف، أما إن أشار إليها، ونقد غيرها، أو أشار إلى غيرها ونقدها أو أطلق ونقدها : أي لم يشر إلى شيء بل
هذه قال : اشتريت بألف درهم ونقد من دراهم الغصب والوديعة، ففي جميع الصور يطيب له الربح ولا يُحِبُّ له التصدق.
فإن غصب وغير" فزالَ اسمه ")
(1)
قال صاحب نتائج الأفكار » ) : أقول : فيه نوع تأمل ؛ لأنَّ الذي حصل في ضمانه وملكه إنّما هو البعض الفائتُ من المغصوب دون مجموع المغصوب ؛ لأن الكلام فيما إذا نقصته الغلة، فوجب عليه ضمان النقصان مع استرداد الأصل، والظاهر أنّ الغلة بمقابلة منافع مجموع العبد المغصوب المستغل لا بمقابلة منفعة وصفه الفائت فقط فما وجه القول بأنه لا يتصدّق.
ولهما: إنه حصل بسبب خبث وهو التصرُّف في ملك الغير، وما هذا حاله فسبيله التصدق ، إذ الفرعُ يحصل على وصف الأصل، والملك المستند ناقص، فلا ينعدم به الخبث. كذا في «الهداية»(۲).
If he usurped and it changed so its name ceased, he hired out and took the hire, so likewise with the hire of a borrowed slave whom he hired out and took his hire. Likewise, he should give in charity a profit obtained through disposition in a trust or usurped property if it is something that is determined by pointing. Likewise, he should give in charity a profit obtained through purchase with a trust or usurped property that is not determined by pointing if he pointed to it and paid for it. So his saying 'or through purchase' is conjoined to 'disposition'. As for if he pointed to it and paid for other than it, or pointed to other than it and paid for it, or was absolute and paid for it: i.e., he did not point to anything but said: I bought for one thousand dirhams and paid from the dirhams of usurpation and trust, then in all these cases the profit is permissible for him and charity is not liked for him. If he usurped and it changed so its name ceased, the author of Nata'ij al-Afkar said: I say: In this is a kind of reflection; because what has come into his guarantee and ownership is only the part that is lost from the usurped item, not the entire usurped item; because the discussion is when the produce decreased it, so he must guarantee the decrease along with the return of the original, and it is apparent that the produce is in exchange for the usufruct of the entire usurped slave that is exploited, not only in exchange for the benefit of its lost quality. So what is the basis for saying that he does not give charity? And for them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): It occurred due to something impure, which is disposition in another's property, and what is in this state, its way is charity, for the branch attains the description of the root, and the ownership that is established is deficient, so impurity does not cease by it. Thus it is in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the requirement of charity (tasadduq) on profit obtained from usurped property that has changed (taghayyar). The text first states the general ruling: if a usurped property changes such that its name and major benefit are lost (fazala ismuhu), the usurper must give any profit earned from it in charity. Examples include hiring out a borrowed slave or purchasing with usurped money. However, if the usurper points to the property and pays for it, the profit is permissible (halal) and charity is not required. The author of Nata'ij al-Afkar raises a doubt: when a usurped property is diminished (e.g., the usufruct is taken), the usurper is liable only for the loss, not the entire property. The profit should be for the owner of the entire property, so why must the usurper give charity? The response from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), cited in Al-Hidayah, is that the profit was obtained through an impure cause (khabath) — unlawful disposition in another's property. The branch (profit) takes the ruling of the root (the usufruct), and the ownership established is deficient, so the impurity remains. Therefore, charity is obligatory. This disagreement shows the nuanced reasoning within the Hanafi school on the interaction between liability and unlawful gain.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasp): Unlawful seizure and usurpation of another's property without right.
فزال اسمه (fazala ismuhu): The name (and major benefit) of the usurped item has ceased to apply due to change.
التصدق (al-tasadduq): Giving charity as a means of purifying unlawful earnings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does profit from a usurped item require charity?
A) When the usurper points to the item and pays for it.
B) When the usurper's disposition causes the item to change such that its name is lost.
C) When the usurper hires out the item and takes the hire.
D) When the usurper buys something with his own money.

Key Takeaway
Profit from usurped property that has fundamentally changed requires charity, unless the usurper specifically allocated and paid from his own funds.

Conditions for Ownership Transfer in Ghaṣb: The Hanafi Position
Explaining Hanafi ruling on ownership transfer upon valuable alteration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for ownership transfer when a usurper alters property, identify which acts qualify as ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah, and apply the condition of change of name and loss of greatest benefits.

Core Idea
Ownership transfers only when the usurper's act changes the name and destroys the greatest benefits.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that a usurper may gain ownership of usurped property if he performs a valuable alteration that changes its identity. The key is the concept of ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah (valuable manufacture). We will break down the matn and commentary to understand when ownership transfers and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states that if the usurper destroys the greatest benefits, he becomes liable and owns it. Identify the ruling level (wajib) and the consequence (ownership transfer).
2
Define ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah
The commentary explains that the manufacture must be valuable and change the name and most purposes. Examples: grinding wheat, cooking meat.
3
Apply to examples
List the examples given: slaughtering+cooking, grinding, planting, making iron into sword, etc. Note that slaughter alone does not transfer ownership.
4
Distinguish non-transfer cases
Dyeing a garment does not transfer ownership because the name remains. Understand the boundary.
5
Note the internal Hanafi variant
Abū Yūsuf has a narration that ownership transfers but the property is sold for debts, with the owner having priority over creditors. The muftā bihi is the matn's position.

Source Text

حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
للأساس عليها، وهذا عندنا ؛ لأنه أحدث صنعةً متقومة صير حق المالك هالكاً" من وجه، وعند الشافعي : لا ينقطع حقُّ المالكِ عنه ؛ لأنَّ العينَ باق

للشافعي له أن العين باقية، فيبقى على ملكه وتتبعه الصنعة، كما إذا هبت الريح في الحنطة وألقتها في طاحونة الغير فطحنت، ولا معتبر بفعله ؛ لأنه محظور، فلا يصلح سبباً للملك على ما عرف ، فصار كما إذا انعدم الفعلُ أصلاً وصار كما إذا ذبح الشاة المغصوبة وسلخها وأربها. ولنا : إنّه أحدث صنعةً متقوّمة فيصيرُ حق المالك هالكاً من وجه، ألا ترى أنه تبدل الاسمُ وفات معظم المقاصد، وحقه في الصنعة قائمٌ من كل وجه، يترجح على الأصل الذي هو فائت من وجه ولا نجعله سبباً للملك . من حيث أنه محظور، بل من حيث أنه إحداث الصنعة بخلاف الشاة ; لأنّ اسمها باق بعد الذبح والسلخ ، وهذا الوجه يشمل الفصول المذكورة، ويتفرّعُ عليه غيرها، فاحفظه. انتهى.
Book of Usurpation / Its Definition and Rulings
And [if he destroys] its greatest benefits, he becomes liable and owns it without it being permissible [for him] before paying its substitute: like slaughtering a sheep and cooking or roasting it, or grinding wheat and planting it, and making iron into a sword, and brass into a vessel, and building on a plank and brick.
As-sājah (with jīm): a carved prepared plank.
His statement: 'and its greatest benefits' – i.e., its greatest benefits are lost, as when one usurps wheat and grinds it; because the purposes related to the wheat's essence are removed by grinding, which are making it seed, porridge, kashk, etc. As in al-Kifāyah.
His statement: 'and owns it' – i.e., the ownership of the usurped-from person is removed from it, and the usurper owns it, such that if the owner refuses to take the value and wants the roasted meat of the sheep when the usurper slaughtered, cooked, and roasted it, the owner has no [right to] that; because ownership has been removed. As in al-Īḍāḥ, this is the gist of what is in al-‘Ināyah.
His statement: 'without it being permissible' – i.e., it is not permissible for the usurper to benefit from the usurped property until he pays its substitute, like one who usurps a sheep and slaughters, cooks, and roasts it, or usurps wheat and plants it... etc.
His statement: 'or roasts it' – therein is an indication that slaughter alone does not remove ownership, as has passed, but slaughter and cooking, or slaughter and roasting, are like grinding wheat. As in al-‘Ināyah.
His statement: 'and as-sifr' – with damm: [meaning] copper that is called 'kansi' in Hindi. As in al-Ghiyāth, and he said in al-Burhān: 'it is melted copper'.
His statement: 'and building on a plank and brick' – I say: the existence of change in them is not without some obscurity. If he had stated in explaining the principle of the issue: 'If the usurped entity changes by the act of the usurper until its name and greatest benefits are lost, or it mixes with the usurper's property such that separation is impossible without difficulty, the ownership of the usurped is removed' – as al-Zayla‘ī did – then all the examples would have been clear. As in Hāshiyat al-Jalabī.

Ḥusn al-Dirāyah fī Awākhir Sharḥ al-Wiqāyah
Upon the foundation, and this is in our school; because he performed a valuable manufacture that made the owner's right null in one respect, but according to al-Shāfi‘ī: the owner's right is not cut off; because the substance remains.
His statement: 'valuable manufacture' – because the value of the sheep increases by cooking or roasting it, and similarly the value of wheat increases by making it flour. As in al-‘Ināyah.
His statement: 'made the owner's right null' – as a precaution against when he dyes the usurped garment yellow or red, for the owner's right is not cut off even though he performed a valuable manufacture, but it was not null in one respect; don't you see that the name of the garment has not changed, and the greatest purposes have not been lost? As in al-Kifāyah.
His statement: 'and according to al-Shāfi‘ī' – he said in al-Hidāyah: al-Shāfi‘ī said: the owner's right is not cut off, and it is a narration from Abū Yūsuf – may Allah have mercy on him – except that if he chooses to take the flour, he does not guarantee the deficiency according to him; because it leads to usury. According to al-Shāfi‘ī, he guarantees it. And from Abū Yūsuf: that his ownership is removed from him, but it is sold for his debt, and he is more entitled to it than the creditors after his death.

Al-Shāfi‘ī's proof: the substance remains, so it remains in his ownership and the manufacture follows it, just as if the wind blew the wheat and cast it into someone else's mill and it was ground. His act is not considered; because it is prohibited, so it is not a valid cause for ownership as is known. Thus it is as if the act did not occur at all, and thus as if he slaughtered the usurped sheep and skinned it and left it. Our proof: He performed a valuable manufacture, so the owner's right becomes null in one respect; don't you see that the name has changed and most purposes are lost? His right in the manufacture stands in all respects, which outweighs the original that is lost in one respect. And we do not make [his act] a cause for ownership from the aspect that it is prohibited, but rather from the aspect that it is the creation of manufacture, unlike the sheep; because its name remains after slaughter and skinning. This aspect covers the mentioned chapters, and other cases branch from it. So memorize it. End.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the transfer of ownership in cases of usurpation (ghaṣb) where the usurper fundamentally alters the property. The base ruling from the matn states that if the usurper destroys 'the greatest benefits' of the property, he becomes liable for its value and ownership transfers to him, though he cannot use it until he pays the substitute. The commentary expands this with examples: slaughtering and cooking/roasting a sheep, grinding and planting wheat, making iron into a sword, brass into a vessel, and building with plank and brick. The key condition is that the act must be a 'valuable manufacture' (ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah) that changes the name and removes the greatest benefits. The passage then enters an inter-madhhab dispute: al-Shāfi‘ī argues that ownership does not transfer because the substance (al-‘ayn) remains, likening it to wind-driven grinding. The Hanafi response relies on the fact that the name changes and most purposes are lost, outweighing the original ownership. An internal Hanafi dispute is also noted: a narration from Abū Yūsuf holds that ownership does transfer but the property is sold for debts, with the original owner having priority over creditors. The passage also distinguishes dyeing a garment, where ownership does not transfer because the name remains.

Key Terms
صنعة متقومة (ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah): A valuable manufacture that increases the value of the property, such as cooking meat or grinding wheat.
ملك (milk): Ownership; in this context, it transfers from the original owner to the usurper upon alteration.
غصب (ghaṣb): Unlawful usurpation of another's property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which of the following cases does ownership of the usurped property transfer to the usurper?
A) The usurper dyes a garment red.
B) The usurper grinds wheat into flour.
C) The usurper slaughters a sheep but does not cook or roast it.
D) The usurper borrows a plank and builds with it.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of usurped property transfers to the usurper only when his act changes the name and eliminates the greatest benefits, creating a valuable manufacture (ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah).
