The Hanafi-Shafi‘i Dispute on Ownership Transfer in Ghaṣb
Inter-madhhab dispute over ownership transfer upon valuable manufacture in ghaṣb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Shafi‘i arguments regarding ownership transfer upon alteration of usurped property, identify the thamarah al-khilaf, and evaluate the Hanafi response to the Shafi‘i objection.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school transfers ownership based on the value of the manufacture; al-Shāfi‘ī retains ownership based on the substance.

This lesson delves into the inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shāfi‘ī over whether ownership of usurped property transfers to the usurper when he performs a valuable manufacture. We will examine the proofs from both sides and the practical consequences. The Hanafi position is that ownership transfers, while al-Shāfi‘ī holds that it does not because the substance remains.

Guided Path
1
Restate the Hanafi position and its basis
Recall from the previous lesson: ownership transfers when the usurper performs a ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah that changes the name and destroys greatest benefits. The basis is that the owner's right becomes null in one respect.
2
Analyze al-Shāfi‘ī's objection
Al-Shāfi‘ī argues that the substance (al-‘ayn) remains, so ownership remains with the original owner. He uses the analogy of wind-driven grinding and the case of slaughter without cooking.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between cases where the name changes (wheat to flour) and where it does not (slaughtered sheep still called sheep). The creation of manufacture itself is the cause, not the prohibited act.
4
Identify the internal Hanafi variant
Abū Yūsuf has a narration that ownership transfers but the property is sold for debts, with the original owner having priority over creditors. This is noted but the relied-upon position is the matn's.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, the owner cannot reclaim the altered property but only its value; in the Shafi‘i school, the owner can reclaim the specific property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب / تعريفه وأحكامه
وأعظم منافعه ضَمِنَهُ وملكه بلا حل قبل أداء بدله : كذبح شاة وطبخها أو شيها ، أو طحن بر وزرعه ، وجعل حديد سيفاً، والصفر إناء، والبناء على ساجة ولبن
السَّاجة بالجيم : خشبة منحوتة مهيأة
قوله : وأعظم منافعه ؛ أي زالَ أعظم منافعه كما إذا غصب حنطة وطحنها ؛ لأنَّ المقاصد المتعلقة بعين الحنطة تزول بالطحن ، وهي جعلها بذراً وهريسة وكشكاً وغيرها. كذا في «الكفاية».
قوله : وملكه ؛ أي زالَ ملك المغصوب منه عنه ، وملكها الغاصب، حتى لو أبي المالك عن أخذ القيمة وأرادَ اللّحم مشوياً في الشاة إذا ذبحها الغاصب وطبخها وشواها، لم يكن للمالك ذلك ؛ لأنَّ الملك قد زال. كذا في «الإيضاح»، هذا زبدة ما في «العناية».
قوله : بلا حل...الخ ؛ أي لا يحل للغاصب الانتفاع بالمغصوب حتى يؤدّي بدله ، كمَن غصب شاة وذبحها وطبخها وشواها ، أو غصب بُرا وزرعه ... الخ.
قوله : أو شيها ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنّ الذبح وحده لا يزيل الملك، كما مرّ، بل الذبح والطبخ، والذبح والشي بمنزلة طحن الحنطة. كذا في «العناية».
قوله : والصفر؛ - بالضم - : روئین که بهـنـدي كـانـسـي كـويـند. كذا في «الغياث»، وقال في «البرهان» و آن مس باقلعي گداخته.
قوله : والبناء على ساجة ولبن ; أقول : وجود التغير فيهما لا يخلو عن نوع خفاء ، فلو قال في تقرير أصل المسألة : لو تغيرت العين المغصوبة بفعل الغاصب حتى زال اسمها وأعظم منافعها ، أو اختلط بملك الغاصب بحيث يمتنع امتيازها لو يمكن بحرج زال ملك المغصوب. كما فعله الزَّيْلَعِي له لكانت الأمثلة كلها واضحة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي»

حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
للأساس عليها، وهذا عندنا ؛ لأنه أحدث صنعةً متقومة صير حق المالك هالكاً" من وجه، وعند الشافعي : لا ينقطع حقُّ المالكِ عنه ؛ لأنَّ العينَ باق
قوله : صنعة متقومة ; لأنّ قيمة الشاةِ تزدادُ بطبخها أو شيها، وكذلك قيمةُ الحنطة تزداد بجعلها دقيقاً. كذا في «العناية».
قوله : صير حق المالك هالكاً ; احتراز عمّا إذا صبغ الثوب المغصوب أصفر أو أحمر فإنّه لا ينقطع حق المالك مع أنه أحدث صنعةً متقومة، ولكن لم يكن هالكاً من وجه، ألا ترى أنه لم يتبدل اسم الثوب ، ولم يفت أعظم المقاصد. كذا في «الكفاية».
قوله : وعند الشافعي ... الخ ; قال في «الهداية : وقال الشافعي له : لا ينقطع حق المالك، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف الله غير أنه إذا اختار أخذ الدقيق لا يضمنه النقصان عنده ؛ لأنه يؤدّي إلى الربا، وعند الشافعي له يضمنه ، وعن أبي يوسف له أنه يزول ملكه عنه ، لكنّه يباع في دينه، وهو أحق به من الغرماء بعد موته.

للشافعي له أن العين باقية، فيبقى على ملكه وتتبعه الصنعة، كما إذا هبت الريح في الحنطة وألقتها في طاحونة الغير فطحنت، ولا معتبر بفعله ؛ لأنه محظور، فلا يصلح سبباً للملك على ما عرف ، فصار كما إذا انعدم الفعلُ أصلاً وصار كما إذا ذبح الشاة المغصوبة وسلخها وأربها. ولنا : إنّه أحدث صنعةً متقوّمة فيصيرُ حق المالك هالكاً من وجه، ألا ترى أنه تبدل الاسمُ وفات معظم المقاصد، وحقه في الصنعة قائمٌ من كل وجه، يترجح على الأصل الذي هو فائت من وجه ولا نجعله سبباً للملك . من حيث أنه محظور، بل من حيث أنه إحداث الصنعة بخلاف الشاة ; لأنّ اسمها باق بعد الذبح والسلخ ، وهذا الوجه يشمل الفصول المذكورة، ويتفرّعُ عليه غيرها، فاحفظه. انتهى.

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the transfer of ownership in cases of usurpation (ghaṣb) where the usurper fundamentally alters the property. The base ruling from the matn states that if the usurper destroys 'the greatest benefits' of the property, he becomes liable for its value and ownership transfers to him, though he cannot use it until he pays the substitute. The passage then enters an inter-madhhab dispute: al-Shāfi‘ī argues that ownership does not transfer because the substance (al-‘ayn) remains, likening it to wind-driven grinding. The Hanafi response relies on the fact that the name changes and most purposes are lost, outweighing the original ownership. Key Terms
صنعة متقومة (ṣan‘ah mutaqawwimah): A valuable manufacture that increases the value of the property, such as cooking meat or grinding wheat.
عين (‘ayn): The substance or essence of the property that remains after alteration.
محظور (maḥẓūr): Prohibited; in this context, the usurper's act is unlawful.

What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) between the Hanafi and Shafi‘i positions on grinding wheat?
A) Hanafi: owner can reclaim flour; Shafi‘i: owner only gets value.
B) Hanafi: owner only gets value; Shafi‘i: owner can reclaim flour.
C) Both schools allow the owner to reclaim flour.

Striking Coins from Usurped Gold or Silver: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership after transformation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on ownership of transformed usurped property and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A usurper does not become owner if the essential attributes of the original metal remain.

This passage examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: if a person usurps gold or silver and then fashions it into coins or a vessel, does the usurper acquire ownership? Abu Hanifa holds that the original owner retains full rights, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that the usurper's valuable craftsmanship transfers ownership. Understanding this disagreement reveals how the Hanafi school weighs essence (‛ayn) versus transformation (sinā‛ah).

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the scenario: usurper takes gold or silver and strikes it into coins or a vessel. State the two positions: Abu Hanifa says owner remains owner; Sahibayn say usurper becomes owner.
2
Follow Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the name (ism) and original meaning (thumaniyyah) persist: the metal is still weighable and subject to riba. The essence is unchanged.
The term 'thumaniyyah' refers to monetary value as a medium of exchange.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the transformation (sina'ah) is valuable and changes the object's function. Raw metal cannot be used as capital in mudarabah, but struck coins can.
They use qiyas to other cases where transformation extinguishes the owner's right.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa's response: the suitability for mudarabah is an attribute of the manufactured item, not the substance. The craftsmanship is not compensable when compared with the same metal.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the original owner can take the coins/vessel without compensating the usurper. Under Sahibayn, the usurper owns the new object and must give an equivalent (mithl) to the owner.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ضرب الحجرين درهماً وديناراً أو إناء لم يملكه، وهو لمالكه بلا شيء)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ لأن الاسم باق، ومعناه الأصلي : الثَّمنية، وكونه موزوناً وهو باق حتى يجري فيه الربا، وعندهما : يصيران للغاصب قياساً على غيرهما.
[٢]قوله : فإن ضرب الحجرين...الخ ; صورته : إن غصب فضةً أو ذهباً فضرب بها دراهم أو دنانير أو إناء لم يملكه الغاصب، بل هو لمالكه فيأخذها ولا شيء للغاصب، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وقالا : يملكها الغاصب وعليه مثلها ؛ لأنه أحدث صنعةً معتبرة صيّرت حق المالك هالكاً من وجه ألا ترى أنه كسره وفات بعض المقاصد، والتبر لا يصلح رأس المال في المضاربات والشركات، والمضروب يصلح ذلك. وله : إن العين باقية من كل وجه، ألا ترى أن الاسم باق، ومعناه الأصلي : الثمنية، وكونه موزوناً، وإنّه باق حتى يجري فيه الربا باعتباره وصلاحيته لرأس المال من أحكام الصنعة دون العين، وكذا الصنعة فيها غير متقومة مطلقاً ؛ لأنه لا قيمة لها عند المقابلة بجنسها. كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
If he strikes two stones [into] a dirham and a dinar or a vessel, he does not own it, and it remains for its owner without anything [for the usurper]. This is according to Abu Hanifa because the name remains and its original meaning is monetary value and it being weighed, and it remains such that riba applies to it. And according to the two [companions], they become the property of the usurper by analogy to other cases.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the transformation of usurped gold or silver into coins or a vessel. Abu Hanifa holds that the owner retains ownership because the essential characteristics of the original material (name, monetary value, being weighable) persist, and the transformation does not create a substantial new property. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the usurper's work creates a valuable new object (like coins usable as capital in partnerships), thus the owner's right is partially extinguished, and the usurper becomes owner, owing an equivalent (mithl) to the original owner. The commentary elaborates that Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the raw metal (tibr) is not suitable for mudarabah or partnerships while the struck coins are, but this suitability is a property of the manufactured item, not of the original metal, and the craftsmanship (sina'ah) is not compensable because it has no value when compared with the same metal. This dispute is recorded in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
ثمنية (thumaniyyah): Monetary value; the quality of being a medium of exchange or standard of value.
صنعة متقومة (sina'ah mutaqawwimah): A transformation that has compensable value; one that partially extinguishes the owner's right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa in the passage, why does the owner retain ownership of gold struck into coins?
A) Because the usurper's act is forbidden.
B) Because the name and original meaning (thumaniyyah) remain.
C) Because the coins are worth more than the gold.
D) Because the owner did not consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the transformation of usurped gold or silver does not transfer ownership if the essential attributes (name, monetary value, weighability) persist.

Owner's Options When Usurper Slaughters a Sheep or Tears Cloth
Hanafi ruling on compensation options for usurper's alteration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner when a usurper alters property by slaughtering or tearing, and apply the ruling to similar partial-loss scenarios.

Core Idea
The usurper does not own the altered item; the owner chooses between full value or the item plus compensation for loss.

This concise passage from the Hanafi text on ghasb outlines the remedies available to an owner when a usurper destroys or partially damages property. The key principle is that the forbidden act of ghasb does not transfer ownership. The owner is given a choice to either take the full value of the property from the usurper (leaving the altered item) or take the altered item and claim compensation for the loss in value. The same rule applies when a garment is torn, causing loss of part of its substance and benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wrongful act
Recognize that the usurper has committed a forbidden act (ghasb) by slaughtering a sheep or tearing a cloth.
2
State the owner's first option
The owner may 'throw' the slaughtered sheep back at the usurper and demand its full value. This means the usurper keeps the item but pays the entire value.
طرحها المالك عليه وأخذ قيمتها
3
State the owner's second option
The owner may take the slaughtered sheep and claim compensation for the loss (nuqsan) in value due to the slaughter.
أو أخذها وضمنه نقصانها
4
Apply the same rule to tearing cloth
If the usurper tears a garment, causing partial loss of the cloth and partial loss of its benefit (but not total destruction), the same two options apply.
وكذا لو خرق ثوباً وفوت بعض العين وبعض نفعِهِ لا كله
5
Understand the underlying limit
The choice exists only when the item still partially exists. If the item were completely destroyed, the owner would only have the option of full value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ذبح شاة غيره طرحها المالك عليه، وأخذ قيمتها، أو أخذها وضمنه نقصانها، وكذا لو خرق ثوباً وفوت بعض العين وبعض نفعِهِ لا كله
If he slaughters another's sheep, the owner may throw it back at him [i.e., leave it with the usurper] and take its value, or take it and claim compensation for its loss. Similarly, if he tears a garment and causes loss of part of the substance and part of its benefit, not all.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is a ruling statement (matn) from the text on ghasb (usurpation). It gives the owner a choice when the usurper has slaughtered a sheep: either take the full value from the usurper and leave the slaughtered sheep with him, or take the slaughtered sheep and claim compensation for the loss (i.e., the reduction in value due to slaughter). The same rule applies if the usurper tears a garment, causing partial loss of the cloth and partial loss of its benefit (but not total destruction). The principle is that the usurper's forbidden act does not grant him ownership; the owner retains rights but the transformation may partially extinguish them.

Key Terms
طرحها المالك عليه (taraha al-malik 'alayhi): The owner leaves the slaughtered sheep with the usurper and demands its full value.
ضمنه نقصانها (daminahu nuqsanaha): Compensation for the loss in value caused by the usurper's act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When someone slaughters another's sheep, what options does the owner have according to the passage?
A) Only take the slaughtered sheep.
B) Only take the value of the sheep.
C) Either take the value and leave the sheep, or take the sheep and claim compensation for loss.
D) Force the usurper to return a live sheep.

Key Takeaway
The owner of an altered item has a choice: take full value and leave the item, or keep the item and claim compensation for the loss.

Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Transformed Usurped Metal
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership after transformation of usurped metal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on ownership of transformed usurped gold or silver and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Craftsmanship does not change the essence of usurped metal; the owner retains it.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a usurper gains ownership by transforming gold or silver into coins or vessels. The passage from Al-Hidayah presents the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples. Understanding their reasoning deepens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists weigh essence (ayn) against craftsmanship (sina'ah) in property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Dispute
Recognize that the case is about a usurper who takes gold or silver and strikes it into dirhams, dinars, or vessels. The dispute is whether this transformation transfers ownership to the usurper.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Its Proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the original owner retains ownership. The proof is that the substance (ayn) remains fully: the name 'gold' or 'silver' persists, the metal is still weighed and ribawi, and the essential quality of being a medium of exchange (thamaniyyah) is unchanged.
The usurper's act is prohibited and cannot cause ownership.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Their Proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the usurper acquires ownership of the transformed item, and must return an equivalent (mithl) to the original owner. Their proof is that the craftsmanship is significant—it destroys the owner's right in one respect, because bullion is not suitable for mudarabah partnerships while struck coins are. This is supported by analogy (qiyas) to other transformative acts.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Rebuttal
Abu Hanifa responds that the new attributes (like suitability for capital) are rulings of the craftsmanship, not changes to the essence itself. He further argues that craftsmanship is not valuable when compared with its own kind (i.e., a craftsperson's work on silver has no added value if one is exchanging the silver for its equivalent weight in silver).
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the original owner can reclaim the struck metal without any compensation to the usurper. Under the Sahibayn's view, the usurper keeps the metal but must pay an equivalent amount (mithl) to the original owner.
The mufta bihi is not specified in this text; both are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب / تعريفه وأحكامه
فإن ضرب الحجرين درهماً وديناراً أو إناء لم يملكه، وهو لمالكه بلا شيء
ولا يعتبر فعلُ الغاصب ، لأَنَّهُ محظور، فلا يصير سبباً للملك.
(فإن ضرب الحجرين) درهماً وديناراً أو إناء لم يملكه، وهو لمالكه بلا شيء)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ لأن الاسم باق، ومعناه الأصلي : الثَّمنية، وكونه موزوناً وهو باق حتى يجري فيه الربا، وعندهما : يصيران للغاصب قياساً على غيرهما.
قوله : ولا يعتبر فعل الغاصب ؛ جواب إشكال مقدَّر، وهو أن يقال فيما ذكرت من الاستثناء لم يوجد فيه الفعل، وفي المتنازع فيه وجد الفعل. كذا في «الكفاية).
قوله : فإن ضرب الحجرين...الخ ؛ صورته : إن غصب فضةً أو ذهباً فضرب بها دراهم أو دنانير أو إناء لم يملكه الغاصب، بل هو لمالكه فيأخذها ولا شيء للغاصب، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وقالا : يملكها الغاصب وعليه مثلها ؛ لأنه أحدث صنعةً معتبرة صيّرت حق المالك هالكاً من وجه ألا ترى أنه كسره وفات بعض المقاصد، والتبر لا يصلح رأس المال في المضاربات والشركات، والمضروب يصلح ذلك. وله : إن العين باقية من كل وجه، ألا ترى أن الاسم باق، ومعناه الأصلي : الثمنية، وكونه موزوناً، وإنّه باق حتى يجري فيه الربا باعتباره وصلاحيته لرأس المال من أحكام الصنعة دون العين، وكذا الصنعة فيها غير متقومة مطلقاً ؛ لأنه لا قيمة لها عند المقابلة بجنسها. كذا في «الهداية».
Chapter of Usurpation / Its Definition and Rulings. If he strikes with two stones a dirham and a dinar or a vessel, he does not own it, and it belongs to its owner without anything (for the usurper). And the act of the usurper is not considered, because it is prohibited, so it does not become a cause for ownership. (If he strikes with two stones) a dirham and a dinar or a vessel, he does not own it, and it is for its owner without anything; this according to Abu Hanifa, because the name remains, and its original meaning is being a medium of exchange and being weighed, and it remains such that riba applies to it. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they become owned by the usurper by analogy to other things. He said: 'The act of the usurper is not considered' is a response to an anticipated objection, and it is said regarding what you mentioned of the exception that no act is found in it, while in the disputed case the act is found. That is in al-Kifaya. He said: 'If he strikes with two stones... etc.' Its illustration: If he usurped silver or gold and struck it into dirhams or dinars or a vessel, the usurper does not own it, rather it is for its owner, so he takes it and nothing for the usurper. This according to Abu Hanifa. They said: The usurper owns it and owes its like, because he created a significant craftsmanship that made the owner's right void in one respect; do you not see that he broke it and some purposes are lost, and bullion is not suitable as capital in mudarabah and partnerships, while the struck metal is suitable. And for him (Abu Hanifa): The substance remains in every respect; do you not see that the name remains, and its original meaning is being a medium of exchange and being weighed, and it remains such that riba applies to it, and its suitability as capital is among the rulings of craftsmanship not the substance, and likewise the craftsmanship in it is not valuable absolutely because it has no value when compared with its own kind. This is in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ownership of usurped gold or silver that has been transformed into coins or vessels by the usurper's act of striking. The matn (base text) states the general rule: the usurper does not own the transformed item; it remains the property of the original owner, and the usurper receives nothing. The commentary explains that the reason is that the usurper's act is prohibited and thus cannot serve as a cause for ownership. Abu Hanifa's position is that the owner retains ownership because the essential attributes of the metal—its name, its value as a medium of exchange, and its weighability—remain intact, and the metal still functions as a ribawi item. His two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, disagree: they hold that the usurper acquires ownership of the transformed item, returning an equivalent (mithl) to the original owner. Their reasoning is that the craftsmanship (sina'ah) is significant and effectively destroys the owner's right in one sense, as bullion is not suitable for mudarabah or partnerships while struck coins are. Abu Hanifa counters that the substance (ayn) remains fully, and the new attributes are merely rulings of craftsmanship, not changes to the essence itself, and the craftsmanship is not valuable when compared with its own kind. The passage includes a footnote clarifying that the principle that the usurper's act is not considered is a response to a potential objection: in the exception (where no act occurred), the ruling is clear; in the disputed case, an act did occur, yet it is still not considered. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not specified here; the dispute remains between the Imam and his disciples.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right and without permission.
الصنعة (al-sina'ah): Craftsmanship or manufacturing process that alters the form of the item.
الثمنية (al-thamaniyyah): The attribute of being a medium of exchange (thaman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why does the usurper own the struck metal?
A) Because the name remains
B) Because the craftsmanship is significant and destroys the owner's right in one respect
C) Because the usurper's act is not prohibited
D) Because the metal is still ribawi

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa holds that the essence of usurped metal is unchanged by striking, so ownership remains with the owner; the Sahibayn consider the craftsmanship significant.

Owner's Options in Partial Destruction of Usurped Property
Rulings on partial destruction of usurped property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner of a partially destroyed usurped item and apply the distinction between major (fahish) and minor (yasir) loss.

Core Idea
Partial destruction of usurped property gives the owner the choice: take the item and claim deficiency, or force the usurper to take it and pay full value.

This passage addresses a common scenario in mu'amalat: when someone partially ruins another's property, such as slaughtering a sheep (destroying its potential benefits) or tearing a garment (impairing its utility). The Hanafi school provides the owner with a choice, balancing the rights of both parties. Understanding this ruling is essential for dealing with cases of damage to property, especially when the damage is not total.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the owner's options
The passage deals with partial destruction of usurped property, illustrated by slaughtering a sheep or tearing a garment. The owner has two clear options: (1) force the usurper to take the damaged item and pay full value, or (2) keep the item and claim compensation for the deficiency (loss in value).
This is the core legal rule.
2
Understand the rationale: partial destruction with remaining benefit
The commentary explains that the sheep's meat remains (benefit) but the milk, offspring, and pregnancy benefits are lost. Similarly, the garment's fabric remains but some utility is lost. Because some benefit remains, the destruction is not complete, allowing the owner a choice.
The Hanafi classification: 'itlaf min wajh' (destruction from one aspect).
3
Distinguish major loss (fahish) from minor loss (yasir)
The passage distinguishes between cases where some part of the substance or benefit is lost (fahish) and cases where only the quality diminishes with no loss of substance (yasir). In major loss, the owner has the two options; in minor loss, only compensation for the monetary deficiency is due.
Key terms: فوت بعض العين والمنفعة vs. نقص يسير.
4
Apply the ruling to other items beyond the examples
The commentary states the ruling is general for any worn item (like a shirt) or unworn fabric (like coarse cloth). The principle applies to any usurped property that suffers partial destruction - e.g., machinery, electronics, vehicles.
The ruling is not limited to sheep or garments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ذبح شاة غيره طرحها المالك عليه ، وأخذ قيمتها ، أو أخذها وضمنه نقصانها، وكذا لو خرق ثوباً وفوت بعض العين وبعض نفعِهِ لا كله.
( فإن ذبح شاة غيره طرحها المالك عليه ، وأخذ قيمتها ، أو أخذها وضمنه نقصانها ) ، وكذا لو خرق ثوباً وفوت بعض العين وبعض نفعه لا كله.
وفي يسير نقصه ولم يفوت شيئاً منها ضمن ما نقص.
أي إن المالك مخير إن شاء ضمنه قيمتها وسلم الشاة إليه، وإن شاء أخذها وضمن نقصانها ؛ لأنه اتلاف من وجه ؛ لفوات بعض المنافع كالحمل والدر والنسل وبقاء بعضها وهو اللحم.
قوله : وفوّت بعض العين ؛ يعني من حيث الظاهر والغالب إذا الظاهر أن الثوب إذا قطع يفوت شيء من أجزائه، ويفوت بعض منافعه، وتبقى بعضها.
قوله : وفي يسير.. الخ ؛ معناه لا يفوت فيه شيء من العين والمنفعة، وإنما يدخل فيه النقصان من حيث ماليته بسبب فواتِ الجودة قبل الأول، إشارة إلى الفاحش، والثاني إلى اليسير، وهو الصحيح، وإنّما وضع المسألة في الثوب إشارة إلى أنّ الحكم عام في الذي يلبس كالقميص وغيره، وفيما لم يلبس كالكرباس.
If he slaughters the sheep of another, the owner may throw it upon him (the usurper) and take its value, or he may take it and guarantee its deficiency. Similarly, if he tore a garment and caused the loss of some part of the substance and some of its benefit, not all. (If he slaughters the sheep of another, the owner may throw it upon him and take its value, or he may take it and guarantee its deficiency) and similarly, if he tore a garment and caused the loss of some of the substance and some of its benefit, not all. And in the case of a slight deficiency and he did not cause the loss of anything from it, he guarantees what is lost. That is, the owner has the choice: if he wishes, he may make him liable for its value and hand over the sheep to him, or if he wishes, he may take it and make him liable for its deficiency, because it is destruction in one respect, due to the loss of some benefits such as pregnancy, milk, and offspring, while some remains, which is the meat. He said: 'And he caused the loss of some of the substance' means from the outward and apparent perspective, because it is apparent that when the garment is cut, some of its parts are lost, and some of its benefits are lost, and some remain. He said: 'And in slight deficiency... etc.' It means that no part of the substance or benefit is lost; rather, the deficiency enters into its monetary value due to the loss of prior quality. The first (major loss) refers to excessive, the second to slight, and this is correct. And the reason the case is set in a garment is to indicate that the ruling is general for that which is worn like a shirt and others, and for that which is not worn like coarse cloth.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for partial destruction of usurped property, illustrated by slaughtering someone else's sheep and tearing a garment. The matn states that the owner has two options: either force the usurper to take the damaged item and pay full value, or keep the item and claim compensation for the loss (the deficiency in value). The commentary explains that this is because the usurper's act is a partial destruction: some benefits are lost (e.g., the sheep's milk or offspring, or the garment's full use), but the core substance (the meat, the fabric) remains. For a slight deficiency where nothing substantial is lost, the usurper only compensates for the reduction in monetary value. The commentary also clarifies that the ruling is not limited to garments but applies to any similar item. The distinction is between fahish (excessive) loss where some parts are lost, and yasir (slight) loss where only quality diminishes. In all cases, the usurper is liable for the damage, and the owner has the right to choose the most beneficial option.

Key Terms
اتلاف (itlaf): Destruction or loss of property; here, partial destruction that eliminates some benefits but not the whole.
النقصان (al-nuqsan): The deficiency in value or benefit caused by the usurper's act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Take the sheep and claim no compensation, or leave it and claim full value.
B) Take the sheep and claim compensation for the deficiency, or leave it to the usurper and take full value.
C) Only take the sheep and claim compensation for the deficiency.
D) Only force the usurper to take the sheep and pay full value.

Key Takeaway
The owner of partially destroyed usurped property has the right to choose between claiming full value or the item plus deficiency.

Usurper's Building: Removal and Owner's Option
Rulings on usurped land: removal of structures and owner's option to pay value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Zahir al-Riwayah position on building/planting on usurped land, apply the hadith 'la haqq li 'irq zalim', and evaluate the alternative view of Imam Muhammad based on maslahah.

Core Idea
The usurper must remove his structure; the owner may pay to keep it to prevent harm.

This passage addresses a common property dispute: what happens when someone unlawfully builds or plants on another's land? The primary ruling, supported by a prophetic hadith, is that the usurper must remove the structure and restore the land. However, a concession allows the landowner to pay compensation and retain the additions, preventing unnecessary harm. We also encounter an intra-Hanafi difference with Imam Muhammad, who would allow the usurper to keep the land if the building is more valuable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the primary ruling
The act is building or planting on another's land (ghasb). The primary ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah is that the usurper is commanded to uproot (qal') the structure or trees and return the land empty to its owner.
This is a wajib obligation.
2
Understand the dalil for removal
The proof is the hadith 'la haqq li 'irq zalim' (no right for a wrongful root) and the analogy of filling another's container with food: the occupier must vacate.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (dalala qati) for the obligation to remove.
3
Note the owner's option to pay and keep
If removal would harm the land (e.g., break soil), the owner may instead pay the usurper the value of the building/trees and keep them. This is a concession to prevent mutual harm.
The amount paid depends on whether all benefit is lost (full value) or only slight deficiency (partial compensation).
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad (within the Hanafi school) held that if the building/trees are more valuable than the land, the usurper can own the land by paying its value to the owner. This is based on preventing waste and analogical reasoning.
This view is not from Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'هذا في ظاهر الرواية' indicating the primary position is the relied-upone one. Zahir al-Riwayah is preferred, so the mufta bihi is that removal is required with the owner's option to pay to keep.
The authority_weight is zahir_riwayah, which takes precedence over the Nawadir (Muhammad's view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن بنى في أرض غيره، أو غَرَسَ أُمَرَ بالقلع والرِّدّ وللمالكِ أن يَضْمَنَ له قيمة بناء أو شجرٍ أمرَ فوت كل النفع يضمنه كل القيمة، وفي يسير نقصه ولم يفوت شيئاً منها ضمن ما نقص.
ومن بنى في أرض غيره، أو غَرَسَ أُمرَ بالقلع والرد)، هذا في ظاهر الرواية ، وعند محمد الله : إن كان قيمة البناء أو الغرس أكثر من قيمة الأرض فالغاصب يملك الأرض بقيمتها.
قوله : بالقلع والردّ ؛ أي يقلع البناء أو الشجر ورد الأرض فارغة إلى مالكها ؛ لقوله : «ليس لعرق ظالم حق» ؛ ولأن ملك صاحب الأرض باق، فإنّ الأرض لم تصر مستهلكة، والغصب لا يتحقق فيها، ولا بد للملك من سبب فيؤمر الشاغلُ بتفريغها، كما إذا شغل ظرف غيره بطعامه. كذا في «الهداية».
قوله : وللمالك... الخ ؛ أي إن كانت الأرض تنقص بقلع ذلك ، فللمالك أن يضمن له قيمة البناء، وقيمة الشجر مأمور بقلعه ويكونان له ؛ لأن فيه نظراً لهما، ودفع الضرر.
And whoever builds on another's land or plants, he is commanded to uproot and return, and the owner may make him liable for the value of the building or trees. In the case of loss of all benefit, he guarantees the entire value; and in slight deficiency without losing anything, he guarantees what is lost. And whoever builds on another's land or plants, he is commanded to uproot and return. This is in the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah). And according to Muhammad, if the value of the building or planting is greater than the value of the land, then the usurper owns the land by its value. He said: 'By uprooting and returning' means he uproots the building or trees and returns the land empty to its owner, due to his saying: 'There is no right for a wrongful root' (hadith), and because the ownership of the landowner remains, as the land has not become consumed, and usurpation is not realized in it, and ownership must have a cause, so the occupier is ordered to vacate it, just as if he occupies another's container with his food. This is in al-Hidayah. He said: 'And for the owner... etc.' means if the land is diminished by uprooting, then the owner may make him liable for the value of the building and trees while ordering their removal, and they become his (the owner's), because there is consideration for both and prevention of harm.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a usurper builds a structure or plants trees on another person's land. The primary ruling is that the usurper must remove the building or trees and restore the land to its owner (hadith: 'There is no right for a wrongful root'). However, if the land would be damaged by removal (e.g., broken soil), the owner has the option to pay the value of the building or trees to the usurper and keep them. This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah. A different view attributed to Imam Muhammad states that if the building or planting is more valuable than the land, the usurper can own the land by paying its value to the owner. The commentary also clarifies that in cases of full loss of benefit, the entire value must be paid; in slight damage, only the deficiency is compensated. The proof for the obligation to remove is from the hadith and the principle that the landowner's ownership is intact. The harm prevention principle allows the owner to retain the structures by paying compensation, thus avoiding waste and mutual harm.

Key Terms
القلع (al-qal'): Uprooting or demolishing a structure or tree to restore the land.
العرق الظالم (al-'irq al-zalim): A wrongful root or encroachment: any unlawful construction or plantation on another's land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah position, what is the primary ruling for the usurper who builds on someone else's land?
A) He may keep the building by paying the land's value.
B) He must remove the building and return the land.
C) He automatically owns the land if the building is more valuable.
D) He can keep the building by paying its value to the owner.

Key Takeaway
The usurper must remove his encroachment; the owner's option to pay is a concession to prevent harm; this is the relied-upon view from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Building or Planting on Usurped Land: Removal vs. Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on usurper's building/planting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling for a usurper who builds or plants, explain Imam Muhammad's variant, and apply the compensation calculation when removal damages the land.

Core Idea
The usurper must remove what he built, but compensation is an option to prevent further harm.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in property law: what happens when someone builds or plants on land they do not own. The Hanafi school has a clear default ruling supported by the hadith 'There is no right for a wrongful root', but Imam Muhammad offers a pragmatic variant. We will walk through the evidence, the intra-madhhab difference, and the practical calculation of compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah
Start by extracting the primary ruling from the text: the usurper is ordered to remove (qal') and return the land empty. This is the mufta bihi position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The ruling is based on the hadith cited.
2
Recognize Imam Muhammad's variant
The text mentions that according to Imam Muhammad, if the value of the building/trees exceeds the land value, the usurper may own the land by paying its value. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
This variant is not the relied-upon view in fatwa.
3
Analyze the owner's option when removal causes damage
The commentary explains that if the land would be diminished by removal, the owner may instead claim the value of the building/trees (i.e., compensation) and keep them. This option protects both parties from unnecessary harm.
The compensation is calculated as the increase in land value due to the building/trees.
4
Apply the compensation calculation to a numerical example
The text provides an example: land worth 100, trees worth 10 when removed less 1 dirham removal cost = 9, so land with trees = 109, owner pays 9. Practice this calculation.
This method is based on evaluation as if the building/trees were to be removed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the thamarah al-khilaf
The default ruling (removal) remains the operative position for fatwa, but the owner's option for compensation is widely accepted. Imam Muhammad's view is an alternative that applies only when the building's value vastly exceeds the land.
The practical consequence: in most cases, the usurper must remove; only in special circumstances is compensation chosen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله: وفي يسير.. الخ؛ معناه لا يفوت فيه شيء من العين والمنفعة، وإنما يدخل فيه النقصان من حيث ماليته بسبب فوات الجودة. [٢] قوله: بالقلع والردّ؛ أي يقلع البناء أو الشجر ورد الأرض فارغة إلى مالكها؛ لقوله عليه السلام: «ليس لعرق ظالم حق»؛ ولأن ملك صاحب الأرض باق، فإن الأرض لم تصر مستهلكة، والغصب لا يتحقق فيها، ولا بد للملك من سبب فيؤمر الشاغل بتفريغها. [٣] قوله: وللمالك... الخ؛ أي إن كانت الأرض تنقص بقلع ذلك، فللمالك أن يضمن له قيمة البناء، وقيمة الشجر مأمور بقلعه ويكونان له؛ لأن فيه نظراً لهما، ودفع الضرر عنهما. بقلعه إن نقصت به، فتقوم بلا شجر وبناء، وتقوم مع أحدهما مستحق القلع فيضمن الفضل. قيل: قيمة الشجر المستحق للقلع أقل من قيمته مقلوعا، فقيمة المقلوع إذا نقصت منها أجرة القلع، فالباقي قيمة الشجر المستحق القلع، فإذا كانت قيمة الأرض مئة، وقيمة الشجر المقلوع عشرة، وأجرة القلع درهماً، بقي تسعة دراهم، فالأرض مع هذا الشجر تقوم بمئة وتسعة دراهم، فيضمن المالك التسعة.
And in a small deficiency and it does not cause loss of any part of it, he guarantees what is deficient. And whoever builds in someone else's land or plants, he is ordered to remove and return, and the owner may claim from him the value of a building or trees. The command to remove and return is in Zahir al-Riwayah. According to Muhammad: If the value of the building or planting is more than the value of the land, the usurper owns the land by its value, and the owner may claim the value of the building or trees. [1] His statement: 'And in a small deficiency...' etc.; its meaning: nothing of the substance or benefit is lost, but the deficiency enters in terms of value due to loss of quality. [2] His statement: 'with removal and return'; meaning he removes the building or trees and returns the land empty to its owner; due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'There is no right for a wrongful root'; and because the ownership of the landowner remains, for the land has not become consumed, and usurpation is not actualized in it, and the owner must have a cause, so the occupant is ordered to vacate it. [3] His statement: 'and the owner...' etc.; meaning if the land is diminished by removal, then the owner may guarantee the value of the building and the value of the trees which are ordered to be removed, and they become his; because there is consideration for both, and repelling harm from them. By its removal if the land is diminished by it, then it is valued without trees and building, and it is valued with either one deserving removal, and he guarantees the excess. It is said: The value of the trees deserving removal is less than their value when removed, so the value when removed, when the removal fee is subtracted, the remainder is the value of the trees deserving removal. So if the value of the land is one hundred, and the value of the trees when removed is ten, and the removal fee is one dirham, nine dirhams remain, so the land with these trees is valued at one hundred and nine dirhams, so the owner guarantees the nine.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a usurper who builds or plants on another's land. The basic ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah is that the usurper must remove the building or trees and return the land empty to the owner. This is based on the hadith 'There is no right for a wrongful root' (لا حق لعرق ظالم). However, if removal would damage the land, the owner has the option to keep the building or trees and pay the increase in value they brought. Imam Muhammad has an alternative view: if the value of the building or trees exceeds the land value, the usurper may own the land by paying its value. The commentary then explains the method of evaluating the compensation: the land is valued without the building/trees, and then with them, and the owner pays the difference. An example is given: if land worth 100, trees worth 10 but with removal cost of 1, net value of trees is 9, so land with trees is 109, owner pays 9.

Key Terms
القلع (al-qal'): Removal of what was built or planted, typically involving demolition or uprooting.
الرد (al-radd): Returning the land to its owner in its original condition after removal.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The established transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books, which carry highest authority in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He must pay the owner the full value of the land.
B) He must remove the building and return the land empty.
C) He becomes co-owner of the land with the original owner.
D) He may keep the building and pay the land's value as rent.

Key Takeaway
The default Hanafi ruling for building on usurped land is mandatory removal, but the owner may choose compensation when removal causes harm, and Imam Muhammad offers a variant for cases where the building's value far exceeds the land.

Owner's Options When Usurper Alters Property
Options for owner when usurper alters property through dyeing or mixing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the options available to the owner when a usurper alters property through dyeing or mixing, and identify the differences between the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A usurper's alteration gives the owner a choice – take compensation for the original or accept the altered item with compensation for any increase.

When a usurper changes the nature of your property—like dyeing a white cloth or mixing flour with ghee—Islamic law gives you options. The Hanafi school provides flexibility: you can either demand compensation for what was originally yours, or accept the changed item and pay for any extra value added. This lesson breaks down the rulings for different types of alterations and compares them with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the passage deals with a usurper (ghasib) who alters the property: dyeing a white cloth red, yellow, or black, or mixing sawiq with samn (clarified butter). The owner is not left without remedy.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position for red/yellow
For red or yellow dye, the owner may either claim the value of the original unaltered item (white cloth, plain sawiq) and leave the altered item to the usurper, or take the altered item and pay for the increase in value caused by the dye or mixture.
3
Analyze the black dye dispute
Abu Hanifa considers black dye a decrease (nqsan), so the owner can take the dyed cloth without paying anything. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) treat black like red/yellow, so the owner must pay the increase if taking. The text notes this may depend on market conditions.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the owner must keep the altered item and pay for the value of the dye or mixture. The owner does not have the option to demand compensation for the original unaltered item.
5
Determine the practical difference
Under Hanafi, the owner can choose the option that best compensates his loss. Under Shafi'i, the owner is forced to accept the alteration and pay for the added value. The difference (thamarah al-khilaf) affects which party bears the risk of value changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن حمر الثوب، أو صفر أو لَت السويق بسمن ضمنه أبيض ومثل سويقه، أو أخذهما وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن، فإن سود ضمنه أبيض، أو أخذه ولا شيء للغاصب؛ لأنه نقص. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: التسويد كالتحمير، قيل: هذا الاختلاف بحسب اختلاف العصر، فلينظر إن نقصه السواد كان نقصاناً، وإن زاده يعد زيادة. وعند الشافعي: المالك يمسك الضرر عنهما. [١] قوله: فإن حمر الثوب....الخ؛ أي من غصب ثوباً أبيض فصبغه أحمر أو أصفر، أو غصب سويقاً فلته بسمن، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض، ومثل السويق، ويسلمه للغاصب، وإن شاء أخذهما وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن فيهما. وإن صبغه أسود إن شاء ضمنه أبيض، وإن شاء أخذه بلا غرم؛ أي لا شيء للغاصب؛ لأنه نقص، أما إذا صبغ بلا فعل أحدٍ كإلقاء الريح، فلا خيار لرب الثوب، بل يدفع قيمة الصبغ لصاحبه؛ لأنه لا جناية من صاحب الصبغ، حتى يضمن الثوب.
If he dyes the cloth red or yellow, or mixes sawiq with samn, he guarantees the white cloth and the like of his sawiq, or he takes both and pays the increase of the dye and samn. If he dyes it black, he guarantees the white cloth, or he takes it and nothing is for the usurper because it is a deficiency. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): making black is like reddening. It is said: This difference is according to difference of time, so consider if blackening is a decrease it is a decrease, if it increases it is considered an increase. According to al-Shafi'i: the owner keeps the harm from both. [1] His statement: 'If he dyes the cloth...' etc.; meaning whoever usurps a white cloth and dyes it red or yellow, or usurps sawiq and mixes it with samn, then the owner has the option: if he wishes he claims the value of a white cloth and the similar of sawiq, and surrenders it to the usurper; and if he wishes he takes both and pays the increase of the dye and samn in them. If he dyes it black, if he wishes he claims the white cloth; if he wishes he takes it without payment; i.e., nothing for the usurper because it is a deficiency. But if he dyes without any action like wind throwing, then the owner of the cloth has no option; rather he pays the value of the dye to its owner because there is no harming act from the owner of the dye to make him liable for the cloth.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a usurper changes the property through dyeing or mixing. The owner has a choice: either take compensation for the original unaltered item (e.g., value of white cloth or plain sawiq) and leave the altered item to the usurper, or take the altered item and pay the increase in value caused by the dye or mix. For black dye, Abu Hanifa considers it a decrease, so the owner may take the cloth without paying anything. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say black dye is like red/yellow, so the owner must pay for the increase if he takes it. The text notes this might depend on market conditions; if black actually increases value, it is treated as an increase. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the owner keeps the item and pays compensation for the dye/samen, but not the option to take compensation for the original. The commentary adds that if the change occurred without human action (e.g., wind blew dye), the owner has no choice and must pay the value of the dye to its owner.

Key Terms
السويق (al-sawiq): Barley flour or a similar roasted grain product, commonly mixed with ghee or oil.
السمن (al-samn): Clarified butter used for mixing with sawiq.

Key Takeaway
When a usurper alters property, the owner has a choice between claiming the original value or accepting the alteration with payment for any increase; black dye’s treatment depends on market conditions.

Usurper Altering Property: Dyeing and Mixing in Hanafi Fiqh
Hanafi rulings on compensation when usurper alters property by dyeing or mixing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the owner's options when a usurper dyes or mixes a fungible item, and explain the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab differences regarding black dye.

Core Idea
Owner can claim original value or take altered item paying increase; black dye is contested as decrease or increase.

When a usurper alters stolen property, the owner is not left without remedy. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced choice: either claim compensation for the original item or take the altered item and pay for any increase in value. This lesson examines the rulings for dyeing cloth and mixing sawiq, the special treatment of black dye, and the Shafi'i counter-argument based on qiyas with building removal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the owner's options
In cases of dyeing (red/yellow) or mixing sawiq with butter, the owner may either claim the value of the original item (white cloth / un-mixed sawiq) and surrender the altered item to the usurper, or take the altered item and pay the increase in value due to the dye or butter.
For sawiq, compensation is in kind (mithli); for cloth, it is by value (qimah).
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on black dye
Abu Hanifa considered black dye a decrease, so the owner may take the black cloth without payment. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) considered it an increase like other colors, so the owner must pay for the increase. The difference may reflect changing customs.
The mufta bihi in later times is the view of the two companions, as black dye typically adds value.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i analogized dyeing to building on land: the usurper must remove his property (dye) at his own expense. The Hanafi response is that in building, the usurper recovers materials; in dyeing, the dye is dissipated and would be lost, so istihsan (juristic preference) gives the owner a choice instead.
This shows the use of istihsan to override qiyas when strict analogy leads to hardship.
4
Distinguish between mithli and non-mithli compensation
Sawiq is a mithli (fungible) good, so the owner's claim is for 'the like' (equivalent in kind). Cloth is non-mithli, so compensation is by value (qimah). This affects how the owner exercises the option.
Hanafis define mithli as goods that can be exactly replaced in the market.
5
Apply the ruling to scenarios with no actor
If the dye falls on the cloth by natural causes (e.g., wind), the owner has no choice; he must pay the value of the dye to its owner, because there is no aggressor.
This highlights the requirement of direct action for ghasb liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب / تعريفه وأحكامه. بقلعه إن نقصت به، فتقوم بلا شجر وبناء، وتقومُ مع أحدهما مستحقُّ القَلْعِ فيضمن الفضل، فإن حمر الثوب، أو صفر أو لَت السويق بسمن ضمنه أبيض ومثل سويقه، أو أخذَهُما وغرمَ ما زادَ الصَّبغ والسمن، فإن سود ضمنه أبيض، أو أخذه ولا شيء للغاصب؛ لأنه نقص. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: التسويد كالتحمير. قيل: هذا الاختلافُ بحسب اختلاف العصر. وعند الشَّافِعِي: المالك يمسك الضرر عنهما، وقوله: أمر بقلعه جملة وقعت صفةً لكل واحد من البناء والشجر على سبيل البدل. فإن حمر الثوب....الخ؛ أي من غصب ثوباً أبيض فصبغه أحمر أو أصفر، أو غصب سويقاً فلته بسمن، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض، ومثل السويق، وسلمه للغاصب، وإن شاء أخذهما وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن فيهما. وإن صبغه أسود إن شاء ضمنه أبيض، وإن شاءَ أخذه بلا غرم؛ أي لا شيء للغاصب؛ لأنه نقص، أما إذا صبغ بلا فعل أحدٍ كإلقاء الريح، فلا خيار لرب الثوب، بل يدفع قيمة الصبغ لصاحبه؛ لأنه لا جناية من صاحب الصبغ، حتى يضمن الثوب. زَيْلَعِي. كذا في رد المحتار. قوله: القياس على قلع البناء؛ أي للشافعي له القياس على قطع البناء، يعني كما أنّ في فصل الساحة يؤمر بقطع الشجرِ إذا لم تتضرر الأرض، فكذلك هاهنا؛ لأنَّ في كلِّ منهما شغل ملك الغير بملكه. كذا في «العناية». قوله: لأن النقض يكون... الخ؛ وهو - بالكسر – المنقوض، يعني أنّ الحاصل من البناء المنقوض كالخشب والآجر للغاصب، أما الصبغ فيتلاشى، ولم يحصل للغاصب منه شيء، فلم يؤمر الغاصب بقلع صبغه، كيلا يفوت حقه بالكلية. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». قوله: والسويق مثلي... الخ؛ هذا شروع في بيان وجه تخصيص الثوب بالقيمة، والسويق بالمثل كما لا يخفى.
Book of Usurpation / Its definition and rulings. He said: 'By its removal if the land is diminished thereby, then it is valued without trees and building, and valued with one of them while the one entitled to removal is valued, and he guarantees the excess.' If he dyed the cloth red or yellow, or mixed the sawiq with butter, he guarantees it white and the like of his sawiq, or he takes them both and pays the increase of the dye and butter. If he dyed it black, he guarantees it white, or he takes it and nothing for the usurper because it is a decrease. This according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions: blackening is like reddening. It is said: this difference is according to the difference of time. According to al-Shafi'i: the owner holds back the harm from both, and his statement: 'order to remove it' occurred as an adjective to each of building and tree on the basis of substitution. Then he said: 'If he dyed the cloth...' etc.; meaning whoever usurps a white cloth and dyes it red or yellow, or usurps sawiq and mixes it with butter, the owner has the choice: if he wishes he takes its value as a white cloth and the like of sawiq and surrenders it to the usurper, and if he wishes he takes them both and pays the increase of the dye and butter in them. If he dyed it black, if he wishes he takes its value white, and if he wishes he takes it without payment; i.e., nothing for the usurper because it is a decrease. As for when it is dyed without any action of anyone, like the wind casting it, then the owner of the cloth has no choice; rather he pays the value of the dye to its owner because there is no aggression from the owner of the dye that would make him liable for the cloth. From Zaylai. Likewise in Radd al-Muhtar. His statement: 'The analogy on removal of building'; i.e., for al-Shafi'i he has the analogy on cutting the building, meaning just as in the case of separating the courtyard, the tree is ordered to be cut if the land is not harmed, so too here because in each case one occupies another's property with his property. From al-Inayah. His statement: 'Because the dismantled material...' etc.; it is the dismantled material, meaning that what is obtained from the dismantled building like wood and bricks belongs to the usurper, whereas the dye is dissipated and the usurper gets nothing from it, so the usurper is not ordered to remove his dye so that his right is not completely lost. From Hashiyat al-Jalabi. His statement: 'And sawiq is mithli...' etc.; this is the beginning of explaining the reason for specifying the cloth with value and sawiq with the like, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Usurpation (Kitab al-Ghasb) in a commentary on al-Wiqayah. It begins by discussing the scenario where an usurper builds or plants on usurped land, and the land is diminished by removal. The land is valued without the additions, and the protector (the one entitled to removal) guarantees the excess. Then the focus shifts to cases where the usurper alters the property—dyeing a cloth or mixing sawiq (a type of roasted barley flour) with butter. The owner has a choice: either claim the value of the item in its original state (white cloth or un-mixed sawiq) and leave the altered item with the usurper, or take the altered item and pay the increase in value due to the alteration. However, if the dye is black, Abu Hanifa rules that black dye is a decrease, so the owner may either claim the value of a white cloth or take the black cloth without paying anything. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that black dye is like any other color—it is an increase, so the owner must pay for it if he takes the cloth. The text notes that this difference may be due to changing times (i.e., black may have become valuable later). Imam al-Shafi'i's position is presented: he analogizes this case to the removal of a building, arguing that just as the usurper is ordered to remove his building or tree from the land if it can be done without harming the land, here the usurper should be ordered to remove the dye from the cloth if possible. The Hanafi response is that in the building case, the usurper recovers the materials (wood, bricks), but in the dye case, the dye is dissipated and cannot be recovered, so ordering removal would cause total loss to the usurper. Therefore, the Hanafi school gives the owner a choice instead. Additionally, sawiq is a mithli (fungible) item, so compensation is in kind (the like), while cloth is not, so compensation is by value. This passage illustrates Hanafi reasoning: preference for preserving both parties' rights through istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): The unlawful taking and possession of another's property without right.
سويق (sawiq): Roasted barley or wheat flour, often mixed with butter or sugar.
مثلي (mithli): A fungible item for which compensation is paid in kind (like for like).
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value; compensation paid in money for non-fungible items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the owner's option when a usurper mixes his sawiq with butter?
A) The owner must take the mixed sawiq and pay nothing.
B) The owner may take the mixed sawiq or claim the like of his sawiq.
C) The owner may only claim the value of the sawiq.
D) The owner must sue the usurper for removal of the butter.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the owner a choice in usurpation-dyeing cases, reflecting istihsan to balance interests, with the mufta bihi considering black dye an increase.

Owner Owns Usurped Property upon Taking Value
Ruling on ownership transfer when usurped property is made absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership transfer in ghasp and explain the necessity-based dalil that distinguishes it from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Taking the value of absent usurped property transfers ownership by necessity to avoid dual ownership.

This passage addresses a key question in the law of usurpation (ghasp): when the owner accepts the value of missing property, does the usurper become the owner? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on a rational necessity argument, while the Shafi'i school disagrees. Understanding this ruling reveals how Hanafi fiqh applies the principle of avoiding harm (darar) and the distinction between property that can and cannot be transferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the specific act? The usurper makes the property absent (ghayyaba) and the owner takes its value (qimah). The ruling: the owner owns it (mallakahu), i.e., ownership transfers to the usurper by necessity.
The owner does not lose ownership unless he receives the value; he has a choice initially.
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is a rational argument (dalil aqli): if the owner receives the substitute (badal), the original property must leave his ownership to avoid combining substitute and substituted in one person, which is invalid.
This is not a direct textual proof but a qiyas based on the necessity of avoiding invalid dual ownership.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The logical link: owning both the badal and the mubdal simultaneously is impossible (bāṭil). Therefore, the mubdal must go to the one who gave the badal (the usurper), provided the property is capable of transfer (qābil li al-naql).
The mudabbir is an exception—it cannot be owned by the usurper by consensus.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that usurpation (ghasp) is pure aggression and cannot be a cause for ownership, analogizing to the mudabbir case where there is consensus.
Shafi'i: the property remains the owner's even after he takes the value; he must return the value.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi). The mudabbir case is distinguished because it is non-transferable. The owner's choice when property reappears with greater value ensures equity.
Intra-Hanafi: Al-Karkhi gives choice to usurper when value equals or is less, but the asahh (most correct) gives choice to owner.

[1] (And if he made absent what he usurped and the owner was made liable for its value, he owns it), contrary to al-Shafi'i, because usurpation is not a cause for ownership. We said: He only owns it out of necessity because the owner owns its substitute; lest the substitute and the substituted be combined in the ownership of one person, unlike that which does not accept ownership, like the mudabbir. And the usurper is believed regarding its value. [1] His statement: 'And if he made absent... etc.' means: Whoever usurps a specific item and makes it absent, then the owner has a choice: if he wishes he waits for it to appear, and if he wishes he makes him liable for its value. So if he did not wait and the usurper made him liable for its value, he owns it. [2] His statement: 'It is not a cause for ownership'; its explanation: Indeed usurpation is pure transgression, and what is like that is not a cause for ownership which is a secondary effect and pure benefit, just as if he usurped a mudabbir and made it absent and made him liable for its value, then the usurper does not own it by consensus. And for us: Indeed for the owner is the substitute, which is the full value, i.e., hand and neck, and whoever owns the substitute of something, the substituted leaves his ownership in exchange for it, otherwise the combination of substitute and substituted in the ownership of one person would be necessary, which is invalid. So it must enter the ownership of the owner of the substitute to repel harm from the owner of the substitute, on condition that the substituted is capable of transfer from one ownership to another, and the mudabbir is not like that. So it is in al-'Inayah. (3) His statement: 'Unlike that which does not accept ownership... etc.'; a response to al-Shafi'i's analogy where he said: Just as if he usurped a mudabbir... etc. as passed. [4] His statement: 'And the usurper is believed... etc.'; because the owner claims an increase, and he denies, and the statement is the statement of the denier with his oath. So it is in al-'Inayah (2). And if the item appears and its value is equal to what he guaranteed or less in this chapter, then the answer is likewise in the apparent narration, which is the most correct; because his satisfaction is not complete since he was not given what he claimed, and the choice is due to lack of satisfaction. And al-Karkhi said: the choice is for him. So it is in al-Hidayah (3). Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school concerning a case of ghasp (usurpation) where the usurper makes the property absent (ghā'ib) and the owner is given its value. The passage begins by stating the ruling: if the usurper makes the property absent and the owner is made liable (i.e., the usurper pays the value), the owner owns the property. This is contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that usurpation cannot be a cause for ownership because it is pure aggression. The Hanafi reply is that ownership transfers out of necessity: if the owner receives the substitute (badal), i.e., the full value, then the original property cannot remain in his ownership because that would combine substitute and substituted in one person, which is invalid. Therefore, the original property must leave his ownership and enter the ownership of the one who gave the substitute (the usurper), provided the property is capable of being owned by the usurper. The exception is a mudabbir (a slave whose emancipation is tied to the owner's death), which cannot be owned by the usurper by consensus because it is not transferable in that context. The author then addresses the issue of valuation: the usurper is believed regarding the value, because the owner claims a higher value and the usurper denies it; the principle is that the statement of the denier (with oath) is accepted. Next, the commentary details the scenarios when the property reappears. If its value is more than what the usurper paid based on his own statement, the owner may either take it and return the value (redeem it) or affirm the guarantee (keep the value and let the usurper keep the property). If the usurper paid based on the owner's claim, or by proof, or by the usurper's refusal to take an oath (nukūl), then the property becomes the usurper's without choice for the owner, because the owner's satisfaction is implied by his claim. There is also an intra-Hanafi dispute: some mashayikh say the usurper's evidence of value should be accepted to drop the oath, analogizing to a depositary claiming return, but the relied-upon position is that it is not accepted. Finally, if the property's value equals or is less than the guaranteed amount, the owner still has a choice in the apparent narration (zāhir al-riwāyah) because his satisfaction was not complete; al-Karkhi held that the choice is for the usurper, but the asahh (most correct) view is that the owner has the choice. Each subtopic follows the author's order, explaining the legal reasoning and basis in the madhhab.

Key Terms
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; taking another's property without right and without consent, with aggressive taking.
بدل (badal): Substitute; here the value (qimah) paid by the usurper in place of the usurped property.
مُدبَّر (mudabbir): A slave whose emancipation is deferred until the owner's death; such a slave cannot be transferred to another's ownership during the owner's life by usurpation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if the usurped property reappears and its value is less than the amount the usurper paid based on his own statement, what is the owner's right?
A) The owner must take the property and keep the excess value.
B) The owner has a choice to take the property or affirm the guarantee.
C) The owner has no choice; the property belongs to the usurper.
D) The owner must return the value and cannot reclaim the property.

Key Takeaway
When the owner takes the value of absent usurped property, ownership transfers to the usurper by necessity to avoid dual ownership, but the owner retains a choice if the property reappears with greater value.

Owner's Option When Usurped Item Appears After Guarantee
Ruling on owner's option when usurped item reappears

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two scenarios of guarantee in ghasb and state the corresponding options for the owner.

Core Idea
The owner's option to take the item or enforce the guarantee depends on how the guarantee was established: admission gives option, while claim or proof extinguishes it.

When a usurped item reappears after the usurper has guaranteed a certain value, does the owner have a choice? This passage explains that the key is the basis of the guarantee: if the usurper himself admitted a lower value, the owner retains an option; but if the guarantee was based on the owner's own claim or proof, the owner is bound. The reasoning turns on the completeness of the owner's satisfaction (rida). The commentary also addresses intra-Hanafi differences regarding evidence and the case when the item's value equals or is less than the guaranteed amount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
First, note the two scenarios: (1) Guarantee by usurper's statement (he admits a lower value) and owner claims higher without proof. (2) Guarantee by owner's claim, proof, or usurper's nukul. The ruling differs based on which scenario applies.
The key is whether the owner's claim was the basis for the guarantee or the usurper's admission.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
In scenario 1, the owner has an option: take the item and return the compensation, or enforce the guarantee. In scenario 2, the owner has no option; the usurper keeps the item and the guarantee stands.
The option in scenario 1 exists because the owner's satisfaction was incomplete (he claimed a higher value but lacked proof).
3
Identify the underlying principle
The principle is the completeness of the owner's satisfaction (rida). If the owner's claim (or proof) caused the guarantee, he is deemed satisfied. If the usurper's admission caused it, the owner's lack of proof does not imply full satisfaction.
This principle explains why the ruler (qadi) does not accept the usurper's evidence to prove a lower value.
4
Analyze the proof cited
The commentary cites a famous hadith as evidence that the usurper's evidence is not accepted because it negates the increase (evidence for negation is not accepted). This is supported by the general rule that evidence for negation is not accepted, except in specific cases like the depositee.
Some mashayikh analogize to the depositee, but the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) rejects the evidence.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi variations
Al-Karkhi differed on the case when the item's value equals or is less than the guarantee: he gave the owner a choice, while Zahir al-Riwayah says same ruling (option exists) because satisfaction is incomplete. Also, regarding the usurper's evidence, some scholars accepted it to remove the oath, but the mast correct opinion is non-acceptance.
The mufta bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah position: option in scenario 1, no option in scenario 2, and usurper's evidence not accepted.
