حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
مع حلفه إن لم يقم حجّة الزيادة، فإن ظَهَرَ المغصوب وقيمته أكثر ، وقد ضَمِنَ الغاصب بقوله ، أخذه المالك وردّ عوضه أو أمضى الضمان، وإن ضَمِنَ بقول مالكه ، أو بحجته ، أو بنكول غاصبه، فهو له ولا خيار للمالك مع حلفِهِ إن لم يقم حجّةَ الزّيادة، فإن ظَهَرَ المغصوب وقيمته أكثر ، وقد ضَمِنَ الغاصب بقوله، أخذَهُ المالك وردّ عوضه أو أمضى الضمان، وإن ضَمِنَ بقول مالكه ، أو بحجته ، أو بنكول غاصبه ، فهو له ولا خيار للمالك) ؛ لأنه تم ملكه ; لأنَّ المالك رضى بذلك حيث ادعى عليه هذا المقدار.
[١]قوله : إن لم يقم ؛ أي المالك حجّة الزيادة، فإن عجز من إقامة البينة، وطلب يمين الغاصب، وللغاصب بينة تشهد بقيمة المغصوب لا تقبلُ بينة الغاصب، بل يحلف عن دعواه، للحديث المشهور في هذا الباب ؛ ولأنَّ بينته تنفي الزيادة، والبينة على النفي لا تقبل.
وقال بعض المشايخ : ينبغي أن تقبل ؛ لإسقاط اليمين، كالمودع إذا ادعى ردّ الوديعة ، فإنّ القول قوله مع اليمين، ولو أقامَ البيِّنة على ذلك قبلت. كذا في «العناية»). [٢] قوله : أخذه المالك...الخ ؛ أي بالخيار ؛ إن شاء أخذه ورد عوضه، وإن شاءَ أمضى الضمان ؛ لأنه لم يتم رضاه لهذا المقدار حيث يدعي الزيادة، وأخذه دونها لعدم الحجة، وأخذ المالك بما دون الزيادة لا يدلُّ على تمام الرضاء ؛ لأنه إنما أخذ ذلك للضرورة، وهي عدم الحجة عنده ، فلا يدلُّ على رضاه ، بخلاف المسألة الآتي ؛ لأنّ دعواه تلك القيمة كانت باختياره. كذا في «العناية»(۲).
ولو ظهرت العين وقيمتها مثل ما ضمنه أو دونه في هذا الفصل، فكذلك الجواب في ظاهر الرواية، وهو الأصح ؛ لأنه لم يتم رضاه ، حيث لم يعط له ما يدعيه، والخيار لفوات الرضا، وقال الكرخي : الاختيار له. كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
[٣] قوله : فهو ؛ أي المغصوب للغاصب، ولا خيار للمالك كما في المسألة المتقدمة ؛ لأنه تم له الملك بسبب اتصل به رضاءُ المالك، حيث ادعى هذا المقدار على الغاصب.
(۱) «العناية شرح الهداية (٩ : ٣٤٦).
(۲) (العناية»(٩ : ٣٤٧).
(۳) ((الهداية» (٤ : ١٩).
With his oath if he does not provide proof of the increase. Then if the usurped item appears and its value is more, and the usurper guaranteed by his statement: the owner takes it and returns its compensation or enforces the guarantee. And if he guaranteed by the statement of its owner, or by his proof, or by the refusal of the usurper to take an oath: it is for him and there is no option for the owner with his oath if he does not provide proof of the increase. Then if the usurped item appears and its value is more, and the usurper guaranteed by his statement: the owner takes it and returns its compensation or enforces the guarantee. And if he guaranteed by the statement of its owner, or by his proof, or by the refusal of the usurper to take an oath: it is for him and there is no option for the owner) because his ownership has been completed, because the owner was satisfied with that since he claimed this amount against him. [1] His statement: 'if he does not provide' means the owner does not provide proof of the increase. If he is unable to produce evidence and requests the oath of the usurper, and the usurper has evidence testifying to the value of the usurped item: the evidence of the usurper is not accepted, rather he takes an oath regarding his claim, due to the famous hadith on this topic, and because his evidence negates the increase, and evidence for negation is not accepted. Some mashayikh said: it should be accepted to remove the oath, like the depositee if he claims return of the deposit: his statement is accepted with his oath, and if he produced evidence for that, it is accepted. As in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'the owner takes it...etc.' means he has the option: if he wishes he takes it and returns its compensation, and if he wishes he enforces the guarantee, because his satisfaction for this amount was not complete since he claims the increase, and he took it without it due to lack of proof. The owner taking less than the increase does not indicate complete satisfaction because he took it out of necessity, which is lack of proof with him, so it does not indicate his satisfaction, contrary to the following case because his claim of that value was by his choice. As in al-'Inayah. And if the item appears and its value equals what he guaranteed or is less in this chapter, the answer is the same in the apparent narration, and it is the most correct, because his satisfaction was not complete since he was not given what he claims, and the option exists because satisfaction is lost. Al-Karkhi said: he has the choice. As in al-Hidayah. [3] His statement: 'it is for him' means the usurped item belongs to the usurper, and there is no option for the owner as in the previous case, because ownership was completed for him through a cause connected to the owner's satisfaction, since he claimed this amount against the usurper.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school, explained with commentary, regarding the owner's options when the usurped item appears after the usurper has guaranteed a certain value. The passage first distinguishes two scenarios: (1) When the usurper guarantees by his own statement (e.g., he admits to a lower value), and the owner claims a higher value but cannot prove it: if the item later appears with a higher value, the owner may either take the item and return the compensation paid, or enforce the guarantee and keep the compensation. (2) When the usurper guarantees based on the owner's own claim, or based on proof, or due to the usurper's refusal to take an oath (nukul): then the usurper gets full ownership of the item and the owner has no option, because the owner's claim itself indicates satisfaction. The commentary explains that the owner's acceptance of the lower value due to lack of proof does not constitute full satisfaction; he took it out of necessity, so his right to choose remains. It also notes a difference among Hanafi scholars: some mashayikh (like the author of al-'Inayah) argue that the usurper's evidence should be accepted to remove the oath, analogous to a depositee who claims return of deposit. However, the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that the usurper's evidence is not accepted because it negates the increase, and evidence for negation is generally not accepted. The passage also discusses the case where the item's value equals or is less than the guaranteed amount: the same ruling applies, and al-Karkhi differed. The overall reasoning is based on the owner's satisfaction being incomplete when he had no proof.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): The guarantee or liability of the usurper for the value of the usurped item, established by his statement, the owner's claim, or other means.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The owner's right to choose between taking the usurped item and returning the compensation, or enforcing the guarantee and keeping the compensation.
النكول (al-nukul): The usurper's refusal to take an oath when required, which results in the guarantee being established based on the owner's claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the owner have no option upon the reappearance of the usurped item?
A) When the usurper guaranteed by his own statement.
B) When the usurper guaranteed based on the owner's claim, proof, or nukul.
C) When the item's value equals the guaranteed amount.
D) When the owner cannot prove a higher value.

Key Takeaway
The owner's option in ghasb depends on how the guarantee was established: admission gives choice, claim/proof/nukul extinguishes it.

Incomplete Ownership: Sale Valid, Manumission Invalid
Ruling on validity of sale and manumission by usurper after guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a usurper's sale but invalidates his manumission, using the concept of incomplete ownership.

Core Idea
Incomplete ownership suffices for transfer of ownership (sale) but not for acts of piety (manumission).

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a key distinction: while a usurper's sale of the usurped item is valid, his manumission of a usurped slave is not. The reason lies in the nature of the ownership the usurper acquires—it is incomplete, established only by necessity. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
State the Hanafi ruling: the sale of a usurper is valid, but manumission is invalid. Note the condition: the usurper must be made liable (ضُمِّنَ).
2
Understand incomplete ownership
The ownership the usurper gains is not full; it is established only to prevent the owner from having both the item and its price. This ownership is sufficient for sale (a transfer) but not for manumission (which requires complete ownership as an act of piety).
Analogy: the mukatib (slave in contract of manumission) has similar incomplete ownership.
3
Apply the analogy of the mukatib
The commentary draws a parallel: a mukatib may sell his slave but cannot manumit him. This illustrates that incomplete ownership validates sale but not manumission.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi usul.
4
Distinguish the practical consequence
The ruling means that if a usurper sells the item and the owner claims its value, the sale stands. But if he manumits a slave, the manumission is void; the slave remains the owner's property.
The owner can still claim the slave's value because the manumission is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفذ بيع غاصب ضُمِّنَ بعد بيعه لا إعتاق عبده ضمن بعده وزوائد الغصب متصلة كالسمن والحسن، ومنفصلة كالولدِ والثَّمر ، لا تضمن إلا بالتعدي، أو بالمنع بعد الطلب
[1] قوله : ونفذ بيع غاصب...الخ ؛ أي من غصب شيئاً فباعه فضمنه المالك قيمته فقد جازَ بيعُه ، وإن غصب عبداً فأعتقه فضمَّنَه المالك لا ينفذ إعتاقه ؛ لأنَّ الملك الثابت في المغصوب ناقص ؛ لثبوته مستنداً أو ضرورة ؛ ولهذا يظهر في حق إكساب المغصوب ، فإنّها للغاصب دون أولاده، فإنّها للمالك، والناقص يكفي لنفوذ البيع دون الإعتاق ؛ كملك المكاتب ، فإنّ له أن يبيع عبده، وليس له أن يعتقه. كذا في «العناية»). (٢) قوله : كاف لنفاذ البيع ... الخ ؛ إنّما قال: كاف؛ لأن الدليل يأبى ثبوت الملكِ بالغصب ؛ لكونه عدواناً ، والملك نعمة، وإنّما يثبتُ الملك له ضرورة القضاء بالضمان ؛ لئلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك واحد والثابت ضرورة ثابت من وجه دون وجه ؛ ولهذا يظهر في حق الإكساب دون الأولاد ؛ لأنَّ الملك يثبت شرطاً للقضاء بالقيمة. والولد غير مضمون عليه بالقيمة، وهو بعد الانفصال ليس بتبع فلا يثبت هذا الحكم فيه، بخلاف الكسب ؛ لأنه بدل المنفعة، فيكون تبعاً محضاً، وثبوت الحكم في التبع بثبوته في المتبوع، سواء ثبت في المتبوع مقصوداً بسببه أو شرطاً لـغـيـره. كذا في «الكافي». [٣] قوله : مضمونةً ؛ متصلةً كانت أو منفصلة؛ لوجود الغصب، وهو إثبات اليد على مال الغير بغير رضا، وكما في الظبية المخرجة من الحرم إذا ولدت في يده يكون مضموناً عليه.
(۱) ينظر: «النكت» (ص ٥٩٥)، وغيرها.
(۲) ((العناية» (٩ : ٣٤٨).
And the sale of a usurper who is made liable after his sale is valid, but not the manumission of his slave after he is made liable. And the accessories of usurpation, connected like fatness and beauty, and separate like offspring and fruit, are not liable except by transgression, or by prevention after demand. [1] His statement: 'and the sale of a usurper...etc.' means that whoever usurps something and sells it, then the owner guarantees its value from him: his sale is valid. And if he usurps a slave and manumits him, then the owner guarantees him: his manumission is not valid, because the ownership established in the usurped property is incomplete, since it is established based on necessity. For this reason, it appears regarding the earnings of the usurped property: they belong to the usurper, but not the offspring of a female slave, for they belong to the owner. And incomplete ownership suffices for the validity of sale but not for manumission, like the ownership of a mukatib, for he may sell his slave but may not manumit him. As in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'suffices for validity of sale...etc.' He only said 'suffices' because evidence refuses the establishment of ownership by usurpation, since it is aggression, and ownership is a blessing. Ownership is only established for him out of necessity for the decree of damages, lest the substitute and the substituted come together in one ownership. And what is established by necessity is established in one way but not in another. For this reason it appears regarding earnings but not offspring, because ownership is established as a condition for the decree of value. The offspring are not liable on him for value, and after separation they are not a dependent, so this ruling is not established for them, unlike earnings because they are compensation for benefit, so they are a pure dependent. And the establishment of a ruling on the dependent is by its establishment on the principal, whether the establishment on the principal is intentional by its cause or as a condition for something else. As in al-Kafi. [3] His statement: 'liable' whether connected or separate, due to the existence of usurpation, which is placing one's hand on another's property without consent. As in the case of a gazelle taken out of the Haram: if it gives birth in his hand, it is liable on him.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school, with commentary, on two issues: (1) the validity of a sale and manumission by a usurper after he has been made liable for the item, and (2) the liability for connected and separate accessories of usurped property. The ruling states that if a usurper sells the usurped item and is then made liable (i.e., the owner accepts his guarantee of value), the sale is valid. However, if he manumits a usurped slave, the manumission is not valid even after he is made liable. The commentary explains the reasoning: the ownership the usurper acquires over the item is incomplete—it is established only by necessity (to avoid combining the substitute and the original in one ownership). This incomplete ownership is sufficient to validate a sale, which is a transfer of ownership, but not manumission, which is an act of piety requiring complete ownership. The commentary draws an analogy with a mukatib (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom), who may sell his slave but not manumit him. The second issue concerns accessories (zawa'id) of the usurped item. Connected accessories (like fatness or beauty) and separate accessories (like offspring or fruit) are not automatically liable on the usurper. They become liable only if the usurper transgresses (taghayyara) by consuming or damaging them, or if he prevents the owner from taking them after demand. This is the Hanafi position, in contrast to the Shafi'i school which holds that all accessories are automatically liable due to the usurpation. The commentary further distinguishes between earnings (like revenue) and offspring: earnings belong to the usurper because they are compensation for the benefit of the item, while offspring belong to the owner because they are not a direct substitute. The ruling on accessories is based on the definition of usurpation as placing one's hand on another's property without consent, but the liability for accessories is conditional on direct transgression or prevention.

Key Terms
الزوائد (al-zawa'id): Accessories of the usurped property, either connected (e.g., fatness, growth) or separate (e.g., offspring, fruit).
التعدي (al-ta'addi): Transgression, meaning the usurper's positive act of consuming, damaging, or otherwise interfering with the accessories beyond mere possession.
المنع بعد الطلب (al-man'u ba'da al-talab): Prevention after demand: the usurper's refusal or hindrance when the owner requests the item, which triggers liability for accessories.
المكاتب (al-mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with his master, paying installments. His ownership of property is incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a usurper's manumission of a slave invalid even after he is made liable?
A) Because the usurper never owns the slave.
B) Because manumission requires complete ownership, which the usurper lacks.
C) Because the owner retains full ownership despite the guarantee.
D) Because manumission is not a valid act by anyone.

Key Takeaway
A usurper's incomplete ownership validates his sale but not his manumission; the latter requires complete ownership.

When Are Accessories of Usurped Property Liable?
Ruling on liability for connected and separate accessories of usurped property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and separate accessories and state the conditions under which a usurper becomes liable for them.

Core Idea
Accessories of usurped property are not automatically liable; liability requires transgression or prevention after demand.

The second part of this passage addresses a common question: if someone usurps property, is he also liable for its fruits, offspring, and improvements? The Hanafi school says no—unless he actively transgresses or prevents the owner. This lesson explains the reasoning and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Define connected and separate accessories
The text mentions connected accessories (like fatness, beauty) and separate accessories (like offspring, fruit). Understand that connected accessories are inseparable parts, while separate accessories are independent.
2
State the general Hanafi rule
Accessories are not liable on the usurper by default. They become liable only if the usurper commits transgression (e.g., consumes or damages them) or prevents the owner from taking them after demand.
3
Examine the rationale
Usurpation is defined as placing one's hand on the principal item. Accessories are distinct and are not covered by that hand. Therefore, liability for accessories requires a separate act.
4
Analyze the analogy of the gazelle
The commentary cites the case of a gazelle taken from the Haram (sanctuary). If it gives birth while in the usurper's hand, the offspring is liable. The act of removing the mother is a transgression that extends to the offspring, making it liable.
This illustrates that transgression on the principal can trigger liability for accessories.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i school
The Shafi'i school holds that all accessories are automatically liable because the usurper's hand extends to all parts and fruits. The practical difference is significant: e.g., fruit that falls from a usurped tree is not liable under Hanafi unless taken or prevented.
Under Hanafi, the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that the owner must take active steps to claim accessories.

Source Text

Validity of Usurper's Sale vs. Emancipation and Liability for Increases
Ruling with proof and expansion on ghasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a usurper's sale but not his emancipation, and state when increases of usurped property become liable.

Core Idea
The usurper's ownership is deficient: it suffices for sale but not emancipation; increases are not automatically liable.

This ruling from the Wiqaya distinguishes between the contract of sale and the act of emancipation for a usurper. While a usurper cannot fully own the property, liability forces a deficient ownership (milk mustanad) sufficient to transfer ownership but not to terminate it. The same logic applies to increases: they are not automatically owed to the owner unless the usurper transgresses or refuses return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
From the matn, extract that the usurper's sale is valid after he is made liable, but his emancipation is not. Also note the ruling on increases: connected and disconnected increases are not liable except with transgression or refusal after demand.
2
Understand 'milk mustanad' (deficient ownership)
The commentator explains that ownership by usurpation cannot be full because usurpation is aggression. It is established only by necessity when the owner claims value, to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted in one ownership. This ownership is valid for some purposes (sale) but not others (emancipation).
This concept is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the analogy to the mukatib
The mukatib (slave with a contract of manumission) has deficient ownership: he can sell his slave but cannot emancipate him. The usurper's deficient ownership is analogous.
The qiyas is from the mukatib to the usurper.
4
Distinguish sale vs. emancipation
Sale transfers ownership to a third party; deficient ownership suffices for transfer. Emancipation terminates ownership; full ownership is required to extinguish it.
5
Apply to increases and note the cross-madhhab difference
Increases are not automatically liable because the usurper's hand did not extend to them initially. Liability attaches only with transgression (ta'addi) or refusal after demand. The Shafi'i school disagrees, holding all increases liable from the moment of usurpation because their definition of ghasb is broader.
The practical difference: under Hanafi, the usurper is not liable for a calf born from a usurped cow unless he refused to return the cow after demand.

ونفذ بيع غاصب ضُمِّنَ بعد بيعه لا إعتاق عبده ضمن بعده وزوائد الغصب متصلة كالسمن والحسن، ومنفصلة كالولدِ والثَّمر ، لا تضمن إلا بالتعدي، أو بالمنع بعد الطلب. ( ونفذ بيع غاصب ضُمِّنَ بعد بيعه لا إعتاق عبده ضمن بعده) ؛ لأنَّ الملك المستند كاف لنفاذ البيع لا الإعتاق. وزوائد الغصب متصلة كالسّمن ،والحسن ، ومنفصلة كالولد والنمر، لا تضمن إلا بالتعدي ، أو بالمنع بعد الطلب)، هذا عندنا ، وعند الشَّافِعِيِّ مضمونة، وقد مر أن هذا مبنى على الاختلاف في حد الغصب. [ 1 ] قوله : ونفذ بيع غاصب...الخ ؛ أي من غصب شيئاً فباعه فضمنه المالك قيمته فقد جازَ بيعُه ، وإن غصب عبداً فأعتقه فضمَّنَه المالك لا ينفذ إعتاقه ؛ لأنَّ الملك الثابت في المغصوب ناقص ؛ لثبوته مستنداً أو ضرورة ؛ ولهذا يظهر في حق إكساب المغصوب ، فإنّها للغاصب دون أولاده، فإنّها للمالك، والناقص يكفي لنفوذ البيع دون الإعتاق ؛ كملك المكاتب ، فإنّ له أن يبيع عبده، وليس له أن يعتقه. كذا في «العناية»). (٢) قوله : كاف لنفاذ البيع ... الخ ؛ إنّما قال: كاف؛ لأن الدليل يأبى ثبوت الملكِ بالغصب ؛ لكونه عدواناً ، والملك ،نعمة، وإنّما يثبتُ الملك له ضرورة القضاء بالضمان ؛ لئلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك واحد والثابت ضرورة ثابت من وجه دون وجه ؛ ولهذا يظهر في حق الإكساب دون الأولاد ؛ لأنَّ الملك يثبت شرطاً للقضاء بالقيمة. والولد غير مضمون عليه بالقيمة، وهو بعد الانفصال ليس بتبع فلا يثبت هذا الحكم فيه، بخلاف الكسب ؛ لأنه بدل المنفعة، فيكون تبعاً محضاً، وثبوت الحكم في التبع بثبوته في المتبوع، سواء ثبت في المتبوع مقصوداً بسببه أو شرطاً لـغـيـره. كذا في «الكافي».
And the sale of the usurper is executed after he is made liable [for the price] after his sale; but the emancipation of his slave is not executed after he is made liable. And the increases of the usurped property, connected like fatness and beauty, and disconnected like offspring and fruit, are not liable except with transgression, or by refusal after demand. (And the sale of the usurper is executed after he is made liable; not the emancipation of his slave after he is made liable) because the ownership that arises [from liability] is sufficient for the execution of sale, not emancipation. And the increases of the usurped property, connected like fatness and beauty, disconnected like offspring and fruit, are not liable except with transgression, or by refusal after demand. This is our [position], and according to al-Shafi'i they are liable, and it has passed that this is based on the difference in the definition of ghasb. [1] His saying: 'And the sale of the usurper...' etc.; meaning whoever usurps something and sells it, then the owner makes him liable for its value, his sale is valid. And if he usurps a slave and emancipates him, then the owner makes him liable, his emancipation does not take effect, because the ownership established in the usurped property is deficient, due to its being established by dependency or necessity; hence it appears regarding the earnings of the usurped property, for they belong to the usurper, but not regarding its offspring, for they belong to the owner. And the deficient is sufficient for the validity of sale but not emancipation, like the ownership of a mukatib, for he may sell his slave but may not emancipate him. So in al-'Inayah. (2) His saying: 'sufficient for the execution of sale...' etc.; he only said 'sufficient' because the evidence forbids the establishment of ownership by usurpation, since it is aggression, and ownership is a blessing; ownership is only established for him by necessity of adjudging liability, lest the substitute and the substituted be combined in one ownership. And what is established by necessity is established in one aspect and not another; hence it appears regarding earnings but not offspring, because ownership is established as a condition for adjudging the value. And the offspring is not guaranteed by value, and after separation it is not a dependent, so this ruling is not established for it, unlike earnings because it is a substitute for usufruct, so it is a pure dependent. And the establishment of the ruling in the dependent is by its establishment in the principal, whether the ruling is established in the principal intentionally by its cause or as a condition for something else. So in al-Kafi.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of the Wiqaya, expanded by the commentator. It establishes three key Hanafi rulings on ghasb: (1) If a usurper sells the usurped property and the owner claims the price (making the usurper liable for the value), the sale is valid and effective. (2) If the usurper emancipates a usurped slave, the emancipation is not effective even if the owner makes him liable for the value. (3) Increases (zawa'id) of the usurped property—whether connected (like fatness or beauty) or disconnected (like offspring or fruit)—are not liable (i.e., the usurper does not owe compensation for them) unless he transgresses (e.g., uses them) or refuses to return them after a demand. The commentator explains that the reason for the difference between sale and emancipation is the nature of the ownership acquired by the usurper when he becomes liable for the value: it is deficient ownership (al-milk al-mustanad), established by necessity to avoid combining the substitute (value) and the substituted (the property itself) in one ownership. This deficient ownership is sufficient for a contract like sale, which merely transfers ownership, but insufficient for an act like emancipation, which requires full ownership to effect liberation. The commentator analogizes to the mukatib (a slave who has contracted with his master for freedom upon payment), who can sell property but cannot emancipate due to his deficient ownership. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with al-Shafi'i, who holds that all increases are liable from the moment of usurpation because the usurper's hand is transgressing. The Hanafi distinction is based on the definition of ghasb: for Hanafis, ghasb is the extinguishing of the owner's hand, so if the owner's hand was never established on the increase (e.g., an unborn calf), the usurper is not automatically liable for it. Liability for disconnected increases attaches only if they are attained by transgression (e.g., consuming the fruit) or if the usurper refuses to return them after demand.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking possession of another's property without right, in a manner that removes the owner's control.
ملك مستند (milk mustanad): Ownership that arises by dependence or necessity, specifically the ownership established for the usurper when he is made liable for the value, to avoid combining substitute and substituted in one ownership.
زوائد (zawa'id): Increases or accessories of the usurped property, divided into muttasila (connected, like fatness) and munfasila (disconnected, like fruit or offspring).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a usurper's sale of the usurped property valid but his emancipation of a usurped slave invalid?
A) Because sale requires less intent than emancipation.
B) Because the usurper's ownership is deficient, sufficient for sale but not emancipation.
C) Because the owner can only demand the value for sale, not for emancipation.
D) Because emancipation is a form of worship, which cannot be performed by a usurper.

Key Takeaway
The usurper's deficient ownership (milk mustanad) validates his sale but not his emancipation; increases are only liable with transgression or refusal after demand.

Compensation for Decrease Due to Childbirth and Offsetting by Offspring
Dispute on whether the offspring compensates for the decrease in value due to childbirth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on offsetting childbirth decrease with the offspring's value, explain the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, and apply the ruling to a simple ghasb scenario.

Core Idea
The offspring's value can offset the decrease in the mother's value because both arise from the same act of usurpation.

When a usurped female slave gives birth, the usurper is liable for the decrease in her value. But if the offspring is also returned, a nuanced ruling applies: the offspring's value can compensate for the decrease, provided it is sufficient. This is disputed by Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who argue that the offspring belongs to the usurper and cannot offset a loss to his own property. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the rule: the usurper must compensate for the decrease from childbirth, but if the offspring is returned and its value covers the decrease, nothing more is owed.
This is the majority view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (within the Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i hold that the offspring cannot compensate because it is the usurper's property.
They consider the offspring as belonging to the usurper since he is liable for the mother.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent argues: the usurper owns the offspring (as it was born in his possession and he is liable for the mother), so it cannot be used to offset a loss to his own property.
Compensation must come from an external source.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond: the owner's hand was never on the offspring (it was not owned at the time of usurpation), so the usurper's possession is not a removal of ownership. Both the decrease and the offspring arise from the same ghasb, so the offspring can offset the decrease.
The cause (sabab) is the same, so compensation is valid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In the Hanafi view, if the offspring is returned and its value covers the decrease, the usurper owes nothing. In the opposing view, the usurper must still pay for the decrease, and the returned offspring is his own property.
This difference affects actual liability in ghasb cases.

وضمن نقصان ولادة معه، وجبر بولد يفي به. (۱) (۲) وضمن نقصان ولادة معه، وجبر بولد يفي به)، خلافاً لزفره والشَّافِعِي (1) له فإنَّ الولد ملكه ، فلا يصلح جابراً لملكه ، قلنا : سببهما شيء ولنا : إنّ الغصب إثبات اليد على مال الغير على وجه يزيل يد المالك على ما ذكرناه، ويد المالك ما كانت ثابتة على هذه الزيادة حتى يزيلها الغاصب، ولو اعتبرت ثابتة على الولد تبعاً لملك الأم فإنّه ما أزالَ اليد، إذ الظاهر عدم المنع ، حتى لو منع الولد بعد طلبه يضمنه. كذا في «الكفاية" واعترض على هذا التقرير بأنّ هذا يقتضي أن يضمن الولد إذا غصب الجارية الحاملة ؛ لأنّ اليد كانت ثابتة عليه، وليس كذلك، فإنّه لا فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا غصبها غير حامل فحملت في هذا الغاصب وولدت، والرواية في «الأسرار». وأجيب : بأن الحمل قبل الانفصال ليس بمال بل يعد عيباً في الأمة، فلم يصدق عليه إثبات اليد على مال الغير كذا في «العناية» (۳) ، وكثير من شروح «الهداية»، وفي الظبية ضمان جناية ؛ ولهذا يتكرّر بتكرّرها، ويجب بالإعانة والإشارة ؛ فلأن يجب بما هو فوقها، وهو إثبات اليد على مستحق الأمن أولى وأحرى. كذا في «الهداية»(4). And he guarantees the decrease of childbirth with it, and it is compensated by an offspring that suffices for it. (1) (2) 'And he guarantees the decrease of childbirth with it, and it is compensated by an offspring that suffices for it,' contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. And if it were considered established over the offspring by way of dependency on the mother's ownership, then he [the usurper] has not removed the hand, since apparently there is no prevention, until if he prevents the offspring after demand he becomes liable for it. Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific ruling within the chapter on ghasb: when a usurper takes a female slave who then gives birth while in his possession, and the offspring dies, he must return the slave and compensate for the decrease in her value caused by childbirth. However, if the offspring itself is returned and its value covers the decrease, the offspring serves as a compensator (jabr) and the usurper owes nothing. This ruling is disputed by Zufar (a prominent Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i, who argue that the offspring belongs to the usurper (since it was born in his possession and he is liable for the mother's value), so it cannot be used to compensate for a loss to his own property—it would be like compensating oneself with one's own asset. The Hanafi response is based on the definition of ghasb: the owner's hand was never established on the offspring because it was not born at the time of usurpation; the owner's hand on the mother does not extend to the unborn offspring. Therefore, the usurper's hand on the offspring is not a removal of the owner's hand (which is the essence of ghasb), so the offspring is not automatically liable. However, if the usurper prevents the offspring after demand (i.e., refuses to return it), then he becomes liable for it—this is because the owner's demand effectively establishes his right to the offspring. The commentary then addresses an objection: if this logic is applied to a pregnant slave-girl at the time of usurpation, it would imply the usurper guarantees the unborn offspring, but the answer is that the unborn is not property; it is considered a defect in the slave-girl. Thus, the offspring only becomes a separate property after birth, and its liability is treated differently. Finally, the passage draws an analogy to a gazelle (from the Haram sanctuary): if someone takes it, they are liable for injury by its very nature, and liability extends even to indirect causes (assistance, indication), so by greater reason it applies to usurpation of a creature entitled to security.

Key Terms
نقصان ولادة (nuqsan wilada): The decrease in value of a female slave due to childbirth.
جبر (jabr): Compensation or offsetting; here, the offspring's value offsets the decrease in the mother's value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Zufar and al-Shafi'i reject the Hanafi ruling that the offspring can compensate for the decrease in the mother's value?
A) Because the offspring belongs to the owner, not the usurper.
B) Because the offspring is the usurper's own property, and cannot compensate for a loss to his property.
C) Because the decrease is not compensable at all.
D) Because the offspring is considered a dependent part of the mother.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the offspring's value can offset the decrease from childbirth because both stem from the same ghasb, and the owner's hand never extended to the offspring.

Compensation for Childbirth Deficiency with the Newborn
Ruling on guarantee of value loss from childbirth offset by child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensating childbirth deficiency with the child's value, explain the Hanafi proof, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The cause is one: both deficiency and child arise from childbirth, so the child's value offsets the mother's loss.

This passage addresses a key dispute in ghasb law: when a usurper returns a slave-girl after she gave birth, does the child's value compensate for the mother's decreased value? The Hanafi school says yes, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i say no. Let us dissect the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and Hanafi ruling
State the facts: a usurper (ghāṣib) returns a slave-girl who gave birth in his possession, causing a decrease in her value. The Hanafi ruling: if the child's value covers the deficiency, the usurper owes nothing further.
The ruling is conditional on the child's value being sufficient.
2
State the opposing position and its proof
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the child is the property of the usurper, so it cannot compensate for a deficiency in his own property (i.e., his liability). The child is a separate asset owned by the usurper, not a substitute.
This objection rests on the principle that one cannot set off his own property against his own debt.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: both the deficiency and the child arise from the same cause (childbirth). The child is therefore a substitute (jabr) for the deficiency. Additionally, the owner never had possession over the unborn child (the increase), so the usurper did not remove it—thus the child is not considered 'taken' from the owner.
Key point: unity of cause makes the child a natural offset.
4
Examine the objection about a pregnant slave-girl
An objection arises: if the child is considered a substitute, then if a usurper seizes a pregnant slave-girl, he should also guarantee the child as a separate item. The Hanafi reply: pregnancy before birth is not property but a defect; hence the definition of ghasb (taking possession of another's property) does not apply to the unborn child.
This refines the scope of ghasb to actual property, not potential.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: under the Hanafi view, if the child's value equals or exceeds the deficiency, the usurper pays nothing extra. Under the opposing view, the usurper must pay the deficiency in cash regardless of the child's value. If the child dies before return, the Hanafi ruling changes—the usurper must return the slave-girl and pay the deficiency because the child is no longer available to compensate.
Remember: the child must survive and be returned for compensation to apply.

He also guarantees the deficiency of childbirth with it, and it is compensated by a child that suffices for it. Contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, because the child is his property, so it cannot compensate for his property. We say: their cause is one thing. And we have: usurpation is taking possession of another's property in a way that removes the owner's possession, as we mentioned. The owner's possession was not established over this increase such that the usurper removed it. If it were considered established over the child by virtue of the mother's ownership, then he did not remove possession, because apparently there is no prevention, even if he prevented the child after demanding it he would guarantee it. Thus in al-Kifayah. An objection was raised against this explanation that it would require him to guarantee the child if he usurps a pregnant slave-girl because possession was established over it, but it is not so, for there is no difference between this and if he usurped her non-pregnant then she conceived in his possession and gave birth, and the narration is in al-Asrar. And the answer: pregnancy before separation is not property but rather considered a defect in the slave-girl, so taking possession of another's property does not apply. Thus in al-Inayah and many commentaries of al-Hidayah. And on the analogy of injury guarantee; for this reason it repeats with repetition and is obligatory by assistance and indication; so that which is above it, namely taking possession of one who deserves security, is more fitting. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'And he guarantees the deficiency of childbirth... etc.' meaning whatever the slave-girl decreased due to childbirth in the usurper's hand, it is in the usurper's guarantee. So if he usurped her and she gave birth in his possession, then the child died, he must return the slave-girl and return the deficiency of childbirth established in her due to childbirth, because the slave-girl by usurpation entered his guarantee with all her parts, and a part that is guaranteed has been lost, as if all of it were lost. If the slave-girl and the child are returned and the value of the slave-girl has decreased and the value of the child suffices to compensate for that decrease, the usurper does not guarantee anything. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The decrease is not compensated by the child because the child is his property, so it cannot compensate for his property. Thus in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an inter-madhhab dispute. The ruling states that when a usurper returns a slave-girl after she gave birth in his possession, if her value has decreased due to childbirth, the child's value compensates for that decrease, so the usurper owes nothing. Zufar and al-Shafi'i object because the child is the usurper's property (since offspring of a slave-girl follows the owner), so it cannot compensate for a deficiency in the usurper's own property. The Hanafi response argues that both the deficiency and the child arise from the same cause (childbirth), so the child is considered a substitute. The commentary elaborates on the nature of possession and the definition of usurpation, and addresses an objection about a pregnant slave-girl.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking possession of another's property in a way that removes the owner's possession.
نقصان (nuqsān): The decrease in value of the slave-girl due to childbirth.
جبر (jabr): Compensation; here the child's value makes up for the deficiency.
يد (yad): Possession (legal control). In ghasb, the removal of the owner's yad is key.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the value of a child born during usurpation compensate for the mother's deficiency?
A) Because the child is the property of the usurper.
B) Because both the deficiency and the child arise from the same cause (childbirth).
C) Because the owner consented to the usurpation.
D) Because the child is considered a gift from the usurper.

Key Takeaway
The child born during usurpation compensates for the mother's deficiency because both arise from the same cause, and the owner never possessed the child.

8 min
Liability for a Usurped Slave-girl Who Dies in Childbirth After Return
Intra-Hanafi dispute on causation and liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the liability of a usurper for the death of a pregnant slave-girl and identify the key difference in identifying the cause of deficiency.

Core Idea
The liability hinges on whether the cause of death is conception (Abu Hanifa) or childbirth (Sahibayn).

This passage examines a specific scenario: a usurper commits zina with a usurped slave-girl, returns her pregnant, she gives birth, and then dies. The Hanafi school is divided on whether the usurper must pay her full value. Abu Hanifa holds him liable because the cause of the deficiency (conception) occurred in his possession. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, arguing that the return was valid and death occurred in the owner's hand, so no liability. Understanding the reasoning behind each position deepens your appreciation of how Hanafi jurists determine causation and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
A usurped slave-girl is returned pregnant, gives birth, then dies. Determine the legal question: is the usurper liable for her value?
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: liable. Sahibayn: not liable. Note the difference in identifying the cause of deficiency (sabab al-nuqṣān).
3
Examine the evidence for each position
For Abu Hanifa, the cause is conception (ʿulūq), which occurred in the usurper's possession. For the Sahibayn, the cause is childbirth (wilādah), which occurred after return in the owner's hand.
4
Analyze the analogical argument
The passage draws an analogy with a sale and retraction of testimony: when a single cause produces both increase and decrease, the increase is a substitute for the decrease. This supports the Sahibayn's view that the child (increase) compensates for the mother's death (decrease).
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilāf is whether the usurper must pay the full value. Under Abu Hanifa, he does; under the Sahibayn, he does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو زنى بأمة غصبها فردت حاملاً ، فولدت، فماتت ضَمِنَ قيمتها هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما : لا يضمن لأنَّ الرَّدَّ وقع صحيحاً، وقد ماتت في يد المالك [١]قوله : وهو الولادة...الخ ؛ قال في «الكفاية» : أي . عند الصاحبين ه : سبب النقصان الولادة ؛ لأنها أوجبت فوات جزء من ماليّة الأصل، وحدوث مالية الولد ؛ لأنه وإن كان موجوداً قبل الانفصال لم يكن مالاً حتى لم يجز بيعه وهبته، وإنما صار مالاً مقصوداً بالانفصال. وعند الأعظم له سبب النقصان العلوق، ويظهر ذلك فيما إذا غصب جاريةً فحبلت . عند الغاصب، فردها فماتت بالولادة فعندها لا يضمن ؛ لأنّ سبب الهلاكِ الولادة، وكان ذلك في يد المالك، ويضمن عنده ؛ لأنَّ سببه العلـوق وكان عند الغاصب. [٢] قوله : ومثل هذا لا يعد نقصاناً ؛ وذلك لأنّ السبب الواحدَ لَمَّا أَثْرَ في الزيادة والنقصان كانت الزيادة خلفاً عن النقصان، كالبيع لَمَّا زالَ المبيع عن ملك البائع ، يدخل الثمنُ في ملكه، فكان الثمن خلفاً عن مالية المبيع؛ لاتحاد السبب، حتى أنّ الشاهدين إذا شهدا على رجل يبيع شيئاً بمثل قيمته ، فقضى القاضي به، ثم رجعا لم يضمنا شيئاً. وهذا لأنّ الفوات إلى خلف كلا فوات، وصار كما إذا غصب جارية سمينة فهزلت، ثم سمنت أو سقطت سنها ثم نبتت كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» «الأكملية». [٣] قوله : لأن الردّ وقع صحيحاً ؛ لأنه أوصل الحق إلى المستحق، وصحة الردّ يوجب البراءة عن الضمان ، فإن قيل : لا نُسَلِّمُ أنَّ الردَّ صح؛ لأنها هلكت بسبب كان عند الغاصب، أجاب بقوله : وقد ماتت في يد المالك ... الخ.
So if he commits zina with a slave-girl he usurped, then she is returned pregnant, then she gives birth, then she dies, he guarantees her value. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): He does not guarantee because the return was valid, and she died in the owner's hand. [1] His statement: 'and it is the childbirth...etc.' He said in al-Kifayah: according to the two companions, the cause of the deficiency is childbirth, because it caused loss of part of the original's value and occurrence of the child's value; because even though it existed before separation it was not property such that its sale or gift was permissible, but it became property intended by separation. And according to the greatest (Abu Hanifa), the cause of the deficiency is conception, and that appears when he usurps a slave-girl and she conceives in the usurper's hand, then he returns her and she dies in childbirth. According to the two, he does not guarantee because the cause of death is childbirth, and that was in the owner's hand. According to him, he guarantees because the cause is conception and it was in the usurper's hand. [2] His statement: 'And like this is not considered a deficiency.' That is because when one cause affects both increase and decrease, the increase is a substitute for the decrease, like sale when the sold item leaves the seller's ownership, the price enters his ownership, so the price is a substitute for the value of the sold item due to unity of cause, such that if two witnesses testify that a man sold something for its value, and the judge rules accordingly, then they retract, they do not guarantee anything. This is because loss to a substitute is like no loss. And it becomes as if he usurped a fat slave-girl then she became thin, then became fat again, or her tooth fell out then grew back. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi al-Akmaliyya. [3] His statement: 'because the return was valid' because he delivered the right to the one entitled, and validity of return obligates release from guarantee. If it is said: We do not concede that the return was valid because she died due to a cause that was in the usurper's hand, he answers with his statement: 'and she died in the owner's hand...etc.'
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage deals with an intra-Hanafi dispute. If a usurper commits zina with a usurped slave-girl, returns her pregnant, she gives birth and dies, Abu Hanifa holds the usurper liable for her full value because the cause of death (conception) occurred in his possession. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree, holding the return valid and the death in the owner's hands, so no liability. The commentary explains the difference in identifying the effective cause (سَبَب النَّقْصَان): for the Sahibayn it is childbirth (ولادة), for Abu Hanifa it is conception (علوق). The illustration is given: if a usurped slave-girl conceives at the usurper's, is returned, then dies in childbirth, the Sahibayn say no liability because the death event (childbirth) was in the owner's possession, while Abu Hanifa says liability because the conception (which caused the death) was in the usurper's possession. The text also discusses that when one cause produces both increase and decrease, the increase replaces the decrease, citing analogy with sale and testimony.

Key Terms
علوق (ʿulūq): Conception; the cause of deficiency according to Abu Hanifa in this context.
سبب النقصان (sabab al-nuqṣān): The cause of the decrease in value or death.
ولادة (wilādah): Childbirth; the cause of deficiency according to the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the usurper not liable for the value of the slave-girl?
A) Because the return was invalid.
B) Because the cause of death was childbirth, which occurred in the owner's hand.
C) Because the usurper did not intend to cause death.
D) Because the slave-girl was pregnant before the usurpation.

Key Takeaway
The liability of a usurper for the death of a returned pregnant slave-girl depends on whether the cause of death is conception (Abu Hanifa) or childbirth (Sahibayn).
